THE RED PAGE.
And you love me ?
I love you.
You are, then, cold coward.
Aye.- but, beloved,
When I strive to ounie to you,
Man’s opinions, a thousand thickets,
My interwoven existence,
' My life,
Caught in the stubble of the world
Like a tender veil,
This stays me.
No strange move’can I make ' ,
Without noise of tearing.
I dare not.
If love loves,
There is no world
Nor word, i
All is lost . ,
Save thought of love
And. place, to dream.
You lose me 7
I love you.
You are, then, cold coward,
Aye; but, beloved • . . A ~ .
Sephen Crame.
The essence of Art is emotion^-a movin gof the
mind, ihat is, in order to give an artistic im-
pression there must be. a certain activity in the
brain to commence with ; from a quiescent or
stagnant brain nothing cjhtin-s, The new book of
Genesis will relate how in the jitter days Art re-
create, d the heayen, and the earth. And the earth
was .without £ain tifig and sculpture ; and dark-
ness _ was upon the face of the poets. And the
Spirit of Man mured upon the face of aesthetics.
The soul of Art is emotion to be moved and to
move. But the limbs afid outward flourishes are
the quantity (force),and q’uality (kind) of emotion.
The limbs-are .easily' dealt with : you can ’say at
once that the lustier they are the better will be
the Art. Plainly, when there is a choice between
a half-dead emotion and one all alive and kicking,
the latter is better, One measure of the value of
any artistic aqhievepiejit is the strength of its kick.
When a poem gives you the sensation of being
turned inside out aim held 'gasping over the In-
finite by a single hair, ‘it has gone far to prove
that it really is a poem, and not an exercise in
verse by an experienced or inexperienced word-
juggler. The new gospel according to .John will
declare that there is more healing in the pool of
Art when it ps troubled by twenty angelpdwer
than by o.tfe or two angelpower,' and that in pro-
portion as-the angel power.is multiplied will in-
crease. a Philistine chippie's chance of being made
whole. ? .
* . . * * T-
It is in consideration of the quality-flourishes
; that,.the trouble begins. You can' say that Art
rests on emotion, and that, .other things equal, a
big emotion is better than a little emotion ; but
—V —what kind of,a big emotion V For the sake of
argument, we may confine , scrutiny to that parti-
cular activity of Art which is. called Literature;,
and to the particular phase of that activity which
is. called Poetry. Standing on the ground' already-
won, cine can see that, in so • far as Literature is
Art, it is the function of Literature to convey big
emotions (of the right kind) by the use of words,
and it is the function of Poetry to convey big
emotions (of the right kind), by-the’ use of: words
rhythmically ordered.
[Since big emotions of the right kind belong as much
to Prose as ,to Poetry, the, essential .difference between
Poetry and ■ Prpse; lies itirthe iime-pulse ; or mhsic-heat
the rhijilimj, which is the peculiar bfoperty of.
Poetry, When the rhythm is well-defined, it is obvious
to every. reader that .a form, of w ords has the charac-
teristic of Poetry; when undefined, that it has the
characteristic of Prose. But (1) the well-defined passes
imperceptibly into the undefined ; and (2) readers’ears
naturally vary in atuiteness, and are variously tuned by
education ; so that there is a borderdine' class of compo-
sitions which we cannot with general acceptance define
either as Poetry or Prose. For example; I have heard a
Master of Melb. University Arts declare that Whitman's
Till with sound of trumpet,
Far, far off the daybreak calls—hark ! how loud and clear
I hear it wind !
Swift! to the head of the army !—swift ! spring to vour
places, .
Pioneers ! O Pioneers !
is plain prose, and should be printed *
Till with sound of trumpet far, far off the davbreak
calls Hark, how loud and clear I hear it wind '' Swift
to the head of the army ! swift, spring to your places
pioneers ! O pioneers !
Yet that verse and the others joined with it seem to
me to have a rhythm quite sufficient to justify Whitman
in calling them Poetry. On the other hand', I find the
rhythm of the fragment at the head of this page insutfi-
cient for Poetry, ami should class it as dramatic Prose.
Bui the book containing it was referred to as “Stephen
Crane’s poetry” in an English review. Plainly there is a
personal equation to he solved ; and the solution depends
upon the factor of physical (aural— i.e., cerebral) effici-
ciency.J
_** * »
J. E 8. Ijenerie has taken the trouble to collate
a number of definitions of Poetry. Here tliev
are— J
Aristotle (“Poe tics,” L.i.): “Poetry is an imitation of the
actions of men by the use of rhythm, words, and melody.’
Plato (Ion) : “ Poetry is not an .art, hut a divine power
which m >ves you like the lodestone. This stone not only
aLtracts iron rings, hut magnetises them, so that they
a.so can attract; so that, you may have noticed a series rf
rings hanging one from another like links of a chain, but
from the si one comes all this power of attraction. Thus,
too, does the Muse, as the lodestone, move men divinely
inspired—the ring nearest the stone being the poet, and
the r. st of the rings the hearers of the poet’-s lay, whom
he inspires divinely likewise.’’
Novalis (“Fragments.’' Berlin, .1850): “Poetical sen-
timent has something akin to the sense of divination, to
the feligious sense, even to
blink.p re (“Timon of Athens, ’ see also “Midsummer;
Night s itryam," VI.):
“ Poesy is as a gum, which oozes
From whence ’t.is nourished ; the fire in the flint
* hews not. till it be struck ; our gentle flame
Provokes itself, and like the current flies
Each hound it chases.”
Wordsworth. (Preface to Lyrical ballads) : “ Poetry is.
the spontaneous overflow of powerful feelings; it takes
its origin from emotion recollected in tranquillity.
The emotion is contemplated until ... the tranquillity
gradually disappears, and an emotion kindred to that
which was before the subject of contemplation is gradu-
alf\ pioduced, and does itself actually exist in-the mind.”
Uoleridge (“ Table Talk ”) : “ Poetry is the use of the
best words in the best..order.”
Mill (“ Essays,’ B nthani) : “Imagination which
enables us by a voluntary effort to conceive the absent as
if it were present, the imaginary as if it were real, and to
clothe it in the feelings which, if it were indeed real, it
would bring along with it, constitutes the poet.”
Jonson (“Discoveries”): “Poetry is a speaking
picture”
Shelley (“ Poetry,” Chap. I.): “ Poetry may be defined
to be the expression of the imagination.”
Goethe-(“ Conversations,'’ 1826): “ A, poet deserves not.
the name while he only speaks out his few subjective
feelings ; hut as soon as he can appropriate to himself qnd
express the world, he is a poet.”
Macaulay (beginning of essay “ Milton ”): “By poetry
we mean the art of employing words in such a manner as
to produce an illusion on the imagination, the art of
doing by means of words what the painter does by means
of colors. . . . Truth is essential to pofetry, but it is the
truth of madness.”
Carlyle (“Survey of German Poetry”) : “ Poetry is the
attempt which man. makes to render his existence har-
monious, the utmost he can do for that end; it spriims
therefore, from his whole feelings, opinions, activity, and
takes its character from these.”
Keble (Essay “Walter Scott”): . . . the indirect
expression in words, most appropriately in metrical
words, of some overpowering emotion, or ruling taste or
feeling, the direct indulgence whereof is somehow’re-
pressed.”
Shairp (“ Aspects of Poetry,” c. iii.): “ When the pene-
trative or interpretative power of imagination )avs hold
of the essential beauty of existence or Nature and ex-
presses it in a few immortal words, we have poetry.”
* * * *
But there is only partial .help iii any of these;
they all leave out the essential quality of rhythm.
Boss net’s sermons are a criticism of life; Pater
and Jefferies .and many another give (in prose) •
exquisite expression to an exquisite impression
aud so on. To begin with. Poetry must have a
rhythm sui,li:;iently define Itoimpres’s the brain (Say,
one brain).' Then, having found its rhythmic vehicle,
it must load with its biggest emotion; and one’s
brain, (say, the best or most sensitive brain), is the
measure of the size of the.emotion. And, above
all, Puatry must make, sure that it has the right
kind of emotion—and, we might add, that the
emotion is expressed in the right way. And when
the kinds and expressions of emotion are brought
to analysis, it will be found once more that
a man inhst measure Ins own universe, form
liis own judgments, crown with his' own’ laurel
his own, poet. “What is. Poetry?” must be
settled in the last resort not by standards, or
schools, or precedents,-but by the character of the
molecules stored in the brain-pan of John Smith.
Nevertheless, as Aryan brains have an average
weight of something like 500 z., and are susceptible
of something like an average polish, at any given
time many people are usually in agreement as to
what is or is not poetry. In easy cases and plain
instances, that is ; for at every fresh advance in
difficulty the problem causes debate as endless as
the diversity of the debaters' minds. Corning
down to the. 'convenient concrete, here is one of
“A Charming Variety of Poems upon Charming
Subjects,” by Frederick Rubery, a resident of
N.S.W.
The author considers this poetry ; though a sen-
sible preface indicates that it is poetry on the
small scale. It has rhythm, certainly ; but it does
not yield the right sized emotion, or the right
kind, in the right way. It may be called Poetry,
class I. Going higher, here is one of “ Poems, by
C. Harpur lie lias two or three better, and a
great many worse.
Now, I would put that piece in tlio same class
ILL as the Harpur piece; It is merely narrative,
winle< Harpur’s contains an idea ; and', chiefly for
this reascm, HarpiirVeniotion seems to me' larger.
But it is not so poetical'in kind as the Stephens
emotion, and it is not so poetically expressed. So
the totality of poetical impression is for me, as
nearly as I can judge,> about the same. And now
w : e will jump away up to class VI., the ether
where Koderic Quinn poetically lives.
That is a piece written for The Bulletin, anc
now first printed; and in one sens® poetry can go no
higher. You can get a larger emotion still, a
more vivid emotion, and conceivably one expressed
just a little more dexterously; but never one
higher in poetical quality. For it has that
glamour, that mystery, that sense of hovering on
tiie verge of the inexpressible which is the
keenest and rarest of all poetical emotions. Ho
to 1113' mind Roderic Quinn stands highest in
kind of all Australian or quasi-Australian poets.
But in the final judgment you have to consider
weight and quantity of achievement as well. And
each of our writers lias his own excellence. Ken-
dall is the sweetest; he is like honey to the palate.
Haley brings intellect to aid his fancy; if he
were only a -little warmer he would be above
Kendall. As it is he is in the first class
with Kendall and others. And when Ogilvie
reaches publication he will certain!}' go into
the. first class too. He is the most spontaneous,
the most lyrical of all ; and his best work is still
to be done he is improving every year. Haley
moves slowly in his verse; even Kendall only
flows ; but Ogilvie gushes, leaps, runs and rushes
singing. His instantaneous reaction to emotion
evokes an emotion as instantaneous. He is
not the best of our bards, though he ranks
level with the best. He has not quite Lawson’s
intensity, but he has a finer note and a broader
sweep ; he has not Paterson’s humor, but he, loves
his horse as well as Paterson, and knows his bush
better. He does not give me the highest emotion,
the most lasting emotion, but he gives me the
most exultant, the liveliest and purest and most
natural emotion. He is the only poet in Aus-
tralia who can bring 1 tears to my eyes.
GOD SAVE THE BANKS.
God save our noble banks,
Those of the lowest ranks,
Long may they bravely stand
In this our sunny land,
And with Thy powerful.hand-
God save the banks.
Lord, we, Thy humble friends
Pray for good dividends,
Daily increase our store,
Sending in more and more,
This we do now implore,
Gpd save The banks.
Think of the many poor
Think what they must endure.
May the Commercial stand
Guided by Thy strong hand,
We echo from our land
God save the banks.
Soon may the Joint Stock share
Blessings named in our prayer.
And also to he frank.
Found us the Nat ional Bank.
We ask from every rank,
God save the banks.
Lord, we shall render thanks
For National S wings Banks,
Put forth Th.v powerful hand,
Then hanks will firmly stand.
We ask throughout onr land,
God save the hanks.
THE FORGOTTEN.
He shone in the senate, the camp, anti the grove
The mirror of manhood, the darling of love.
He fought for his country, the star of the brave,
And died for its weal when to die was to save.
And Wisdom and Valour long over him wept,
And Beauty for ages strewed flowers where he slept.
And the bards of the people inwrought with their lays
The light of his glory, the sound of his praise.
But afar in the foreword 1 have faded t'heir strains
And now of his being what record remains'.? ’
Within a lone valley a tomb crumbles fast,,
And the name of the Sleeper is lost in the past......
That may be set down as. Poetry, class III. It
has rhythm; it conveys an emotion of a tolerable
size and kind with tolerable skill. Compare it
with:a piece by Brunton Stephens, in “Fayette
which the late T. J. Byrnes praised—. ’
Australia ! Beautiful Australia
A land of beauty ! -By a rustic bridge.
Formed by a fallen trunk, w&.reached a shrine
Whose pillars were, of hrowmstemmVl fern, whose roof
Was delicate tracery of fronds, whose spires
Were soaring- euealvpts clad from slenderest branch
To lowest bole in <*ai‘j» of silvery white
Here pink-veined o.chids bloomed ; in-every nook
Sweet violets nesile-i ; through the shafts of fern
A runlet crimsonf-d in the sunset glow,
Edged with luxuriant growth of maiden hair,
Bv willow-leaved ac-icias overhung ; •
V\ hile through the f athery green that fringed the .’bank
The wild lobelia pushed her purple flowers,
Sister’d by many a yellow starry bloom,
Anri graceful things with spike or panicle,
Chalice or bell, to us all rare and strange.
THE CAMP WITHIN THE WEST.
0 did you see a troop go by
Way-weary and oppressed,
Dead kisses oh the drooping lip
And a dpad heart in the breast ?
Yea, I-have seen them one by one
' Way-weary and oppressed,
And when I asked them, “ Whither speed?”
They answered “ To the West ! ”
And were they pale as pale could bo—
Death pale with haunted eyes,
And did you see the hot white dust
Range round their feet and,rise ?
- O',-they were paleas pale could be,’
A nd pale as embered lea f ;
The hot white dust had risen, but
They laid it with their grief.
Did no one say the way is long,
And crave a little rest ?
0 no, they said, “-The night is nigh,
Our camp is in. the , West !
And did pain pierce their feet, as though
The way with thorns were set,
And were they visited by strange
Dark angels of regret ? ”
O yes, and some were mute as death.
Though shot by many a dart,
With them the salt of inward tears
. Went stinging through the heart.
And how are these wayfarers called,
And’whither do they wend'? ’
The Weary - Hearted—and their road
At minuet hath an end.
Shed tears for them . . . Nay, nay, no tears
They yearn for endless rest;
Perhaps large stars will burn above
Their camp within the West.

Failing Manhood.
When your manhood begins

Do your faoil nerves twitch ?
Are you all run down ?
Spots before the eyes?

Have you shooting pains?
Do you feel fatigued?
Are you weary ?
Does your head swim ?
Do your knees knock?
Does your hand tremble?
Nervous Twitchings.
A nervous man easily gets all
of a tremble, gets fits of weak-
ness, fits of twitchings. Here are
some of his symptoms :
Do your eyes twitch?
Do your hands tremble?
Do you feel shaky ?
Can you walk without halting?
Are you melancholy?
Do you have ringing in the ears?
Is your blood poor ?
Are your eyes weak?
Are your eyes bleary?
Does your head swim?

Verse at a Venture.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Black Sheep.
By WILL H. OGILVIE.
They shepherd their Black Sheep down to the
ships,
Society’s banned and cursed ;
And the boys look back as the old land dips—
Some with a reckless laugh on their lips,
And some with a prayer reversed.
And it's Goodbye, England l and Goodbye, Love !
And maybe ’tis just as well
When a man falls short of his Heaven above
That he drop to the uttermost Hell.
And the anchor lifts and the sails are set:
!Now God to your help, Black Sheep !
For the tray world laughs ** They will soon for-
get!”
But fired in the embers of old regret
The brand of the world bites deep.
They turn their Black Sheep over the side
To land on a stranger’s shores ;
To drift with the cities’ human tide,
Or wander away where the rovers ride
And the flagless legion wars.
And Hope for some is a broken staff,
And for others a golden stair,
Who live for the echo of Love’s low laugh
Or Somebody’s face in a photograph,
Or a coil of Somebody’s hair.
And some that have carried a parting gift
May kiss it and fling it away
Far over the clouds that no winds lift
To follow where our dead hopes drift
And rest where dead hopes may.
They bury the Black Sheep out in the Bush
And bury them none too deep
On the cattle camps and the last gold rush,
And the grasses grow over them green and lush
And the bush-winds sing them to sleep.
And it’s Goodbye, Struggle ! and Goodbye, Strife l
And maybe’tis just as well
When a man goes down in the Battle of Life
That he shorten his road to Hell /
Memory Town.
From dawning to dusk moves the crowd in her
street
With eyes looking upward, quick pulses that beat,
And slow feet that loiter, and dumb lips that call
Where sunshine and shadow are crossed for them
all.
In mystic mosaic her pavements are set,
A stone for Sweet Thoughts, then a stone for
Regret.
The walls of her houses are handsome and high,
Whose balconies break the blue line of the sky.
On one side the sun fires the columns with light,
On one side the shadow lies blacker than night.
On one side Youth’s Joys from their windows look
down
To watch the wayfarers in Memory Town.
They laugh with low music that each understands
And wave their white kerchiefs and kiss their
white hands.
They fling their gay garlands, white roses and red;
But Care gallops past us and tramples them dead!
On the other side stand the Gray Griefs of the
years,
The hands on the railings are wet with their
tears.
With sad eyes and wistful they lean and look
down
On the lone hearts that loiter in Memory Town.
They bind no white garland, and weave no red
wreath
But strew the dark cypress that whispers of Death.
So sunlight and shadow are crossed in the crown
That the old years have wrought us in Memory
Town.
The Front Bank.
We fight on far tracks unknown ;
We ride the way of the rover,
Each with a line of his own ;
Our banner the blue sky over,
Our bugle the bushwind’s tone.
We charge where no red squares kneel,
We ride with no helmets glitt’ring,
We carry no gleaming steel;
But our reins are foam to the bit-ring,
Our spurs are red to the heel.
We war by the watching stars,
No women look to our wounded,
No white hands bandage our scars ;
Fjr us no medals are rounded
No ribbons or clasps or bars.
Swordless and swift we go;
O ir brown arms bared to the slaughter,
Our hearts with the quest aglow ;
We battle and ask no quarter,
Our faces turned to the foe.
At last in the smoke of the years
Far from where camp and tent lie,
From clashing of shields and spears,
We slip to the earth so gently
That scarcely a comrade hears.
We slip to the earth and lie
Clay-cold in the golden grasses,
White faces turned to the sky ;
And the last of our longings passes,
The last of our dreams goes by.
. But the drums beat, year to year,
And men from the wings close round us,
And men ride up from the rear
To win—where no smile has crowned us,
Or lose—where it costs no tear.

whether in the form of Political Social, or other Articles,

communications will be regarded as strictly confidential.

desire those sending MS. to distinctly understand that

SATURDAY, OCTOBER 15, 1898.
The N.S.W. Marine Board.
The New South Wales Marine Board, which
has been advertised of late in a variety of
ways, is down in the Ministerial programme
as an institution whose extinction is to be
attended to. But many other institutions
have been down on the same list for many
years, yet they still survive ; and, unless the
public keeps an eye on its Marine Board,
that aged and incapable institution will pro-
bably live for a long while yet. The politi-
cal interests involved are comparatively few,
and all the votes that could be secured by
attention to this matter are hardly enough to
be worth counting. The seamen are the
class most interested, being the one most
frequently drowned under the present sys-
tem, but seamen are not a political power of
any magnitude. A very large part of the
mariner class has no vote ; it is merely a
casual Hans or Jorgensen who has dropped
into an Australian port in passing ; and the
part which has a vote is not very numerous,
and is more often absent on polling-day than
any other section of the community. The sea-
faring vote which is actually recorded at
any given time is inconsiderable. The
Marine Board’s methods may help to drown
a few passengers now and then ; but the
average passenger is very little acquainted
with nautical details, and if he gets ashore
after a wreck he seldom knows exactly what
struck him. It isn’t the interest of the
captain or the owner to enlighten him,
either. And if the Marine Board, in
its own tired way, lets vessels go to sea im-
perfectly equipped, and these vessels come
to grief, the Board’s tired investigation after-
wards is not likely to bring out very clearly
that the Board’s own tiredness and incapacity
had anything to do with the disaster. Also
the Board, being gentlemanly and perfunc-
tory, and mainly representative of the ship-
owning class, gives satisfaction to the bulk
of the shipowners, so their influence, at all
events, won’t go to actively help on its ex-
tinction.
It is questionable if the Marine Board ever
showed any signs of efficiency—except in
one direction only. It doesn’t profess to in-
spect or supervise the mere cargo vessel
unless incited thereto by a demand from the
crew that the old tub shall be inspected ; if
there is no such demand the cargo vessel
is practically under no supervision at all.
To all intents and purposes the Board is
authorised to leave the cargo ship alone, and
the efficient manner in which it has left the
cargo ship alone is beyond all praise. But
it is generally understood that the passenger
vessel is under strict supervision. It has to
carry sufficient boats to accommodate every-
body on board in case of necessity, and the
condition of these boats is supposed to be
under rigid scrutiny. The supply of life-
buoys and danger-signals and other appli-
ances, the nature and sufficiency of the
passenger accommodation, the smellfulness
of things, the ventilation, the fire-extinguish-
ing apparatus, the alleged water-tight bulk-
heads which are not water-tight, and which
have doors in them that won’t fasten—all
these and other matters are alleged to be
carefully attended to. And there is every
reason to believe that they are not.
* * * *
Up to some time in 1894 a distinction was
made in New South Wales in the treatment
of foreign - going as apart from coastal
steamers. The former were inspected on
leaving Sydney by a person strangely de-
scribed as Surveyor to the Emigration [De-
partment, who saw that they were reasonably
safe and wholesome. The coastal vessels
were under the glazed and filmy eye of the
Marine Board, which apparently saw no-
thing. The results were so bad that in 1894
the Collector of Customs refused to clear
the over-crowded vessels in the Westralian
trade unless they were subjected to the same
survey as the foreign-going craft, and the
system of inspection then inaugurated showed
how effectively the Board had slumbered at
its post in previous years. The following
were some of the discoveries made :
S.S. : 255 passengers. Had berths for 3XO steer-
age passengers though only sufficient space for 238.
Access to women’s compartment was through single men’s
open berth compartment only. Three life-boats were in
very bad order, a large number of the air chambers hav-
ing holes in them. There were no means whatever of
connecting any fire-hose to the pumps, as none of the
hose had couplings on them.
: Had berths for 186 passengers. Proper space
for 94 only. The steerage was excessively crowded, and
one small and steep ladderway was the only means of
access to the deck. Boats were in very bad order and
very indifferently equipped. Two of the boats were con-
demned here as being quite unfit for the service. The
fire hose were defective and insufficient. Crew: boat-
swain, carpenter, and two able seaman ; the rest boys.
Vessel detained.
. : Life boats in very bad order. Fire hose defec-
tive. Life belts defective.
: Practically no fire hose and distress signals
deficient.
* • * »
Some more samples :
: The watertight doors in the principal bulk-
head of this vessel were found to be out of order, and not
capable of being closed on an emergency. Vessel was
leaving for Newcastle when I made this discovery, with
the intention of filling bunkers with coal, after which the
bunkers could not be got at . . . The Marine Board de-
clined to take any action in the matter.
: This vessel, having space for 290 steerage
passengers, had berths for 417, and recently landed as
many as 432 passengers at Fremantle.
and : These vessels have each space for
246 steerage passengers, but carried berths for 299 each.
... I have now caused the berths to be reduced to the
proper number, and in this I had the concurrence of the
inspector of the Sydney Board. But they (the Board)
were not prepared to take the initiative.
: When this vessel was under survey, prior to the
fire which took place on board, I had an additional fire-
hose placed on board. And I have since been informed
that but for that length of hose the fire could not have
been got under control. . . . The same vessel on her last
survey was carrying old and badly-conditioned boats in
the davits intended for the principal lifeboats.
: The first time this vessel came under the
Emigration Survey it was found that there was no means
of closing the doors of the principal water-tight bulkhead,
although the vessel had just passed the Marine Board
survey.
: A similar defect existed, overlooked by the
Marine Board for several years.
* * * »
This much-needed supervision went on for
two years. Then, in 1896, the control of
the work was transferred to the Marine
Board—the same institution which had pre-
viously let everything slide so affably, and
the Board soon got rid of the surveyor
(Captain Vine Hall) who made all this pain-
ful worry about defective boats, and unwork-
able fire-hose, and overcrowded ships, and
alleged watertight bulkheads with doors that
wouldn’t shut. So things dropped back into
the old groove, and have remained there
ever since. There was no known reason for
the change, unless it might be that all this
activity disturbed the Board in its sleep.
So far, no big holocaust can be directly
traced to the Marine Board’s laxity, though,
when there is such a thing, it is hardly likely
that-the Marine Board will do the tracing
with sufficient energy to bring the deed home
to its own door and deposit it on its own
doorstep. Such a thing would be too much
to expect of any Board" And with luck the
Board’s method of doiixg its work may
produce no serious disaster for a long time.
On the other hand, the disaster may come
any day. The situation, so far as the public
is concerned, is this : The Marine Board is
partly nominated by the Government and
partly elected by the shipowners. Also, the
elective members are themselves shipowners.
So far as it is a representative institution it is
solely a shipowners’ institution. In its judi-
cial capacity, its business is largely to try the
shipowner. The sailors and passengers and
the public at large constitute the plaintiff,
and the shipowner is the accused, and the
unwritten charge includes everything that
can be done in the way of endangering
human life by sending rotten or badly-
equipped vessels to sea. And, so far
as the Board is elective, it is elected
solely by the accused ; it is a case ’of
the suspected burglar electing the Chief-
Justice with the additional proviso that
only a person suspected of burglary is quali-
fied for election as Chief-Justice. If the
elected member supports an inspector who
insists on too much expenditure in the
way of life-saving apparatus, or is too much
given to inquiring into the condition of the
old vessel which left its bones in the deep
sea—well, the shipowner is as human as any
other human being, and he isn’t likely to
elect that member to his salaried billet
again. But it is very unlikely he would
make the mistake of electing any such mem-
ber in the first place. And the member
being himself a shipowner has his own ships
inspected by his subordinate, and the subor-
dinate is supposed to peremptorily stop his
boss’s or his boss’s friend’s “ cronk ” ship
from going to sea,, and to give evidence, if
necessary, before his boss’s board about the
lamentable state of his boss’s ship. And
the said boss is supposed to appoint a man
who will take an absolutely unbiassed view
of the boss’s ship, and to bear him no ill-
feeling for doing it. Which is all so pro-
bable that it is simply bursting with proba-
bility.
* * * *
To put the matter shortly : No shipowner
should sit on the Board which may have to
inquire into the condition of his own ship.
No shipowner should have a voice in electing
the Board which is to inquire into the con-
dition of his ships. The board shouldn’t be
crowded with ancient mariners who haven’t
been actively engaged in their profession for
20 or 30 years, \vho know practically nothing
of seamanship as it is now —the ancient
brig and schooner and ketch crowd to
whom the practical working of a steamer
is a black, blank mystery. And the
ancient mariners should be paid a suffi-
cient salary to make them independent of
any income from any private pursuit con-
nected with shipping and shipowners. Of
course, if the public, which pays the
salaries, wants the present kind of Board it
is entitled to have it. But in case it doesn’t
want this kind of Board it is only fair that
the public should know what kind of a Board
it has got.
The Middleman in Some of His Aspects.
The South Australian Early Closing Bill—the
one which states in its interpretation clause
that “ woman ” means a “person of the female
sex ” —is another encouraging outrage on the
liberty of the subject. It provides for the
shutting of certain classes of shops at six, and,
in the shops which are exempted from closure
at fixed times, it is provided that no female or
child shall be employed over 48 hours, and no
adult male over 52 hours, per week. Which,
after all, will mean that exactly the same
amount of work will be done, and,"in the main,
by the same people—all the jeremiads about
the ruin of the tradesman notwithstanding.
The great mass of the people spend all their
earnings, and must needs do so even if the
shops only kept open four hours per day—the
sufficient reason being that they have to spend
all their earnings in order to keep sufficient
clothes on them and sufficient victuals in them ;
therefore the early-closing movement will make
no difference, so far as this very large class is
concerned, in the takings. And, in the great
mass of cases, the fact that the business is com-
pressed into fewer hours won’t necessitate the
employment of any more hands. Innine-tenthsof
the shops of any town or city—the smaller ones
that is—a large proportion of the time of the
lone, lorn proprietor is spent in leaning up
against things to fill in the intervals between
customers, and a good deal more in furtively
blowing the dust off the stock, which dust has
gathered there because there are not enough
customers to keep the stock in a reasonable
state of disturbance. And if his customers are
compelled to do their business in eight hours
instead of in twelve, lie can stand the rush per-
fectly well without having to hire extra assist-
ance. The universal provider who, as a rule,
manages to keep his staff at high pressure all
the 12 hours may be compelled to hire extra
assistance if his business has to be done in two-
thirds of the time, but if so it will be so much
the better for the labor-market. And the
universal-providery man, as a rule, can afford
to stand the shock.
* * * »
The early-closing idea, however, valuable as
it is, only touches the fringe of a great subject.
The whole art and mystery of shop-keeping is
a social problem. The shopkeeper, to begin with,
is a non-producer; he adds nothing to the aggre-
gate wealth of the community (wealth being a
term of wide application which covers every
kind of useful furnishing for the body or mind);
therefore it is to the community’s interest that
there should be only as many of him as are
actually required. It is to the community’s
interest that every wholly non-producing class
should be kept down to the smallest convenient
dimensions, because the non-producer has to
live on the producer—somehow. The way he
does it may be none too clear, but the fact is
incontrovertible, for there is no one save the
producer that the non-producer can live upon.
And shopkeepers—wholesale and retail, but
principally retail—are probably twice or three
times as numerous as there is any need for.
Instead of there being just about as many as
are needed to satisfy the public requirements,
there are, in the cities and large towns especi-
ally, just as many as can possibly eke out a
living at the game, and a large margin in addi-
tion. The margin slops over periodically into
the Insolvency Court and elsewhere. There is
probably about as large a superfluity in the shop-
keeping class as in any other ; and when the
vast superfluity of lawyers, brokers, financiers,
and all the vast crowd of mysteries who seem
to live on nothing in an office is taken into con-
sideration, this is saying a great deal*
* * * *
The losses, so far as the public is eoncernfed,
appear in so many \VaVs that they abe difficult
to enumerate. The producer, who pays fbr
everything ih the tend, has to find the rent for
All the superfluous shops. He has to pay for
the sickly glimmer of gas inside, and to find
the proprietors and their families in food, and
clothing, and taxes, and miscellaneous ex-
penses. If there are two or three miles of
superfluous shops in a large city lie has to keep
these two or three miles of roadway in repair.
If the superfluous trader goes insolvent and
cloesn t pay for his stock it makes no difference
in the ultimate loss. The population can only
buy a certain quantity of food, and clothes,
and boots and other things, and if these goods
are distributed in 10,000 shops where 5000
could do all the trade, then the prices must be
higher to pay for the rent of the other 5000
establishments, and to support the people who
run them, and to pay for the gas and shndrifes;
And even this doesn’t tepresent the whole loss.
If there are 60,000 adults ih a community and
10,000 of them are trying to live bv
being middle-men of various degrees, it is ob-
vious that the 50,000 producers must
needs support the 10,000 middle-men somehow.
And, if 5000 are sufficient to do all the middle-
man work which actually wants doing, and the
number can be reduced to that, and the other
5000 can be forced into the producing classes,
then, instead of 50,000 producers supporting
10,000 non-producers, it becomes a case of
55,000 supporting only 5000. The superfluity
of the other 5000 can’t be wholly gauged by the
amount of time which the small' melancholy,
unprosperous shopkeeper spends leaning up
against something in absolute idleness. If there
are two cheap bootmakers in a street where
one would suffice, the time which the other
bootmaker spends in putting his stock out in
the morning, and taking it in at night, and
cleaning his big window, and blowing the dust
off his wares—the dust which comes from lack
of business caused by being a superfluous boot-
maker, and putting the big fat price-tickets on
his goods, and doing many other things—is all
loss and waste. And the community pays.
* « * „
Of course, any interference with the super-
fluous non-producer would be an interference
with the liberty of the subject. But, seeing
that the non-producer must needs live on the
producer, the latter has a fair and legitimate
excuse for interference. Some day, probably,
he will interfere by calling on everybody who
runs a shop in any town or city, or who lives
by any kind of mysterious expedient in an
office, to pay a tolerably heavy annual license-
fee. Such a proceeding ought to produce quite
a number of useful results. It would supply
revenue, and help to relieve the burden of the
producer. It would considerably reduce the
number of middlemen, and probably the re-
mainder would make so much better a living
that they could pay the fee without increasing
their prices. It would increase the number of
producers by forcing a portion of the unpro-
ductive class into other occupations. It would
help to keep down the city population, which
is much too large in proportion to the size of
the community for the profit of the nation at
large. Also, it is too large proportionately fot
the good of the nation’s health ; a community
with a capacious chest and a robust lung and a
powerful muscular development can’t be grown
in the shops of the city streets. The
community already asserts its right to
charge a license-fee to the hawker, who is
merely an itinerant shopkeeper, or even
to refuse his license if the non-producer who
comes round to the back-door is growing too
numerous ; and its right to license the shop-
keeper, who is simply a stationary hatvker, is
quite as incontrovertible. It licenses the grog-
seller, and retains full power to limit the num-
ber of dealers in grog where there are already
enough to supply all the grog that the com-
munity seems to require; and it is quite as
reasonable to license the dealers in boots and
sugar, and to restrict them if they appear to be
growing more numerous than is necessary,
seeing that the existence of twice as many
dealers in boots as are really essential only
results in the public paying more for its boots.
Ihe superfluous men’s rent and living expenses
must come out of the price of boots somewhere.
Up to a certain point competition reduces
prices by preventing monopoly. Beyond that
point it raises them, by compelling the com-
munity to support too many people and too
many people’s landlords, and to pay too many
gas-bills, out of its aggregate boot expenditure.
* * * *
In a casual sort of way the compulsory early-
closing idea is a recognition of this evil. When
there are more people trying to live on the
boot-trade than the boot-industry can support
there is always a temptation on the part of
boot-dealer No. 1 to remain open an hour later
to snatch the last possible remnant in the way
of sales, and the natural result is that boot-em-
poriums Nos. 2 to 100 are apt to keep open as
late, or even later, to prevent No. 1 annexing
an undue proportion of a trade that none of
them can afford to lose. And the end of it is
that the whole crowd remain open to the very
last hour when it is possible to make enough
profit out of the very last boot-buyer to pay
for the gas. And the miserable employe natur-
ally sutlers ; there are no more boots bought
in the aggregate, for even if all the boot-shops
in Christendom remained open night and day
no one would have more than two feet after all,
therefore there are no more profits, and the ex-
tra hours bring no more wages. The compul-
sory early-closing idea is an attempt by the
State to remedy one result of excessive compe-
tition and to save the employ e from the em-
ployer ; also, incidentally, to save the employer
from himself and his gas-bill. And it is quite
as reasonable to go a step further and compul-
sorily reduce competition among the non-pro-
ductive trading class by licensing the whole lot,
and by reserving the right, in extreme cases,
to refuse licenses. In this respect it is neces
sary to draw a sharp line between the pro-
ducer and the non-producer. The middleman
is a necessary evil, and, when he grows to the
dimensions of an unnecessary evil, the unneces-
sary part of. him calls for active discourage
ment.


PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Municipal One-Man-One-Vote.
It is curious to find two such Tory authorities
as the Argus and Mayor M'Eacharn advo-
cating the establishment of a big corporation full
of Socialistic ambitions, out of the 20 tin-pot
cities and boroughs of Melbourne. M‘Eacharn
even urges that Melbourne should follow the
splendid example of Glasgow, Birmingham and
London; that the corporation should run the
trams, buy up electric-light works and other
public utilities, establish baths and washhouses,
buy up slum properties and erect suitable
lodging houses and artisans’ dwellings thereon,
provide municipal bands, improve the ap-
proaches to the city, lay out drives and public
squares, and provide technical education. A
large order, but perfectly feasible. Also, most
thinking people in Melbourne now hold it to be
essential that the City Council, when it comes,
shall take over the business of that horribly-
confused pale elephant, the Metropolitan
Board of Works, and fire out Bard Fitzgibbon
from the mismanagement thereof. The great
Fitzgibbon institution is incapable, slow, dun-
derheaded, and practically insolvent. It gets on
so slowly with its work that instead of connecting
15,000 houses every year with the new drains as
promised, it barely overtakes 2500, at which
rate the whole work will take some 30 or 40
years to finish. It has a deficit of ,£40,000 a
year, and it made a dead loss of <£265,000 by
putting a large proportion of one of its loans
into the most notoriously rotten bank of issue
in Melbourne. Also, when it sent its Fitz-
gibbon loan-floating in London that scrambling
old poet lost nearly ,£BO,OOO in one lot by sell-
ing a 4 per cent, loan at ,£9l 17s. 6d. per £lOO.
And the Melbourne city corporation, with its
huge debts and deficits, and its yearning to
build monuments in the shape of useless mar-
kets, is nearly as bad. There is plenty of
reason why these institutions and the 19 other
corporations should be led out and gently pole-
axed in a quiet place, and a County Council on
the London pattern created in their stead.
Where the trouble comes in is just here. Lon-
don didn’t achieve its success by simply creat-
ing its County Council. It never had a decent
municipal Government till it went in for
one-man-one-vote in municipal matters.
Its old plural property-vote corporations smelt
just like the Thames in its old, unclean days,
and the change only came in when the pro-
perty-vote was abandoned. Exactly why the
difference happened is a matter for conjecture.
Probably it was partly because, when the
municipal and Parliamentary votes were prac-
tically assimilated, it became worth the while
of an able and ambitious politician to reach out
for a municipal billet, by way of getting a
firmer hold on his Parliamentary constituents.
And probably it was chiefly because the
multiple-property-vote puts the bulk of the
power in the hands of the landlord, v/ho lives
in a nice, broad, clean street, and he doesn’t
need to care what happens to the streets that
are nasty and narrow and foul. In fact, his
interest lies in leaving them as they are, in
order to save the expense of improvement.
Until the votes of the people who actually live
down the disgusting back-lane become of as
much direct importance to the municipal can-
didate as those of any other people, the dis-
gusting back-lane is likely to receive little
attention. And that is just what one-man-one-
vote in municipal affairs is meant for.

The Missing’ Allotment-Owner.
A useful little measure to enable municipali-
ties to let or sell land on which five years’ rates
were unpaid, and thus recover their money,
foundered last week in N.S.W. Assembly.
According to the daily-paper report :
Mr. E. M. Clark opposed the measure on the ground
that it would inflict a lasting injustice on the thousands
of landowners who are annually wrecked on desert
islands, and are never rescued until after five years. It
might also lead to valuable city properties being sold for
a few shillings, because the holders had lost all their
money gambling in South Africa, and were not able to
pay their passage back to Australia in time to claim their
property.
Apart from this cheerful blob of humor, the
idea is a good one, and should be universally
adopted. If it had been adopted 40 years ago
it would have been better still. Australia is
dotted all over with bits of land which were
bought as a spec, by some ancient pioneer, who
afterwards pegged out somewhere beside a dry
creek, or otherwise vanished from moral
ken and became part of the great Billjim
mystery. His allotment has 25 years’ rates
due on it, which the municipality much re-
quires ; and it is an unsightly gap beside the
street, with old kerosene-tins on it, and it is
mainly useful for the small boy to swear upon.
The unclaimed allotment is locked-up capital,
and anything that will put it in circulation is
good for the community, for prosperity doesn’t
mean abundance of capital so much as the
vigorous circulation of what capital there is.
Moreover, the proposed .law would be a just
retribution on the absentee. Many vacant
eyesore allotments in and around our cities are
owned by gentlemen in Europe who pay no
rates, but are quite willing that their proper-
ties should be improved and made valuable by
the rates of other people. The seizure and sale
of the allotment owned by the gentleman in
Europe seems highly advisable.

The Upheaval of Donald.
The enfant terrible of the Vic. Leg. Council is
Donald Melville, who makes most scandal-
ously truthful comments on bis opulent mates.
Only last week this disgraceful rich landlord
remarked that Fitzgerald, the Great Fitz-
gerald, had been “asleep for the last 35 years.”
Also Melville informed Dr. Embling that he
was assuming the People of Victoria to be the
people who surrounded him at his club, and
sang his praises at public-houses. After which
the shocking old capitalist upbraided Fatman
in general for “ thinking more of property than
of manhood.” This cannot be allowed to go om
The Vic. Leg. Council is more or less a syndi-
cate for securing the re-election of its own
members, but The Bulletin must respect-
fully suggest to President Zeal and co. that
Donald Melville’s admission to the House
was a sad mistake. Probably he got there
under false pretences, but it really doesn’t
matter whether he was a traitor to the cause all
the time, or became a traitor from conviction
after a year’s experience of Leg. Council
“debates.” Enough that he is now unfitted
to express the feeling of the country, and does
not obstruct business in a whole-hearted man-
ner. The House despises its Donald Melville,
of course, for he is merely one man against 47
as a rule, but if those 47 ask The Bulletin
whether the odd man’s presence is or is not an
Insult to the House The Bulletin’s answer is
Yes. He ought to be thrown out unceremoni-
ously, as though he were an improved Land
Tax or any other dangerous measure.

Railways and Wool Exports.
For the quarter ended 30th September, ’9B,
N.S.W. exported 121,000 bales of wool in all,
and of this quantity 73,000 bales were shipped
direct from N.S.W. ports. On the other hand,
39,000 bales went via Melbourne, and 8600
went by South Australian ports. The quantity
shipped direct from N.S.W. ports was 1351
bales more than in the corresponding period of
’97 ; the quantity shipped via Melbourne
showed an increase of 3732 bales. The N.S. W.
wool-export business, in its relation to the
Federal railway problem, calls for a lot of hard
consideration. At present the parochial crowd,
which desires that each province shall keep
its railways under its own separate manage-
ment, is distinctly on top, and the general
ignorance of the multitude, and the general
incapacity of politicians, are likely to keep it in
that lamentable situation. Yet there are plenty
of obvious and admitted disadvantages about
the provincial system, among which the follow-
ing are the most conspicuous :
(1) It involves the cost of six managements instead of
one.
(2) It perpetuates the break of gauge, with the result
that goods have to be painfully and expensively loaded
and unloaded at the borders. This trouble will get worse
as railways increase, and there are ten border junctions
for every one now existing. The break of gauge trouble
can’t be cured except by Federal control, for the pro-
vinces can’t possibly agree to any fair division of the cost
of unification. The only way is to have one system, and
charge the cost of unification to the general fund without
any division.
(3) The present system also involves the cost of build-
ing 500 miles of line where, under federal management,
200 would be ample. For it requires that every place in
N.S.W. shall have a line to Sydney or no line at all, even
if Melbourne or Adelaide is 300 miles nearer.
(4) Many places which are near to Melbourne or Ade-
laide, but very far from Sydney, have consequently to do
with no line at all. Therefore, the progress of the out-
lying parts of N.S.W. is retarded, population doesn’t
increase, the State loses land revenue, land-tax, income-
tax, and other profits.
(5) Many other places in N.S.W. have to pay twice, or
more than twice, the freights that would be otherwise
necessary, or else their goods have to be carried at a loss
and the loss made good by levying exorbitant rates in
other districts.
(6) The country is loaded with needless debt and need-
less interest through building 500 miles of line in many
places to connect with “ our ” city when 200 miles to con-
nect with the nearest city—the one across the border—
would suffice.
And all this loss and worry and foolishness is
to no purpose after all. About one-fourth of
N.S.W. is nearer to Melbourne than to Sydney,
and despite the fact that N.S.W. stands the
heavy loss of five unprofitable railways to drag
the Riverina trade to Sydney, Victoria takes
more than a fourth of the trade after all. It
gets the entire amount that would naturally
go its way if all this dead loss were
avoided, and N.S.W. ceased protecting its
railway industry by cutting rates, and build-
ing needless lines, and followed the Free-
trade principle of letting commerce take its
natural outlet. The object of the present
policy is to develop “ our ” territory, and it
rather hinders than aids development by
making it impossible to supply railways in
many outlying districts. Its other object is to
retain “our ” trade, and the trade, after all the
millions wasted in retaining it, refuses to be
retained any more than it was before. The
short way out of the difficulty is to put railway
management and construction under Federal
control, and abandon the hopeless struggle. No
policy that will ever be devised can make 500
miles as short as 200, and that is just what is
the matter.

Ah. Toy and the Color-Line.
The juristic works in the legal alcoves of the
Ark, no doubt, contained the maxim that eter-
nal vigilance is the price of Liberty. Victoria
showed rather effectually a few years ago how
to preserve her population from undue admix-
ture with saffron or leprosy or Confucian con-
fusion. Although she had a Chinese Restriction
Act with a poll-tax, she denied that the pay-
ment of this tax gave any alien any right to
land in Victoria, and promptly warned off a
crowd, including Ah Toy, from her shores.
Her action, though disapproved in her own
courts, was upheld by the Privy Council. It is
somewhat mysterious why, in the face of all the
talk against alien immigration to the Australias,
this power is not made use of by the growling
Governments. Victoria had a hard fight to as-
sert the principle : why don’t the others profit
by and support her victory ? Any Government
in Australia could, under that Privy Council
decision, prevent a single Chinese, Japanese,
Javanese, or, if they wished, Kanaka, from
landing in its province. Imperial dictation is
the only bar, and the “ crimson thread” busi-
ness is genuine enough to make the probability
of that negligible. If the Governments don’t
act on that principle, and if they continue to
sympathise with those who don’t want the
Asiatic, the only conclusion one can come to is
that the said Governments aren’t sincere in the
matter. With eternal vigilance, in Ah Toy
fashion, upon every ship from Asia for a few
years, there would be no color-line problem
and there would be several sincere Governments
in Australia.

The Line that Can’t he Drawn.
When a Sydney jury, the other day, found
Napoleon Jean Lisson guilty of the slaughter
of his sister-in-law, they illogieally recom-
mended him to mercy, on the ground that “ he
was insane when he committed ” the act. For,
if insanity means irresponsibility, the ques-
tion of Lisson’s mental condition, before
or since the deed, should have been entirely
outside the record. No matter how sane a man
may have been all his lifetime, if he becomes
insane for five minutes he is for that five
minutes just as irresponsible as if he had been
a lunatic for half-a-century. And, in any
case, no matter how normal a man may be
at other times, the epileptic furor which,
according to Dr. Angel Money, was pro-
bably responsible for Lisson’s outbreak, is
known to be, while it lasts, an insanity as
acute and imperative as that of any chronic
homicidal mania. The point upon which the
judge in this case mainly relied as constituting
criminal responsibility was that Lisson was
conscious of the nature of his murderous acts.
Exactly through this point passes the line
of cleavage between the judicial conception of
insanity and the medical; because it is a
commonplace of alienism that a man may, by
either sudden maniacal impulse or overmaster-
ing fixed delusion, be forced helplessly into acts
of which he not only understands the nature
but actually has the utmost horror. The law is
doubtless compelled to some mechanical rule of
differentiation, but the forensic theory—as so
stringently insisted upon in this case—would,
if carried to its legitimate conclusion, exclude
from irresponsibility every mentally-affected
person who isn’t an obvious drivelling idiot.
And though, as the Judge said, there has not
yet been laid down any definition of
insanity which will entirely hold water,
there was nevertheless, in Lisson’s case, about
the best-obtainable practical test of insanity.
If a man is not mad who suddenly and cause-
lessly makes a murderous attack on those for
whom in his normal state he would probably
lay down his own life— (Lisson when he killed
his sister-in-law also savagely hacked at his own
two children) —we may as well strike the word
“ insanity ” out of the dictionary.
The Parson’s Business Circular.
With the accession of a new Postmaster-
General The Bulletin returns to an old subject
and desires to draw the attention of Hon,
Varney Parkes to the number of registered
publications allowed to pass through N.S.
Wales G.P.O. as newspapers, although not
coming under the definition of such laid down
by the Postal Act. This paper has before it a
copy of a four-page leaflet (one of about 60
similar publications) registered for transmission
as a newspaper and published by All Saints
Church, Petersham. The front page consists
entirely of the specification of the church ser-
vices for the month ; page 2 contains the offer-
tories, baptisms, and burials ; page 3, an
account of the tea-meeting, mission, and con-
firmation services ; and page 4, a list of
Sunday-school lessons. In direct violation
of the postal regulations also, there is in-
serted in the publication a circular (with
nothing to show, as required, specific
connection with the main sheet) expa-
tiating upon the merits of a deceased clergy-
man, and soliciting subscriptions towards a
memorial to the rev. defunct. The Bulletin
simply takes this particular “ newspaper ” at
random, out of a host of similar sheets—all
merely exploiting some insignificant kirk or
tin-pot bethel—which are being carried at
State expense, and which thus give the parson
a distinct advertising pull over the other trades-
man, who has to pay postage at Jd. each on his
business circulars. The R.C. person may not
object to the State carrying loads of Church of
England literature as long as it does ditto for
him —and vice versa; but this paper entirely
fails to see why the unsectarian citizen
should be penalised with the expense of this
neat little Church family arrangement. The
postal regulations are perfectly explicit upon
the point. Will P.M.G. Parkes allow a con-
tinuance of this flagrant violation thereof? If
he does, it will be more than ever plain that
State aid to religion has not been abolished in
N.S. Wales.

The little row--it was only a little one, after
all—which woke-up Queensland Assembly, last
week, was disappointing, inasmuch as it wasn’t
carried to its legitimate conclusion. It began
with a trivial altercation between Speaker
Cowley and Labor-member M ‘Donald, and
the new egg-bald Premier, climbing into the
breach, carried a motion suspending M'Donald
for a week. Then two officials arrived to carry
the suspended member out, but M'Donald
held one and Hoolan the other, and paralysed
the machine of State. If the Opposition,
having once got into a state of insurrection,
had risen unanimously just then, it might have
locked the Assembly in, and emptied the gum-
bottle on Dickson’s bare cranium, and forced
Speaker Cowley, under pain of being undressed
and inked all over, to suspend the whole Minis-
try for a fortnight, for, though it was in a
numerical minority, a good many of the Minis-
terialists are too old to count for much in a
free aud off-hand shindy. But, after shaking
the official for a minute or two, M'Donald
agreed to go out, and there was no fight after
all. The most exciting moment in the proceed-
ings was when Hoolan alluded to the Speaker
as a “contemptible little nigger - driver.”
Which remark was partially inaccurate as well
as unusual, for Cowley isn’t formidable enough
to drive any adult nigger.

The attention of the Social Gimlet Brigade,
the W.C.T.U. purists and others of that species,
who are perpetually prating about the pro-
tection of girls and age-of-consent, is hereby
cordially invited to the case of Charles
Howard, committed for trial at Fitzroy (Melb.)
Court, on Sept. 22nd. The girl in the case,
aged just 15, was induced by her companion-
one Minnie Harmer —to go to accused’s house
in Fitzroy, where, it is alleged, the offence was
committed in the presence of aforesaid Harmer.
It seems to have been a cash transaction, the
consideration being the sum of one shilling,
which the Harmer damsel promptly claimed as
hers, though subsequently the money was
divided. La Harmer corroborated the other
girl’s evidence, giving her own age as 18. The
man, as stated, was committed for trial, but
nothing was said about confining or restraining
Miss Harmer. Wherefore, the moral people
before-mentioned are desired to take her in
hand and exhibit her as a shining example of
the want of protection afforded to innocent
girls from the wiles of designing men.
Harry Stockdale, who probably knows more
about the alleged de Rougemont country than
any other living man, writes to The Bulletin :
While hardly considering the be Rougemont romance
worthy of serious contradiction, let me emphasise a few
points which should discredit this ingenious fictionist
even with the most credulous. If be R. had led for 28 years
such an existence as he describes, he would now be an
old and enfeebled man, and would to a certainty bear on
his person some of the raised markings customary
amongst the people in question, even if he had not under-
gone circumcision or the terrible “ Sturt’s rite.” 2.
There is no Australian tribe of which something is not
known, and with none of them has been found any
knowledge of the manufacture or use of metal. 3. The
universality of the exchange and barter custom amongst
the aborigines makes it incredible that, if the gold
utensils be R. describes existed, no other white man
should ever have heard of them. 4. The boomerang is
unknown in the locality referred to by be R. The natives
for hundreds of miles of the adjacent territory are spear-
men pure and simple ; and, even if the boomerang had
been used against him, it would not have been the “ re-
turning ” missile but the heavier war-boomerang, which
is not constructed so as to come back to the thrower.
Besides these, there are scores of apparently insignificant
points which, to anybody knowing the country and people
in question as I do, would be quite sufficient of theniselves
utterly to discount this ingenious Switzer’s veracity.
[Re The Bulletin’s recent observation that,
if anybody could throw light on this de Rouge-
mont business, it would be Harry Stockdale, it
now turns out that de R.’s island is actually
identical with La Crosse Island, in Cambridge
Gulf entrance, which Stockdale rented for
some time from the W. A. Government. — Ed. B.]

“ Disgusted ” to The Bulletin :
Premier Reib, in his speech on the censure motion,
laid much stress on the pluck of his Government in
tackling civil service reform, and on the number of votes
it lost thereby. He so well knew the jelly-fish nature of
said service as to be aware that he could jump on it with
all his feet. When the Crown appealed to the Privy
Council against the decision of N.S.W. Full Court in the
case of Abams v. Young, civil servants were asked
by circular to assist Abams (a prominent civil
servant, of ability and integrity, who had been dismissed
under the plea of retrenchment, and replaced by a
temporary official), who had already borne heavy expense,
in vindicating their rights. Three thousand circulars
were posted at a cost of £7 10s., and responses from under a
dozen, totalling £5 10s., were received ! The Crown
appeal was dismissed with costs, but Abams has since
been non-suited on a point which his legal advisers main-
tain has already been decided by the Privy Council. He
has now again appealed to the Full Court. But the
noble army of public servants are unanimous in allowing
him to do it at his own cost. Even those of the retrenched
brigade, who are ready to serve writs or have already
served them in anticipation of reaping the fruits of the
fight which Abams has fought, have, with one or two ex-
ceptions, refrained from assisting. Surely such an
emasculated lot deserve any treatment a callous Premier
prompted by a needy Treasury may inflict.
Sagasta attributes the reverses of Spain to
national anaemia ; and Lord Salisbury spoke
recently of the inevitable transfer of the
possessions of decadent races to their more
vigorous neighbors. History is full of such
instances. Two centuries after the conquest of
Asia by Alexander the Great the Romans
humbled Greece to the status of a province,
only to succumb in their turn to the barbarians
of the North. The might of the Crescent,
which long threatened to bring all Europe
under the Mohammedan scimitar, has in our
own day become the “Sick Man.” At the
present time the three races which stand out
in vigorous pre-eminence are the Anglo-Saxon,
the Slav, and the German. Although France,
the only nation that counts beside these three,
still dominates civilisation by her national
elegance, and wit, and gaiety, the three
races named have hopelessly outstripped her
by sheer force of reproduction—by big bat-
talions of babies. It seems probable that the
weight of Anglo-Saxon power will be largely
transferred in the coming century to the shores
of the Pacific ; and that Russia will be the
great Asiatic power, assimilating or being as-
similated by the Mongolian hordes into a new
civilisation ; while Germany, with her disci-
plined energy, farseeing statecraft, and philo-
sophical insight, will become the great middle
kingdom of Europe, gradually absorbing her
smaller neighbors until her dominion stretches
from [the Skagerack to Salonika, and possibly
through Asia Minor to the Persian Gulf. This
seems to be the geographical trend of national
expansion : but what social cataclysms, what
internal revolutions—intellectual, moral, and
political—will be passed through can be only
dindy guessed at. The 19th century has been
an era of scientific and mechanical triumphs. In
this age men have learnt to work. In the 20th
century will they learn to live ?
One of the contradictions of N.S.W. Parlia-
mentary Government glaringly asserted itself
last week, when after it was loudly proclaimed
that no M.L.A. should vote to put money in
his own pocket, all the Ministers of the Crown
cast their votes to repel a censure motion—
thereby securing themselves in office and conse-
quent pay. In several European and American
Legislatures, Ministers don’t vote when their
existence is at stake, and in some Australasian
Parliaments Premiers have been known to
voluntarily forego Ministerial votes on critical
occasions; but the general practice, especially
the N.S.W. one, is for the Premier to squat on
the bench when the division bell rings and vote
that he himself has the confidence of the coun-
try, and for his Ministers to do the same thing
in their , own regard. This is certainly voting
themselves office and pay; and when the
majority for the Government is only equal to
or less than the Ministerial votes cast the
viciousness of the practice is self-evident.

Some vigorous language re the little ways of
the Salvation Army :
Editor Bulletin, —Some more about the Blood and
Blazes Brotherhood. The social annual appeal pans-out
an average of £25,000 to £30,000. A progressive pro-
vincial town and district is expected to return about
£lOO. All officers are expected to canvass district within
a radius of 50 miles. No travelling exes, allowed. Mean-
time, captain and “sub” get their “screw” best way
they can out of the ordinary five-tinies-a-Sunday collec-
tions. When the “social appeal” is on no “screw”
is forthcoming. Lucky if they pay rent. Every
penny collected in the district is at once sent to
the C.W.O. in Sydney. There may be scores of Mag-
balens where that money was collected, but not a penny
is used to attempt to raise them from the mire, or any-
body else there. Centralisation up to the hilt! Then,
on top of the S.A. appeal come self-denial collections, and
the province commanding-officer fixes financial targets
for the officers, who in turn fix them for Brother Bang the
Drum and Sister Tambourine. And the thing goes on
again, so the town loses another “ monkey ”or so. And
the officers revel in the luxuiy of bread and dripping
or, say, liver. Wretched indeed is the condition of most
country captains, while the “big-guns” live in clover,
travel all over the shop, and, on an avt rage, support a
family of six. For head officers are invariably champion
well, mullipliers ! 7x 7.

Town Hall Popular Concerts.
Dante, a man of many wonders, made a
successful debut at the Palace Theatre, Syd-
ney. The Palace is just about the size for a
one-man show, and Dante fits it admirably. He
is a bright, clever performer who does a lot of the
old magic much more smartly than it has often
been done before (or else he patters in a fashion
that covers the joints in the performance better
than the patter that the usual patterer patters
with). Also, he throws in sundry items that are
not the old magic by any means. The lady whom
he sets on fire and reduces to one grinning skull
and a puff of fine ashes is probably the best
illusion in the list. It isn’t so wonderful as some
of the items, but the most wonderful items go
rather heavily by comparison ; there is so much
tying up and bagging and boxing of the victim
that the most astonishing freak on the bill strikes
one at last like an iron safe that has fallen on him
by appointment. When the big Jap. is bagged,
and put in a trunk, and locked and corded in, and
then that trunk is piled into another trunk and it
is laboriously locked and corded, and afterwards,
in exactly eight seconds, it happens quite unac-
countably that the big Jap. is outside, and, when
the trunks are unpacked, the small Jap.—who was
outside all the time the packing was done-—proves
to be inside, it is wildly amazing in a laborious
way, like dragging six tons of wonderful coals up-
hill. But the burnt lady is a quick, off-hand de-
lusion—just a woman and a blaze and a skull and
a shock ; and it goes better than all the rest.
* * » *
The present Professor gives a tremendous
amount for the money. His mesmeric show would
have made the fortune of Kennedy if that bulky
horror could have done anything like it ; and he
doesn’t jump on the stomach of his patient or
otherwise render himself disagreeable and un-
gentlemanly. His vanishing womah —which isn’t
the same as his burnt woman—is a pleasing little
fiction which takes well, and though there is a
certain streak of weariness about any magician’s
show when he is doing his 126th impossibility
about 11.5 p.m., still the capability of
the showman is beyond all question, and
Dante ought to burn his lady assistant profit-
ably through a long season. The illusionist him-
self is a slender young man, who does most of his
miracles with bare arms, as a guarantee that the
six large, indignant, live ducks are not concealed
up his sleeves j and he has a large friendly smile,
which makes the audience feel acquainted with
him at once. But, then, all illusionists have that
smile. His assistant, Mdlle. Edmunda, is a grave-
looking young lady, who is burnt in a reticent sort
of way, and she only smiles when she is riding
head-downwards, with her inverted bicycle on top
of her.
The present comic opera season at Her
Majesty’s (Sydney) closes at the end of next week.
The last episode will be a brief revival of the
“Gondoliers.” That double-barrelled king, who
proved not to be loaded after all, will start reign-
ing on Wednesday (19th), and will dry up again
at the usual late hour on Saturday night. After
which the Firm’s dramatic co. will be about due.
** * *
Bland Holt leaves Sydney at the end of
next week, bound southward to the great carnival,
where all Australia is gathered together to see a
horse in a hurry for three minutes. Miss Pattie
Browne, provided she recovers soon enough (Miss
8., at time of writing, was a fair and interesting
invalid in Melbourne), will succeed him at the
Royal for a two weeks’ season, under Rickards’
management, and then Pattie also passes on, and
becomes only a remembrance.
** # *
The present Lyceum programme will last
till Friday, and “My Jack ” will follow on Satur'
day night. The Bulletin has already recoin"
mended the “ Flight for Life,” in a moderate sort
of way, despite the frantic adventures in the in-
terior of Africa, and the recommendation still holds
good in a moderate sort of way. For this sinful
paper is very weary and disgusted over the hero
who, being unjustly suspected of the manufacture
of dead bodies, says that he will stay and clear his
character, and that Providence will demonstrate
his innocence. As it happens, Providence never
does go out of its way to demonstrate anything.
And the hero who runs for his life, and leaves
Providence to telegraph to him when it is quite
done clearing his character)'is a sensible, flesh-and-
blood person whom it is a pleasure to meet. As it
happens, Providence neglects its business so
shamefully that he has to come back in the end
and attend to the clearance himself, but the moral
holds good all the same.
** * *
This paper was a notable prophet when it
foretold, in last week’s issue, that the days of the
dramatic co. at Sydney Criterion would be few
and gone at the knees. The regrettable show has
already pegged out, and no one threw the friendly
old boot of remembrance after it as it left. Miss
Inez Bensusan did a vast amount in a bright and
capable fashion to lift the “ Dangers of London ”
into some kind of respectability ; while Cosgrove
and Johnson Weir and the burglarious father also
gave it a vigorous hoist in places ; but the drama
and the stage management and the utter hope-
lessness of the heroine were three things that
couldn’t be lifted, except by dynamite. As for
the Phonograph, which had a hollow voice like
one weeping bitterly in a bottle, it was a shriek
of burlesque all through. The profession gave
Cosgrove a benefit on Saturday, and then the
Criterion put up its shutters, pending the arrival
of Alfred Dampier and his new co. on the 22nd.
Dampier has come back with a new stock of plays,
and The Bulletin hereby bespeaks him a good
welcome.
*» * *
Friends at a distance, also remote enemies
and other people, will please accept this intimation
that the Tivoli is much as usual. Ada Reeve
leaves next week after a benefit, and Ugo Biondi
takes his departure a few days later. Some other
people will kindly soften the blow by remaining a
while longer, but it is a serious blow none the
less.
** * *
A few weeks ago alleged mesmerist Ken-
nedy stepped quietly on board the San Francisco
mail boat and left his loathsome show behind. The
loathsome show paid very badly in its latter days,
which was probably the reason of the exit. There
appears to have been no formal settling up ; the
candle-eater was unpaid, and the kerosene-drinker
was left lamenting, and the person who catalepted on
two chairs while the big man bounded on his sto-
mach didn’t get his money—at least, they all say so,
and it looks at least possible. These people have
since started explaining, and their explanation is
mainly to the effect that the alleged professor
didn t mesmerise them—that he couldn’t mes-
merise worth a cent; and that they merely ate the
disgusting candle and drank the foul kerosene for
a small salary. The reason they looked happy
when doing it was simply that they dare not
look otherwise for fear of not being paid,
and why they postponed being unspeakably
sick till the show was over was because any
man who dared to be sick on the spot would have
lost his billet. The men who had pins stuck in
them and the other sons of misery allege that they
had to practise the misery in private till they could
look as if they enjoyed it. The Bulletin doesn’t
profess to know whether the candle-eaters and
kerosene-drinkers are telling the whole truth or
not. Its own idea is that the Professor was some-
thing of a mesmerist once, and a very coarse and
brutal one at that, and that the Australians
who crowded his show in his prosperous days
were just as coarse and brutal as himself.
Also _ the Respectable Press, which spoke
of him as rather an amusing entertainment,
was ditto. _ In those days Kennedy certainly did
leave a trail of used-up subjects behind him, and
they were apparently nervous wrecks—not simply
digestive wrecks. Latterly it was commonly re-
ported the Professor’s own nerves began to give
out, and possibly as time went on he became less
and less of a mesmerist, and grew at last to be a
mere hirer of enslaved candle-eaters. Anyhow, it
is a sad reflection that many Australians paid
their money to see their fellow-countrymen publicly
drinking oil for the baggy alien who is gone, and
if they aren’t ashamed of themselves they ought
to be.
*m » *
Rickards has had cruel luck with his
dramatic companies. In Sydney the Cartwright
season was put out of gear by the accident to
Edith Morley. Lesly Thomson, the little Scotch-
man who was imported to realise “The Little
Minister,” couldn’t come up to the scratch, and
Cecil Ward was called upon to play the part in a
different key. It may be said that Ward was a
more effective actor, but, on the other hand, it is
certain that the piece didn’t get a fair chance
to run on the author’s simple Scotch lines. And
last week’s revival of “The Minister” at Melb.
Bijou was upset by Pattie Brown’s illness.
** * *
As a natural consequence of Pattie Browne’s
illness, “The Dove-Cote” didn’t appear on Satur-
day as intended. The new comedy was then an-
nounced to take the stage on Wednesday of this
week if the course of Miss Pattie’s convalescence
ran smooth, but instead that charming lady’s re-
covery bumped dreadfully, and the end of it all
was the Bijou’s sudden closure. If there is
any balm left in Gilead the “ Dove-Cote ” may
yet get a chance to catch on in Sydney.
When Pattie retired from the bill, Miss Noni
Rickards who had shaped promisingly as boy
Micah Dow, took up the part of Babbie and
scored a respectable success. In a general
way, the young understudy gave an intelli-
gent imitation of the Babbie she had seen. Some-
times she introduced new bits of feeling with good
effect, and the performance, considering all things,
was an astounding improvement on the Miss
Rickards, who tried to act ingenue parts when the
Cartwright co. came to Melbourne eight months
back. The closing scene of “The Little Minis-
ter’ as interpreted by Lesly Thomson, Miss R.,
and Warner Gregory, had an air of reality which
was not there when the three characters of Gavin,
Babbie, and Lord Rintoul were in more ex-
perienced hands.
** * *
“ Under the Red Robe ” seems to be pro-
ceeding comfortably at the Princess’s, and Car-
dinal Richelieu, who is a beautiful old gentleman
of about eight and twenty, will continue to outwit
his invisible enemies for another week or two
Kobody can get the hang of the plot which
Richelieu circumvents, but the audience goes home
happy in the belief that His Eminence holds a
trump-card, or something’, up his large red sleeve
and will make things warm for his unknown rivals
after the non-apparent King has granted him an
audience. “ Under the Red Robe ”is full of scope
for the imagination.
** * *
At the Royal, a persevering management
produces “Sons of the Sea ” seven times a week,
the Wednesday matinee being thrown in to ac-
commodate clergymen and young children, per-
haps. Familiarity breeds gentle toleration for
“Sons of the Sea.” It contains, for one thing,
a solemn warning to ship-captains who whop
their crews, inasmuch as the stern, unyielding
skipper gets left on a cold Patagonian coast
owing to his “cat’’-like policy. Unfortunately,
the abandoned tar doesn’t die. If he were to
perish rapidly all would be forgiven, but he lives
on and suffers at great length in the last act until
his relatives come and take him aboard a yacht.
That’s where the drama makes a sad mistake. If
The Bulletin had a voice in the arrangements
the dreary castaway would be buried with musical
honors, and the neatly-appointed ballet would
dance on his place of interment.

m time of weariness, and the Poluskis amount to a
