** * *
On Saturday (15th) the Yic. Operatic
Society will open a bold attack on “ The Bo-
hemian Girl ”in aid of St. Vincent’s Hospital. A
while ago the V.O.S. made poor old “Maritana ”
fairly hum for a week, and the equally ancient
but less weather-beaten “Girl ’ is expected to go
one better than the previous show. It should be
a well-warbled battle, in any case. A double cast
is available for the three leading parts, and the six
voices will work in shifts whilst the basso, and the
second tenor, and a chorus 50 strong turn up every
evening until further notice. Hanstein and
George Leopold are directing the operations, and
business-manager Kennedy states that scenery and
costumes will be quite gorgeous. Also, an ama-
teur ballet will assist the good cause in the first
and second acts ! At this point the writer is
naturally tempted to beat the floor with one hind
leg and make guttural noises expressive of his feel-
ings re the joy in store.

A variety co. has broken out on a fairly
large scale at St. George’s Hall. Likewise, C. j.
Frank has leased the Town Hall for a series of
Saturday “Pops,’’and will unfold an attractive
shilling programme on Saturday night.
** * *
Olga Nethersole has been explaining to a lady
interviewer her theory of stage-dressing. She
“ feels intensely in colors ” and suits her costumes
to the emotions of the character. Red is the
emblem of passionate love, hatred, vengeance, and
anything else of a violent nature. Pink means
tenderness, whereas grey indicates neutral feeling,
or a condition ,of “ hovering on the unknown ex-
panses of life ” with a tendency to sadness. Re-
cently the conscientious Olga was playing a tem-
pestuous wife, and she dressed the part “first in a
flowing gown of tomato-red, ” but when the lady’s
passion had cooled off she put her into yellow,
which reminds one of the naughty anecdote re the
Scotch spinster who decided that the amber satin
lining to her coffin ought to include “a wee bit
lavender ev-ery here and there ” —but no matter.—
We have it from the poet-sage,
The dictum, “ All the world’s a stage,”
And women (bless their hearts !)
Appearing in a round of parts
Should ever dress to suit each r6le,
The same as Olga Nethersole.
A damsel, innocent and young,
Unspoiled as yet by flat'ring tongue,
Whose moods are rather grave than gay,
Must sport a gown of dovey grey.
But when her mood begins to change,
And, stirred by feelings new and strange,
The darling cannot sleep a wink,
She needs a pale, expressive pink,
Which, as she sentimental grows,
Should deepen into blushy rose.
Anon the stern decree of Pate
Compels the girl to love or hate.
The hot blood surges co her head,
Then must she wear tomato red,
Except when passion’s baser hue
Demands a “harmony in blue.”
Love passes, with its joy and pain,
Like fickle moons that wax and wane;
A woman wearies of a fellow
In many pretty shades of yellow.
He travels ; ordered change of scene ;
Grass-widowed then, she flirts in green.
The years roll on, and all her gowns
Are studies in autumnal browns,
Till, left alone to mourn, alack !
We find her looking nice in black.
At Poverty Point.
At the recent Shoalhaven district show was an Italian
hurdy-gurdy man with a monkey. A local milkweed,
taking the turn-out to be some new-fangled kind of game,
dropped a shilling into the plate held out by the monkey.’
The money was placed in a receptacle on the organ.
After a time Hayseed put down another shilling, with
same result; but after this had happened a third time
Hayseed got Giacomo by the scruff, and roared “ What
the (&e.) sort of a game do you call this, where the blanky
monkey wins every time ?’’
“ Thought I had met Actor Titheradge last Sunday,”
writes The Bulletin’s London correspondent, “ but con-
versation realised only a modest gentleman who, coaxed
and invited by the Royal Horticultural Society, is shortly
about to expose the doings of the Daffodils in Australia.
Oh, yes, his name was George, and when not daffodilly-
ing he had simulated a Silver King, an Abb6, two or three
officers, and several ‘ walking gentlemen ' for the enter-
tainment of the public, but his intentions here did not
run at all in that direction. An engagement as the Daffo-
dill King with Regent’s Park as a realm might detain him
in a land where summer days are chilly and celebrated
musicians never slept, but—for a time only.”
The husband of a beauteous actress much admired in
Australia and elsewhere is giving her heaps of trouble
in London. He is on the warpath for divorce evidence,
and his detective-spies haunt the lady’s house and foot-
steps, and bribe the maids and the watch-dog, and dis-
cover—nothing. The lady scorns hubby’s offer of terms
to simply-the unlinking, but takes an interest in what
her servants are making in handsome tips from the enemy
and soars on to the ‘ show ’ of her life.”
A Hobart correspondent to The Bulletin: “I hardly
think your ‘ Poverty Point’ bottle-dealer comes up to our
Hobart cunning-man. Bottles are here sold for Is. 3d.
per dozen, and, on Saturday afternoons, this man goes to
the Domain, where he has his roundabout, and all the
nippers in. the place there assemble and take gallops at a
bottle a ride ; any kind of bottle. At this rate, he makes
about 255. on a fine afternoon. More pay for less trouble
than your man, don’t you think ?"

translation from the German made by Jaffray, for (I
thinkl a Miss Gladstone. I had a small share in it, inas-
much as I fagged “ dictionary ” for Jaffray, at Medi.
Public Library, and stood up manfully to have the lines
read at me, on Sunday afternoons. Jaffray was a well,
educated man, and a right stood fellow. But l don’t
think he could ever have written an original drama of

which “ Ilaresfoot ” jusily places at the “ highest Austra-
lian water-mark.”
Can physiologists tell us what is the connection between
the lungs and the violoncello ? It is a significant-looking
coincidence that the three prominent ’cellists at present
in Sydney are suffering more-or-less from lung-trouble.
Does ’cello-playing affect the lungs, or does a weak chest
cause one to yearn to play the ’cello ?
Maud Jeffries, the beauteous star of the Wilson Barrett
co., “has retired from the stage,” according to latest
announcement. Not even the temporary excuse of mar-
riage has been offered. Mary Anderson and her Aus-
tralian understudy, Essie Jenyns, are the only two well-
known actresses up to date who have retired from the
stage into private life in the days of their youth, and
stayed there, so her admirers naturally cherish hopes of
Maud being drawn back very soon to the footlights. At
present, Lillah M’Carthy takes her place.
Bateman and Maurice, of Melb, Royal, were success-
fully sued for £56 last week by a young man who had
been engaged to play the part of a “calm, cool, phlegma-
tic Englishman,” but didn’t come up to the managerial
conception of the character. There was some uninten-
tional humor in the evidence for the defence. Maurice
(lately a masseur in Melb.) stated that the young man
shaped at rehearsal like a—vat you call an am-a-teur •
whilst Bateman (lately a dentist) also testified to the
plaintiff’s “incompetence.” Bateman, who finds the
cash, and plenty of it, for the Royal enterprises, described
himself as “a theatrical manager at present."
Get thee to London, young man, and write for the
stage. We read that Henry Arthur Jones has expressed
his approval of the lady (Miss Mary Moore) who plays the
heroine in his latest comedy, “ The Liars,” by presenting
her with “a magnificent set of inscribed gold fruit-
dishes.” Playwrights are the only sort of literary men
who scatter gold fruit-dishes around for birthday pre-
“ Stroller”: What passes for dramatic criticism is
mostly literary. The journalist is concerned with ab-
surdities of plot, characterisation, quality of dialogue •
but the actor in front- cares little for these things " He
views the show with another sense. His ear is tuned to a
finer appreciation of intonations, his eye to faults in
“ business,” and his emotions to that nice cultivation of
climaxes on which the interest of spoken dialogue de-
pends. He looks for theatrical consistency, which is
different from literary. It matters not to him whether
Hamlet talks of “ siege ” or “ sea ”of trouble. The vital
point is, does he get Hamlet’s drear, weird weariness into
his voice, face and attitude—either word will do, provided
that emotion be there. Talking with most journalists
about drama is like asking a deaf man’s opinion
of a song. They have the picture sense, the literary
sense and a broader view of life and art than most actors
but they are generally stone-deaf to those fine shades of
speech which at once tell the actor whether the player is
really thinking and feeling the words he pretends he is.

The WILD CAT
COLUMN
Maori land Government proposes to add an
Accident Department to its State Life Insurance office.
It looks a promising idea. Maoriland’s one big accident
co. started in 1879 with £5OOO paid-up capital. It pays
15 per cent, dividends regularly and has accumulated
about £30,000 of reserves. The State, with no 15 per cent,
dividends to pay and a far strop er capital, should lie able
to do the business at 30 per cent, lower premiums and
still make a respectable profit out of it.
• * • *
The Victorian Government starts its new public
works hoom by putting on the Melbourne market a loan
of 1250,000 at 3 per cent. This is the first half of the pro-
posed local loan of £500,000, and if it is a success it is just
possible that the idea of floating the other million in
London will be abandoned. In one notable respect, at
all events, the new loan is a conspicuous piece of mis-
management. It falls due at a fixed date, in 1917, instead
of being redeemable in 1917 or at any time thereafter at
the notion of the Government. Some day or other the
loan which falls due at a fixed date will land Australia in
an incredible hole. For instance, in 1921 and 1923 Vic-
toria has £10,000,000 of 3J per cent, stock to redeem.
Supposing these loans had been floated on the under-
standing that thev could be redeemed then or at any
Irt’-r dote, the difference would he just this: If the
value of money, in 1921-23, is 3J4 per cent, then the old
loan could simply be allowed to go on till further notice,
and something like £200,000 in new flotation expenses
would be saved. If there is a huge convulsion on just
then, and the biggest war on record is blazing in Europe,
and some of the provinces have gone insolvent—well,
Australia’s credit may be back at 7 or 8 per cent., and it
may be necessary to renew these loans at £350,000 or
£400,000 a vear more interest than they now cost; whereas
on the “interminable” plan the 3)4 per cent, loan would
simplv run on till it could be redeemed at a profit, and
both the flotation expenses and the £350,000 a year would
be saved. Such a disaster is perfectly possible ; the
Napoleonic wars brought Britain’s credit down with quite
as bad a crash as this ; and if another such war breaks
out,. Australia may, when its big loans fall due and have
to be met on a fixed date, require to do a fresh borrow at
even more than 7 per cent.—or else default. And
when it has floated its new loan at 5 or 6 or 7 per cent,
and consented to fix it for 10 or 20 years at the dictation
of the lenders, and then peace is re-established and in-
terest goes down to 2J per cent.—well, there will be noth-
ing for this country to do except tear its hair, and pay.
So far the foolishness of the present system
has attracted little notice. One reason is that in-
terest has been falling almost constantly since Australia
started borrowing, and, in almost every case where a loan
fell due, the country wanted to redeem it so as to save in-
terest. Therefore the compulsory redemption was no
hardship. But interest can’t keep failing for ever ; some
day Australia will find itself compelled to renew its bor-
rowings in a market where rates are going up “ some-
thing" terrible,” and then it will begin to understand
where it has got to. Another reason why the matter has
attracted little attention is that only a few loans, com-
paratively speaking, have yet fallen due ; but now there
is a big renewal operation to be done every year till
1920, and almost every year till 1947. Sometime in
that period the fixed-loan idea will probably land Aus-
tralia into a stupendous disaster.
Tasmania also proposes to float a local loan
(£lOO,OOO at 3 per cent.) with a fixed date of repayment.
The troub’e—which must needs ensue some day—is
always 10 or 20 or 30 years ahead when the loan is floated,
and no Australian Government looks that distance into
the future in its financial dealings.
Turner’s other great .mistake, in The Bulle-
tin’s opinion, lies in offering his loan by tender. The
most successful local loans in Australia have been those
of N.S.W., which were simply put on sale at a fixed price
at the Treasury, and traded off as the money came in.
That method reaches the small investor, who doesn’t
understand the mysteries of the tender system. Any old
woman who knows how to buy her own groceries can buy
Government stock on the N.S.W. system, but the tender
method is beyond her comprehension. And, even if she
does understand it, she can’t fill up the tender form
because she has mislaid her spectacles.
* * • *
The Victorian Government hopes that its pen-
sion list has at last reached the maximum, and will
steadily decline, hereafter. The pension curse is a legacy
from the old roaring days of the Duffys and M’Cullochs
and O’Shannassys. It was cheerfully enacted in th at
remote time that every civil servant should be entitled to
a pension from the public revenue. About ’B3 the country
began to waken to a knowledge of the situation, and it
was enacted that no future appointees to the Service
should have pension - rights, but the old pensioners
had still to be worked off, and they are a hard
lot and very slow to die. There are about 2400
actual pensioners and 1900 civil servants appointed
under the old Act and still in harness will fall due for
pensions by and by. Last year the bill was £347,000 —the
largest figure on record for one year since the trouble
began, and six times as much as the pension outlay for
N.S.W., Queensland, S.A., and Tasmania all put together.
The pension bill may be fairly set down as the whole
cause of Victoria’s financial difficulties, and the load must
he carried for at least another 30 years. Turner reckons,
however, that a lot of the very aged burdens must die
soon, and by rigorously keeping every remaining public
servant of the old brigade out of his pension till the last
possible moment he calculates on making a steady and
continuous reduction in the bill.
Some 36 years ago Nathaniel Levi nearly passed
a Bill providing that every Minister who had held office
for five 3'ears in all should have a substantial life-pension,
and this scheme fell through only by being crowded out
at the end of a busy session. If Levi’s Bill had passed, the
accumulated results would have made Victoria sit up.

The Colonial Finance, Mortgage, Investment
and Guarantee Corporation (Sydney) publishes a balance-
sheet which is sickled o’er with the pale cast of deficit.
The loss on the ordinary operations of the year is £6BBO
(net income, £760; outgoings, £7640). _ Office expenses
and rent (£1386) are nearly twice the net income ; interest
on borrowed money (£3323) is over four times the net in-
come ; so the case of the Colonial Finance Corporation
looks only a shade less hopeless than that of the famous
Standard Bank. The balance-sheet boils down to the fol-
lowing propositions:
Liabilities. Assets.
Owing to Share- Visible assets of
holders .. .. £127,912 various kinds .. £64,226
Owing to Deposi- Accumulated
tors and other Losses .. .. 131,198
Creditors .. 67,512
£195,424 | _ £195,424
This represents that the paid-up capital is more than all
lost, and that the saleable assets are quite insufficient to
pay even the depositors and outside creditors. And the
coid breath of suspicion is upon these saleable assets also,
for there is no definite statement that they have been
written down to bed-rock value, and the fact that they
only yield an apparent net revenue of £1 4s. per cent, per
annum raises a doubt if they would fetch anything like
half their alleged value in case of immediate realisation.
The Colonial Finance Corporation reconstructed
In ’94, and agreed to pay off its liabilities by instalments
6pread over seven years wilh interest at the astonishing
rate of six per cent. Since then it has made calls with
energy worthy of a better cause (£72,000 is being called
up now, and the Board hopes to get a good deal of it any-
how), and the deposits are now down to £26,000. In the
process of repayment the bank overdrafts have got up to
£33,000. Apparently by hard ca.i-making the creditors
may be paid in full some day (at least a strong effort is
being made in that direction, though the prospects look
none too good at present) and then the shareholders will
go and die with a clear conscience and with very little else.
They seem to be a rather unusual lot of shareholders and
deserving of better luck. Of late the results of all
their toil have been very limited. Four years ago
the shareholders’ funds were £94,899, and other direct
liabilities, £67,265, so after much toil, and the calling up
of £32,000 capital the liabilities appear to have increased
by over £2OO. The Bulletin thinks a lot more of the
energy of the shareholders who put £32,000 into such a
dismal show than it does of their prospects.

The big Svdney calico firm of \V. and A.
M'Arthilr, of which ex-Treas. M’Millan is a member, is
being floated into an absentee corporation in London.
The M’Arthur concern has been mostly a British absentee
all its life (like a very large proportion of the Sydney
warehouses), so the change makes little difference. The
prospectus sets forth that the annual profits of the con-
cern are £19,326, and the sellers fix the purchase-money
at £300,000 (one-half shares and one-half cash). After
the complicated fashion of the British financier, the co.
starts by issuing £75,000 worth of 5i per cent, preference
shares, and £75,000 U per cent, debentures. The most
interesting* features in the prospectus are that a business
yielding £19,326 is put down as worth £300,000 m all.
From which it would appear that the Calico-Jimmy trade
is only expected to yield 6J per cent, per annum nowadays.

The Vic. Mining Accident Relief Fund (nee
the Creswick Disaster Relief Fund) is another grisly
spectre of Smellbourne’s land-boomed past. In 1888 the
treasurer of the Fund had £20,000 to deal with, and the in-
evitable manner of dealing with coin at that time was to
use it directly or indirectly for the inflation of land-
values. The treasurer was the Smellbournian Mayor, old
friend Bingey-Benjy, and to that woolly-headed potentate
Jacobs, Hart and Co. offered a “ desirable investment” in
the form of a mortgage on their new, uncompleted ware-
house in Queen-street. Cornelius Job Ham, M.L.G., ran
his boomsome eye over the property and valued it at
£26,000 — i.e., £i6,600 for the land and £lO,OOO for the
warehouse when finished as per contract, not reckoning
another £2OOO for the proposed fittings thereof. On the
Ham valuation the £20,000 of trust moneys were
advanced. Things seem to have gone wrong with mor-
tao-or Jacobs. Anyhow, the trustees of the M.A.R.F. are
disposed to release him from the mortgage and take over
the property which is now estimated to be worth £9OOO.
A Parliamentary select committee Is enquiring into this
little matter of the £lO,OOO warehouse erected on the
£16,600 block of land, and the mining world will be glad
to know how the difference between Ham’s valuation and
the present estimate.is accounted for. Also, it will be
glad to know why £20,000 was loaned when apparently
onlv £16,000 was asked for.

* * * *
Late O. H. James, of Melbourne, was probably
an unparalleled bankrupt. For years the straight-bearded
ex-grocer and boomster wj-ls understood to be di awing a
comfortable income from financial bustedness. He re-
mained outwardly solvent owing to his liabilities being so
big that “ the banks ” were afraid to wind him up. It
seemed better to pay him a salary to manage his own
private insolvency and wind himself up. What an ex-
ample for the younger generation of Victorians was the
late C. H. James ! After living in great style on the ruin
of his land-boom dupes he proceeded to live on the ruin
of C.H.J. For the man who doesn’t borrow from other
people, but from the custodians of other people’s cash,
there is no easier profession than insolvency provided that
he borrows on a sufficiently large scale.
Mclb. Arbus says of C. H. James: “In the
height of the land-bcom, had it been possible for him to
have realised his assets, and had he so desired, he would
have been a millionaire.” Rats ! Had James, or Mat-
thew Davies, or Fink tried to turn his vast paper posses-
sions into money at any period of the boom—morning,
noon, or night—the bubble would have burst im-
mediately. The boomsters never quite knew what they
were doino-, hut they knew they were bound to keep on
doing it. The only gambler to get away with any con-
siderable profit was G. W. Taylor, who sneaked off Ome
under cover of a promise to give £50,000 to the Baptist
Church. His example perhaps tended to prove that one
moderate-sized boomer could realise and get out without
wrecking the show. But if two of them, or one big
boomer, had put their or his load of paper on the market
it would have burst. The writer personally held up a
small boom once, and speaks feelingly. He owned 2800
shares in a 5000 share mine ; the rest were held in small
lots. The shares were worth 14s. He started to unload
as gently as the soft-footed Indian after a scalp, and
before he was through the price was down to 2s.

There is a N.S.W. lady—receiving £1 a week
from her legally-separated husband—for whom the Crown
has been graciously acting as receiving and paying agent
for the past three years. She has a standing account in
the G.P.O. Savings Bank; amount to her credit, Is.
She leaves her pass-book at certain place on hubby’s pay-
day, and he banks £1 to her credit and return? book to
where he got it. Following Monday, wife calls for book,
gives notice, and draws the £1 hanked on previous
Friday. And so on, every week, till the book has been
filled with deposit and withdrawal entries, and a good-
natured State has given her a new one free. Yet, if a
bona-fide depositor loses his book, he is charged Is. for a
new one. Interesting to calculate loss of time to receiv-
ing-clerk, teller and ledger-keeper, and cost of stationery,
due to this domestic situation.

this issue) proposes to take over four blocks of 50 acres
each in the North Pieman district, 26 miles from Mount
Bischoff and 22 from Zeehan (Tas.). The Emu Bay rail-
way will, before the end of the year, run close to the pro-
perty, which has been well cleared and tracked.
Exploration work to date shows big possibilities, and the
vendors’ terms are satisfactory enough. 1200 shares to
be retained in full payment for the property, 1200 are
offered to the public ; £2 10s. on application, £2 10s. on
allotment. No further liability. The £5OOO subscription
goes to the credit of the co., the vendors paj'ing all pre-
liminary expenses.

• * * *
The new battery of 40 stamps lately started at
the Galleymont gold-mines is one of the finest gold mill-
plants at work in N.S.W., and is well worth inspection by
parties contemplating erection of similar machinery. The
ore is fed automatically through rockbreakers and feeders
without shovelling, and the whole is most substantially
built. The engines are a fine pair of horizontal compound
type, driving the battery with ropes. Mr. M. Eissler,
the managing engineer, speaks in the highest terms of
the construction and design of the plant, which was built
by the Austral Otis Engineering Company, of South
Melbourne.*

“Shellback”: In Bulletin, 20/8/’9B, it is
stated that people journeying to Klondyke should go per
Chilcoot Pass. But it was on that pass that the great
snow-slide destroj ed so many lives, and a friend of mine,
who has got through to the diggings, writes that the best
way is per White Pass, and that a better way still is not
to come at all. He and his mate packed their loads on a
bullock to Taggish Lake, where they killed him and sold
him for Is. per lb.—£42 10s. Further, says he has heard
more fuss on Klondyke over a Joz. nugget than would be
made in Australia about a 50oz. slug.
Snuffle and Life Assurance.
Someone leaves at this office monthly a paper
called the Bible Echo, published in Melbourne,
and sold for the good of the poor, or heathen,
or something. The Bulletin does not know
whether the heathen derive much benefit from
the monthly organ, but it has certainly derived
many pen’orths of amusement from the Bible
Echo. This sage sheet devotes almost the whole
of one issue (Sep. 5) to a violent condemnation
of the theory and practice of life-assurance.
Extracts :
Life insurance leaves God out of the reckoning. It
leads men to trust in riches instead of in God ; to lay up
treasures on earth instead of in heaven ; to provide for a
contingency which may not occur —the death of the per-
son insured before the death of the beneficiaries —and for
those who may never be in need at all, to the neglect of
those who are now in actual want. The whole philosophy
of life insurance, therefore, is contrary to the teachings
of Christ, and to the principles of Christianity. . . .
Considered only from a worldly standpoint, life insurance
is a poor speculation on the part of policy-holders. On
the whole, they do not get out of it as much as they put
into it, the loss of the use of the money, of course, in-
cluded ; for if they did, life insurance societies would soon
cease to exist. . . . Suppose a young man . . . should
insure his life for, say, £lOOO, and should soon afterwards
die. Who would pay the £1000? The young man, him-
self had paid into the insurance company, in premiums,
only a few pounds. Who would make up the rest / Evi-
dently the very men who advised him to insure his life
so that they would not have to care for his family when
he died. The only difference from a monetary stand-
point would be, that instead of doing it in a Christian
and truly philanthropic way, they would do it by having
paid their money into the insurance company from
purely selfish motives —with a view simply of caring for
their own.
Now, what ought to be done with people who
write-such stuff as this? Truly the civilised
parson and the aboriginal medicine-man are
twin brethren.
“ Artificial” Fatherhood.
“ A Democratic lawyer ” to The Bulletin :
The late Vic. maintenance case, with which Constable
White was prominently associated, tends to make one
scratch one’s head and agree with Bumble. By the com-
bined operation of the Marriage Act and the Neglected
Children’s Act, White is adjudicated to be not the
natural but the “ artificial ” (to use the quaint language
of one of the Full Court Judges) father of a certain waif
(he having:, in the opinion of the justices, recognised the
child as his own by writing letters in which he referred
to “the youngster,” and by forwarding money to the
mother). As the natural father of the child, he would
have had to pay (sav) 7s. 6d. per week to its
mother for its keep; as the “artificial” father he
has to pay (say) 7s. 6d. to the Neglected Child-
ren’s Department for its keep. All which may
be good law, but to the ordinary person it looks sus-
piciously like trying a man twice for an offence of which
he has been by a competent court adjudged innocent.
And the more one looks into tbe case the more unsatis-
factory it seems. The final decision appears to turn on a
hair-splitting distinction between “parent” in one Act
of Parliament and “ parent "in another Act. The dis-
tinction is such a fine one that one feels bold enough to
surmise that it would never have been thought of at all
by the Judges had they not been confronted with the
alternative of deciding that a decision of justices in a
maintenance case was binding against the world unless
appealed against in due time. The possibility of having
to arrive at such a conclusion was too much for the Vic.
Judges, to whom the versatility of Vic. J.P.-dom is only
too well-known. They avoided the possibility by doing a
little comic opera on their own account, and by
enriching legal terminology with the “ artificial father.”
Perhaps the consequences of the decision, except to Con-
stable White, will not be nearly as serious as if, following
the old legal doctrine that “decisions in questions of
status are good against the world,” they had given a
dangerous finality to magi/iterial adjudications of pater-
nity. The decision has other lessons. It calls pointed
attention to the dangerous propensity of latter-day legis-
lators to allow elastic definitions to secrete themselves in
statutes dealing with private law, without adequate or
any discussion. It will come as a surprise to many people
who have adopted children out of kindness of heart when
times were good, to find that they are the “artificial”
parents of such children, and will be bound to support
them as though they were their own if, through change
of circumstances, they have been compelled to hand them
back to the State. And very many people will get *
startling shock some day when they find that they are
compelled to support for long years lunatic relatives to
whom they owe little or no duty, on account of a sweep-
ing and practically undiscussed definition in the Vic.
Lunacy Act.
A Scotch member of the N.S.W. Legisla-
ture, recently touring South, reached a hotel a
little ahead of the reporters. It was bitterly cold,
and, when they arrived, he met them with:
“ Well, boys, you’re wet and cold. They have
some splendid rum here—take my advice, and
have some, hot.” The point of this story is that
the statesman never offered to pay for that rum.
The politician who invites the press to drink at
its own expense is Doomed.
Certain vulgar-iiiinded Radicals have made
disloyal comments on the little matter of Private
Purvis and our Gracious Queen. Purvis, it seems,
did something very heroic for Hingland in an
engagement with the Dervishes, wherefore the
Queen commanded him to call upon her, at
Osborne, to receive a decoration from her own
fair hands. When he arrived at Osborne, with
fourpence or so in his pocket, he found nobody to
receive him, and the night was dark, and he didn’t
know his way about, and he wouldn’t have cared
to go and knock the Queen up at that late how,
anyhow. So he sat in the park all night, in the
soaking rain, and felt truly sorry that the Queen
had taken such an interest in him.

idea of a N.S.W. bush State-school teacher at-
tempting to instruct four classes at once. But
Westralia goes one better, and insists on a single
teacher splitting his school of from 20 to 30 pupils
into eight and nine classes. Writer has painful
recollections of attempting to teach under this
difficulty, and of the repeated snubbings he got

At recent Melb. Christian Endeavor out-
break a sainted bootmaker gravely related that
when, in the course of his business, he met with
an obdurate piece of sole-leather, he always asked
the Almighty to intercede with the recalcitrant
bullock-hide, with the result that it at once
became amenable to manipulation. He quite
omitted to state why he didn’t ask Heaven to
make the boots for him right out, and be done
with it!

The “ new-chum ” Jap. isn’t, as a rule, a
hard drinker, but at Thursday Island the habit is
growing', Ins favorite swill being “ long colonials.”
Ihe httle man “ passes ” English because he gets
less for his 6d. Further south, the coast climate
being more humid and thirst-provoking than
the insular his diffidence in the presence of
ardent liquor disappears with great sudden-
?i5 SS -«r, ihe c,r ' n^s ’ n gangs, often on
the trerman tambaroora” system—sixpence
in. Common in Townsville on Saturday even-
ings to see about a dozen neatly-dressed Japs,
swarm round the cedar and polish off, with
intense solemnity, about seven stiff gins or
whiskies as fast as their little innards will accom-
modate the stuff. Then the party purchases
several square gin-bottles of “colonial” for the
lady Japs.—and the procession leaves with as
much order and decorum as if the fluid had been
ginger-beer.
It was edifying to hear a certain chief of
police curse a political adversary t’other day
within the sacred precincts of Parliament House.
Though ’tis a duty of this gilded official to see
that the casual boozer, the user of obscene lan-
guage, the breach-of-the-peace man, is consigned
to durance vile without delay, Const. O’Pafferty
was not in evidence on this occasion.
* » * *
Naval officers seem to be unlucky up North.
A while back, when a lieutenant of H.M.S.
Mohawk, at anchorage near Cairns, was hurrying
(in plain clothes) to the wharf to catch last ship’s
boat, two policemen grabbed him and ran him in.
A burglary had been committed in Cairns that
mght, and the lieutenant’s hurry was suspicious.
His declaration that he was a naval officer was
received with, “We’ve heard that before, voung
man; come along.” Also, a few days ago, at
Cooktown, when an officer from H.M.S. Gold-
finch was en route to a ball, the local fire-brigade
happened to be practising, and one bold brigadier
turned his hose on the navy man, and drenched
mm from head to foot. The only excuse the fire-
man made was that he had taken the officer for a
local shipping-agent, to whom he had long meant
to give a ducking.” Cooktown seems a nice
place -to be shipping-agent in.
* * * *
Volunteers for the new Scotch regiment
in Melbourne must be sft. 6in. high and measure
33m. round the chest. Not sft. 6in. round the
chest and 33m. round the calf, as was generally
supposed. A sad reflection this upon the present
degeneration of braw Scots.
* * * *
Sydney artists and pressmen are going
ahead with their idea of a Bohemian Club, and
the future is to be discussed at a dinner this week.
Years ago most of the pressmen were driven out
of their only resort by a big influx of auctioneers
and other Philistines, and the new club will be
founded upon a strong determination to keep the
aliens out of Bohemia.

The other day there was a conference
between the Tasmanian Council and Assembly
and business was suspended in both Houses while
it was on. In the Assembly, auctioneer ITartnoll,
M.. 1., of .Launceston, got into the chair and con-
ducted a free-and-easy, to the delight of the press-
gallery and the few strangers present. He gave a
dramatic recitation himself— addressed to Treas
Fysh who looked “as if he couldn’t help it’’—
and then called on Orosby Grilmore, who 6 6 favored
with another recitation.”' Hartnoll himself gave a
song and called on the clerk of the House for a
Scotch reel, but that well-behaved official cour-
teously declined. Then enormous sums of money,
as compensation and so forth, were passed to
members present—Reibey getting half-a-million or
so for some horses distrained on for rates ’’—and
the show ended with Hartnell's appointment as
third judge of the Supreme Court.

* * * *
Why not make the medical drug-trade a
Govt, monopoly, so that the dispenser shall have
no interest in the profits ? By this means, with
supervision of an expert - inspector, standard
strength and purity could be assured. In Eng-
land, out of a large number of bottles of medi-
cine recently analysed, 23 per cent, would have
failed of the effect intended by the prescriber.
And if this is the state of things in such a densely
populated country, how much more rampant must
the evil be in sparsely-settled and warmer Aus-
tralia ?

Premier Kingston’s enemies accuse him of
being under the heel of the W.C.T.U., and even
riends fear that he is rapidly becoming pious.
His Govt, recently introduced a Bill raising the
age of consent to 17, and also prohibiting children
under the age of 13 years from being in a public
place after 8 p.m.—a peculiar piece of restriction,
considering the oppressive conditions prevailing in
summer the measure (prompted by the State
childrens council, a “mixed” body) stands no
chance of passing.

Some 20 third-grade Vic. State school-
teachers have charge of second and first-class
schools, without higher pay. The Turner Govt.’s
retrenchment game—move a man into a superior,
and what should be a higher-paid, position, call
him “acting ’’ so-and-so, and keep him at the sub-
ordinate rate of pay.
How do these things get in the papers %
Here is the Illustrated London News, reporting
Col. Hutton’s appointment to the command of
the Canadian military forces, saying that the
colonel “has been recently employed in command
of the military forces of N.S.W., where lie was
instrumental in bringing forward the question of
the federation of Australia.” “ Instrumental ”is
good. One suspects that the handwriting of the
“copy” of that paragraph would be at once recog-
nised, say, by G. Republican Dibbs.
Canon Godby lately quoted the woman
Jukes to the Neglected Children’s Society in Mel-
bourne. Jukes was an American criminal whose
descendants, to the number of 800, have figured
on the crime records of N. Y. State. Mr. Godby
moralised that if Jukes had been caught young
by a Neglected Children’s Society all that crime
might have been prevented. Unfortunately, Jtikes
was “saved” by such an organisation, and the
case is quoted by Herbert Spencer as an illustra-
tion to prove that kindness to the individual may
prove seriously harmful to the race. It must be
painful for the Canon to have the moral thus
knocked out of Jukes, who has been a stock illus-
tration and chestnut for many years.
THE BEST IS THE CHEAPEST.
Drink the best only, no matter how expensive.
“D.C.L.” Whisky is the best product of Scotland
and bottled in Scotland under the supervision of
Her Majesty’s excise officers.*
The Australian Mutual Provident Society
intimates in another colomn that with the close of
the present year the Society will terminate its
first half century of existence. Starting in the
year 1849 on the mutual principle without capital
or other recommendation, it is now, at the ter-
mination of nearly 50 years, the largest monetary
institution in the colonies. The invested funds
reach the enormous total of .£14,479,578 while the
annual income amounts to .£2,094,706. The cash
surplus divided among the members during the 49
years ending 1897 aggregate £7,726,170 while
the cash bonus distributed for the single
year 1897 amounted to =€445,235. This sum,
we understand, is- a larger amount of profit
than has ever been divided for a single year
by any other life-office in the British Empire.
The next division of the profits will be made as at
31st December next, when all policies issued prior
to that date will share in the bonus to be then dis-
tributed amongst the participating policyholders.
In order that the new business of the Society for
this year may be a record one in the history of the
institution, the directors of the Society invite the
assistance of the members to bring about the de-
sired result by taking out additional policies them-
selves and by inducing their friends who are not
already assured to become members.*

* * * *
That unbelieving old man, the late Coroner
Foul, must have turned in his grave the other
day. His successor, Coroner Candler, sitting on
the drowned body of a Melb. mining-agent,
directed the jury to find deceased of unsound
mind because, in a farewell letter to his family, he
had said—“l dare not ask forgiveness from God,
if there is a God.” Mr. Candler appears to think
that doubt as to the existence of a personal
Almighty is certain evidence of brain-disease in
the doubter. What a change from the humorous
agnosticism of the departed Youl, who would
have argued that the poor fellow’s suicide proved
his insanity at the time, in spite of his intelligent
unbelief! Youl held that this life is the only
thing worth living for.

maids were kept up till 3 a.m. “ Ah,” retorted
Glynn, “I have never been up till then.” Then
the House laughed.

Archbishop Carr lately told a Total
Abstinence Society at Armagh that, during his
12 years residence in Melbourne, he has “never
seen a drunken man or woman.” Which is not so
astounding, after all. The Bulletin has never
heard of a drunken man or woman running against
an Archbishop. Even the average sober citizen
might move about Melb. for a dozen years with-
out crossing a high church dignitary’s line of
vision. These holy personages can’t be said to
live in the common or worldly sense of the word ;
they divide their time between prayer and respect-
ability, doing their duty in that sphere of exist-
ence where nobody turns up under the influence
of liquor.
» * * *
Adelaide’s biggest draper threatens, if the
Early Closing Bill is passed, to stop the three
weeks’ holiday now annually given to employes,
and also to make the assistants pay for the days
on which they are absent through illness. The
Bill, which is through the Assembly, provides that
shops shall shut at 6 p.m. on four days, 9 p.m. on
Fridays, and 1 p.m. on Saturdays.
Why should Sydney D.T’s London corres-
pondent rage so furiously in the chestnut-grove ?
Saturday week’s London notes gave to a wonder-
ing Sydney world a fine old yarn about how a
Miss Balfour took a bath at a Johannesburg hotel,
and was asked not to use soap, else the cook would
be short of water for the soup. And then, un-
satisfied with the re-hash, the D.T. man lets it
loose again as having happened to a Miss Bond at
Okayama, Japan. Which, considering the water-
supply and bathing facilities at that pleasant spot,
is tommyrot. And, moreover, the yarn was told
25 years ago in Queensland of old Judge “Pat-
o’-butter” Blake, when that eccentric judicial
luminary was on circuit in the arid west of Leper-
land.
* • * *
On Friday Island Q. keeps 13 lepers,
nearly all South Sea Islanders. These poor devils
seem to have no protector or care-taker ; they wait
on one another; cook for one another; when
rendered incapable by the progress of disease they
are entirely dependent on the kindness of their
fellow sufferers. Apparently they receive no regu-
lar medical attendance —the health officer of
Thursday Island pays them a visit occasionally.
Above interesting and scandalous facts are gleaned
from a report of Dr. Tilston, medical officer afore-
said.

According to an old Act of Charles ll.
who should surely be an authority on the subject—
damsels who may have had a misfortune should
have no difficulty in getting maintenance for their
offspring. Lawyer Eagleson unearthed and quoted
the musty old law in a recent Melb. appeal case.
Said enactment provides that, “if one be ordered
to keep an illegitimate child, and this, upon
appeal to the sessions, is revoked, that person is
absolutely discharged, and, unless a father can be
found, the court said that the justices of the peace
must keep it themselves.” They evidently kuew
their J’sP. well in those old days ; but, were the
Act considered binding to-day, there would be a
great hiatus in the list of honorarium magistrates
—especially at North Melbourne. The statute

“ H.” This incredible-looking narration,
is vouched for in two books, the just-published
“Reminiscences of Mrs. Simpson,” daughter of
Senior, the economist, and Hare’s “Story of My
Life.” A servant-girl went to India, and married

* * * *
“A Maoriland Girl”—solemn fact! to
The Bulletin :
Compulsory monogamy is the most absurd institution
that has never been advocated by Christianity. How
everyone contrives to associate it with Christianity is a
mystery. Polygamy was rife in early Christian days, yet
there isn’t a word in the New Testament condemning it.
The injunction that the bishop should be the husband ot
one wife seems to infer that other men might have more.
At the most, monogamy is hinted at as being the more
ideal union; but that may be classed with bt. Paul s
“ better remain single.” The mind-tendency of this age
is towards a limited and respectable polygamy. A cer-
tain old writer quaintly remarks : “ After all, a woman
means very little in a man’s life ; she is only a side-issue,
as it were, like Eve.” Some day women also will dis-
cover that men are not the pivot round which their whole
lives turn ; at the same time the desire for a certain
amount of husband will increase. There is a good deal
of nonsense talked about the “ companionship between
man and wife. The Orientals are wiser. Providence
never meant men and women for ; companionship ; it
meant them for marriage. For friendship, your own
sex. They'll do more for you in the long run. The
boasted soul-union of husband and wife —it occurs in one
of a hundred marriages—would be just the same, or
more, if he were Bill and she were Jim. Married people
are “ chummy” in spite of their sex, not because of it ,
and, after all, it is just as well to keep your “ chummi-
ness ” for another woman ; less bother ; no misconcep-
tions.

A Bulletin correspondent:
Given that Brown has proposed to Miss X. and been
accepted by her, what word should I use to describe
Miss X. in her relation to Brown? “The girl Brown is
engaged to ” is the commonest phrase, a very clumsy one.
Shall I say “ fiancee ? ” Not if an English word can be
found. “Bride-elect,” then? It reminds one of the
Mikado’s “daughter-in-law elect.” “ Affianced ” and
“ betrothed” belong to the language of romance. Shall
I, wearing an apologetic grin, say “ Brown’s ladylove,
or his “ well-beloved ” ? It is correct to say that Brown
is her lover, but can Miss X. be called “ Brown’s love .
Must I, alas, call her his “best girl?” If the parties
concerned were rustics, the good old word “ sweethearts
might be employed. On a certain social level we find
“ Brown’s young lady,” and just below is “ Brown’s young-
woman,” who marries him and becomes ipso facto his “old
woman.” In “push society” such terms as “donah
and “tart” might meet the case. Bub an ordinary
middle-class engaged girl is in the curious position of
being indescribable in any satisfactory way.

“P.A.” asks a question and makes rude
remarks :
Are editors, as a rule, henpecked ? It isn’t to their dis-
credit if they are, since bookmakers, footballers, pugi-
lists, actors, musicians, and followers of other pursuits
not requiring brains, frequently bully and sometimes
bash the “ missus.” The man is more manly in submit-
ting meekly to the virago’s tongue than he would be in
“ stoushing ” her. All honor, then, to the henpecked
brigade !—the “mute, inglorious heroes” of the’appy
’oine ! The average woman has no respect for brains.
Milton or Shakespeare, toiling under the lamp for death-
less fame, would stand no chance with the posturing
mummer or the shallow-pated swashbuckler. The AAV.
most respects the man of loud words and hard knocks.
She Worships Power and Noise. Outside the London
Pavilion last year the gaudy poster of Sandow, meaty
body and legs, was continually surrounded by crowds of
women. In their turn Buffalo Bill and John L. Sullivan
were the idols of London society women, and even Capt.
•Duncan Boss had his conquests in the “ highest circles.”
A brain-worker makes no noise, wears no livery, doesn’t
carry a drum or a spear —hence the A.W. has no use for
him except to marry him and live at his expense. Her
hero is the aggressive idiot who repays her worship with
• “stoush.” lie gets the champagne and oysters, while
hubby must put up with the thick cup and the cold pie.

“Hermes”: Be “The Breaker” and his
caramel nurses. What always breaks me up about
a nurse is her feet. The hospital-uniform does not
display to advantage even a pretty ankle and foot;
and when one watches the average nurse splash
down the ward, felt-shod, it kills all the romance
of her face and figure.

“ Q.” : Be temperance reform. Met a
prisoner in Pentridge, who solemnly assured me
that he deliberately committed a crime because he
found himself drifting into the drink-habit, and
recognised that a spell in gaol was the only effec-
tive cure for him. He simply used Pentridge as
an inebriate asylum.
IN SHADOWLAND.
The breezes come, the breezes blow,
In Shadowland.
A peopled past flits to and fro,
The voices of “ Lang Syne ” speak low,
And, mingling with the phantom band,
I clasp once more a snow-white hand,
I see a face of long ago,
In Shadowland.
But shadows pale and pass at dawn
To Shadowland,
When footsteps of a dainty morn
Tread softly up the eastern bourn ;
And so once more from out my hand
The phantom slips, the phantom band
By day-dawn’s light is gently borne
To Shadowland.
CARINI.

“ Where are you going to, my pretty maid ?”
(Wide blue eyes and a wisp of hair)—
“ Up to the Mount for a change of air ?
Down to the sea and the gold-sands’ glare V ”
(Drooping lids and a tinge of pink)—
“Now, where should I travel to? Can’t you think?”
“ Haven’t a notion, O bush-bred maid ! ”
“ I’m goin’ to Sydney, Jim,” she said.
“ Where are you going to, tny pretty maid ? ”
(Half-closed eyes and a burning cheek)
Bust of woman and limbs of a Greek —
Tongue that was glib—not afraid to speak?
(Glare of the arc, and the thick, black night).
What has become of the glances bright!
Where is the laugh of my myall maid ?
“ I’m goin’ to blazes. Cornin’ ? ” she said.

Victor Daley writes “ I have been
glancing through an advance copy of Ogilvie’s
volume, “Fair Girls and Gray Horses.” In my
opinion it will be the most charming book of Aus-
tralian verse that has yet appeared. For Ogilvie
can not only write ringing rhymes that make the
blood spin through the veins ; he can also sing
dainty and delicate songs which remind me at
times of the cavalier songs written between a
battle and a banquet. There is much of the
cavalier and, as becomes his name, not a little of
the moss-trooper in Ogilvie. He writes with a
galloping heart. But, above all, there is the
passionate bloom of youth in his verses. He looks
out upon the world around him and finds it fair
and young, and makes it so for his readers by the
magic of his rhyme. Sadness is not his metier.
If I were asked to name the source of his literary
inspiration I would say—the old Scottish ballads.
He is essentially lyrical in his manner. His songs
need no musical setting. They provide their own
music.”
After all, isn’t truth (Sydney Truth)
far stranger than fiction ? Weren’t the proceedings
in the Sydney eowhide-and-pistol journalist-politi-
cian (Norton and Meagher) vendetta a lot more
lurid and almost as funny as any mythical occur-
rence ever set out in the columns of the Arizona
Kicker? And, after all, what could the presiding
magistrate do except what he ultimately did—
i.e., clear the court ? He couldn’t punish the
eloquent pair by adjourning the hearing and
refusing* them bail, because Meagher wasn’t “be-
fore the Court”—Norton only being then accused.
But perhaps he might have had them charged
W1 * 1 i insulting behaviour.” Something will
surely have to be done to prevent such outrageous
jamborees and the magistrate who does that some-
thing may depend on getting the sympathy of the
Supreme Court, which as an institution has one
conspicuous merit—that of making every body who
enters its precincts behave himself to some extent
while he is there.
The World s Racing Carnival—Melbourne Cup
season.—Visitors to Melbourne during the Cup Carnival
who desire to seek originality in dress—a weakness, by
the way, with all ladies—would be wisely directed to the
establishment of Messrs Stainer & Co.,'of Swanston-st.,
in that city. This firm is, of course, widely recognised'
? 8 V le .-der par excellence tor freshness and novelty
in fashion, and there is distinctive “ chicness” and
smartness about all its productions that rank them
easily first as vendors of high-class novelties. For this
Cup season they have excelled themselves in the simple
beauty of their millinery, the richness and uniqueness of
their mantles, costumes, and blouses ; the delicate and
varied novelties in their lace jabots, scarves, and blouse
fronts ; the combination of taste and elaborateness in
their Cup parasols ; and the delightful tints in their
French gloves. Most pleasing of all, the prices through-
out are well within the range of moderation.*
The Bulletin has undertaken to act as
special agent in Australasia for San Francisco
Argonaut, the most readable of all American
newspapers— the paper which for years and years
has been scissored right and left “by certain sec-
tions of the Australian press without acknowledg-
ment. The man or woman who once reads the
Argonaut never leaves it off. Single copies of
the Argonaut may be obtained at The Bulletin
office after arrival of the American mail, price
(id. ; posted, 7d. The paper will be mailed to any
Australasian address for per annum and
4s. 4d. postage.

~ A ,y. * he . gnarly old sceptics’ theoretical bogies and
the little indisputable facts of tortures and murders
&c., cannot allay the Australian maidens’ fever for
Chinese mission fields. Ten more have just had a
send-off meeting in a sister province, and funds
come in without asking. Forty Australian girls
are there already, and between times, when Ah
Goo and Kong Ki are having a rest from “Who
was the first man ? ” these forty virgins write long
and highly-colored letters to their friends, telling
how warmly the real lady Chows receive them, and
what a fuss is made over them in upper Chow
circles, and how dearly beloved they are by the
lower classes, and so on. One lady missionary
wrote to her mother lately: “Our will is law.
W e have only to look at a thing to get it, and the
women pay us just as many compliments as the
men. We could go to parties every day in the
rp 6 fi ’ aIK / are * n g rea t request in society here.”
talk of Rougemont! As long as women have
the chance of sending long, unlimited epistles
from a strange land, other imaginations may just
give up the struggle.
The latest method of wiling summer hours away
in mountain boarding places has its points. There
must be statues in the garden to begin with:
then the summer boarders dress them up in imita-
tion of real live men with coats, hats, sticks and
even cigars. At a holiday resort called Sylvania,
Bacchus was converted into a very presentable
leading Georgie Porgie, but all the ingenuity of
the fair workers couldn’t make a statue look very
like George Reid.
People rolled up to Gaddy Hallewell’s concert
who never hear any music now but the hurdy-
gurdy at the office door, and the eldest daughter
carolling “Good-bye for ever” in the front draw-
ing room. Half the house had taken a quarter or
80 of do-ra-me’s from Gaddy at one period or
other of its life, and a family beam spread itself
over the audience when those famous toes
trotted on to the platform. Gaddy had an
untimely cold, but was as broad in his effects
as though his 17 years in Sydney were but a
day. Simon the Cellarer and Hallewell have
been to so many concerts together that it’s
a trouble sometimes to disentangle them, and
Saturday’s concert showed that they still shared
that genuine fat laugh between them, even if
Gaddy doesn’t double up as easily over his ha-ha-
ha’s as of yore. It was good, too, to hear the big
voice rolling out its “ Nazareth ” once again. The
rest of the programme included a pretty girl, in
white satin, who, in response to an encore for her
warble about the Past, bade “ Goodbye to Hope ”
with the usual fortitude of young things in white
satin. Arthur Geane brought along the best
voice he has exhibited since his return, and sang,
as he always sings, with a sort of overblown mean-
ing. and manner, but with fine vocal effect. Miss
Edith King’s big, brilliant organ, that never gets
any warmer, yet always suggests possibilities,
showed off m the only operatic selection on the
programme, and the Pleyel quartette made some
fine Schubert music—high up over the people’s
heads.
A large party from Govt. House sat out the
programme quietly, but Hon. Captain Brand, in
white revers, betrayed that his training in local
concerts hasn’t been carried to his father’s fine
pitch of excellence. He moved about restlessly,
and changed his seat often, and one time there
was a whisper, which I wouldn’t like to corrobo-
rate, that he had gone right out of the house to
see a dog. The man m the street who says
potentates don’t work for their money should
just follow the local vice-regal set round one
season’s concert-going. After that, I believe even
the man-in-the-street would own he didn’t know
what work was rather than go over that search
for proofs again. Think of it! Think of sitting
in the front seat year after year, and coming early
and staying late, and listening to every suburban
vocalist, fiddler, pianist, and smilinr/1 For Govt.
House quite understands that all the rest counts
as nothing without the smile.
The great fresh air and strawberry party of the
week brought nearly 4000 to Govt. House gardens.
Of course with 4000 people you don’t expect every
one to take either fresh air or strawberries in the
same way. A marked estrangement with ventila-
tion showed itself in many people’s faces ; and, as
for strawberries, I saw one large lady lift the
whole dish from the table, and set to work with
her fingers to demolish ’em, not even turning her
back on respectable eitizenesses who fed on the
orthodox number of berries on the orthodox plate.
The frocking was more varied than any seen in
Sydney for a long time. I noticed several ladies
carrying little brown bags on their arms a la tea
meeting. But the most exquisite toilet was that
of a little brown man in a long brown coat to the
heels of pale brown boots, with brown rnoustachioes
running well out on either side, then curled
into two perfect Q’s. Back view showed a brown
O suspended from each end of a skewer, as it were.
A little actress in garden green and a white flop hat
Was voted disappointing without her make-up.
Ope tires a,t always chronicling the frocks of the
best-dressed woman in Sydney, but this time her
gown was a repetition of race-week, so I leave it
alone. Lady Hampden with that delicate, mater-
nal smile that lifts her up alongside of popular
Lady Carington, greeted her four thousand guests
m black grenadine cooled down with a white silk
lining. The charge of the four thousand through
the narrow gate, that let them in to the front
garden and the vice-regal recognition, would be a
better soporific than the good old sheep-getting-
through-the-rail dodge. “Such a gittin’ through
the gate” cried out for Thackeray to come and use
it up.
Foster-mothers and stolen heirs sound like a
relict of Udolpho days, when a novel without
either or each of these two romantic commodities
was an impossible undertaking. Then they died
out, except in penny fiction, where everything that
dies out of other literature flourishes as greenly as
if there were no such tense as past. Little Louis
Poussard and the nurse who has reared him from
before his teeth give the newest adaptation of the
archaic theme. Poor old Poussard’s will left his
son (which was all the old musician had to leave
but debts) to his god-parents. Whereupon the
foster-mother settled things quite simply by run-
ning away with the boy. But, after all, she only
w ith her charge as far as Melbourne, to con-
sult M. Poussard’s oldest friend.
The story of that nurse is, I am told, a very sad
one. She tended the boy devotedly, and, when
his old father died, was shocked to find that she
was to lose her protege, to whom she had become
greatly attached. Also, poor woman, she found
that, M. Poussard having left not a solitary sou,
she would be unable to recover a penny of the (to
*}? r ) vast sum, .£3OO or so, in which her employer
died indebted to her for long years of service.
That scientific and enthusiastic professor who
courageously ran Sydney University into a few
thousands for scientific appliances, where only tens
had been understood by the Senate, is going to
leave this part of the world soon, and make for a
permanent stay in Cambridge. Mr. Pollock, a
local lecturer, may possibly fill the gap, if the
Senate’s craving for new blood doesn’t get the
better of it. But, as advts. exposing the vacancy
are already going the round of English papers, it
seems that the Senate, like smaller and unworthier
bodies, is going to yield to its thirst.
Speaking of this same ailment, stage fright in a
mild way took hold of some of the actors in one of
the mother city’s melodramas, the other night. It
was the kind of stage fright that gets under the
tongue, and pushes it about until it misses words,
and begins at the end of a sentence and then goes
u&ck to the beginning and finishes up in the
middle. One paroxysm forced the hero to the
singular confession “ I suffer from a craving for
thirst. ” The house was nearly all gods that night,
and nobody thought of laughing. The gods took
it seriously, knowing that a craving for thirst was
just as feasible as a craving for drink.
. To go back to Prof. Threlfall, his beautiful and
intellectual wife, who has just eonie back from a
trip abroad, doesn’t profess to be sorry either that
then; stay in Sydney is over. Her poems, most
of which originally appeared in these columns, and
which were published in book-form years ago, lift
her a long way above the line of Sydney society
women. At the Women’s College “At Home, ” the
other night, this slender, aesthetic-looking poetess
attracted more attention than anyone else, in
shapeless white satin and diamonds, supposed to
be an iniitation of Trilby as dressed by her sister,
the Trilby of the English stage. Remarkable
women, all the three ex-Miss Bairds.
The “at home” was intended as a welcome to
the University bride, Mrs. Wilson, but the spirit
fizzled out when it was known the death of the
bride s brother-in-law, Mr. G-. H. C. Simpson,
caused the absence of the bridal pair. Great sym-
pathy is felt for Mr. Justice G. B. Simpson and
Mrs. Simpson, who have thus lost their only child ;
their daughter died years ago, on the verge of
womanhood.
Country districts are very proud of their local
squads of “ Australian Horse,” and are showing it
at present by providing the provender, &c., for
local camps. Men of large appetite are “the real
Mackays.” One camp was held at Mudgee
recently and this month the warriors of Gundagai
and surrounding districts assemble to defeat the
local enemy with great slaughter.
He was what Mark Twain would call a theolo°i-
cal engineer, and he. had taken his celestial degree
and his marriage lines almost together. A Natural
History Society had sent him on a scientific expe-
dition to the wilds of Gippsland. With him went
many others, male and female. They all
carried cameras, and consistently took each other
singly and in groups. The Little Minister alone
would never be photographed. It would pain his
absent bride, he said, to see him pictured among
strangers as if he were enjoying himself away from
her, and he carefully sat apart when the cameras
were abroad. One afternoon a pretty, artful
maiden found her hammock pillows not too com-
fortable, and innocently appealed to him to shake
them up. He complied, bending over her with
care and thoroughness. And so he was snap-
shotted in various poses of the pretty work and
the pictures went off next post. And next day
came a wire which caused the little Naturalist to
leave suddenly for home.
Strange, sad stories go about concerning the
high-class marriages of Australian belles and
moneyed Englishmen. A very smart Aus-
tralienne, who made much splash and dazzle at
her wedding ceremony some years back, is said to
u u without ”in the lodge cottage of her
husband s ancestral dominions. There she cooks
his potatoes, and isn t received in the county be-
cause the parents-in-law have always refused to
smile on the match. Another “catch” turned
out to be a still greater swindle. Arriving in Eng-
land, the bride discovered that there wasn’t even a
lodge cottage, as there were no ancestral domains.
Ihe money was apparently there, but instead of
balls and presentations and an atmosphere of
strawberry-leaves she was taken to live in dull ex-
pensive lodgings never introduced to a soul, and
soon found that her husband was merely an insig-
mficant unit unknown outside his office. A bank-
account isn’t the magic key in London that it is
here.
And yet they will do it. “ Going to marry such
a rich Englishman ! ” is still the pet delusion of —
say, from seventeen to thirty. After thirty, some-
thing more definite is required, and so the old
belles marry slangy local merchants and
squatters, and settle down to far better times than
the youthful butterflies who have fluttered into
chilly English air. Moral—always marry in your
own country, a man of your own country who
means to live in his own country.

Speaking of Chinamen, a Chinese cook lately
left his situation m a highly elevated Sydney
family to become sub-editor of a Chinese journal
in this city. Four days later he came back and
asked to be taken on again. “No good paper*
editor send away his cook day after I go,” was his
explanation.

Last Saturday, for the first time in the case of a
large fire, the Hospital nurses turned out to show
their usefulness. The firemen found their atten-
tions of great value, as the nurses deftly removed
grit from their eyes at critical moments, and one
womanly creature had the nous to bring a hot cup
ot tea to an exhausted and over-grateful fireman.
Some classes of the community are by no means
partial to nurses, but henceforth the Sydney fire-
men will swear by them.

A novelty at the marriage feast of that elderly
Westralian Major who ran over from the gold-fields
t other day and chose a pretty young majoress
hereabouts, was the drive longshore indulged in
after the ceremony by the happy pair and their
mends. Apart from its digestive value, the
weddmg-dnve has few recommendations. For one
thing, revelry demoralises the most capable of
whips, and for a bridegroom to take the reins, is
sheer madness. Recently a Melb. girl, after an
up-country wedding, found herself driving home
with a party of new acquaintances. The squatter
m charge, the only he on board, tooling along in
great style, soon began to boast and show off to
the town visitor: ‘ see him clear this tree and
shave the other stump.’ His better-half from her
back-seat coaxed, scojded, commanded in vain and
openly taxed the vainglorious Jehu with a glass
too many. Other passengers cajoled and nagged
with no better success. The culprit grew more
and more reckless. At last when the horses were
ready to bolt and the ladies, who had been hysteri-
cal from the start, were all in tears, Mrs. What’s-
her-name announced bitterly that as ‘ Pa ’ seemed
to have eyes and ears only for his neighbor, the
latter had better persuade him to give up the
ribbons. The town girl was crying too, by this
time, partly through being bumped and jolted
nearly out of her skin, but she did what she could,
and, throwing her arms round the driver so as not
to fall out, pleaded broken-windedly, ‘ Kind, good,
dear Mr. J., please give me the reins.’ Mr. J.
tumbled asleep on her shoulder just then, and his
rightful owner, standing up at the back, soon
reduced the gee-gees to order, got the party home
and ‘ himself ’ to bed.
“There is in a Melb. architect’s office at pre-
sent, ‘ serving articles, ’ a young and pretty damsel
who aspires to become the pioneer woman-architect
of Australia. How the lady purposes inspecting
the different buildings under her charge has not
yet been revealed ; but probably she will don a pair
of Ada Reeve’s brown knickerbockers, and in them
wander over the joists and rafters. If rational
costume is not to be an attribute of the female
house-designer, the spilling of a couple of bags of
lime in an unfrequented street will probably suffice
to draw as many baldheads as the latest ballet.
. *‘ ‘Zack, ’ authoress of the book of stories, ‘Life
is Life, ’ which has been much praised by some
English critics, is the niece of a member of Melb.
Stock Exchange. Her name is Gwendoline Keats,
but she avers that she wrote under a pen-name
because of her ‘ veneration for a name which I felt
belonged in literature to the poet alone.’ ‘ Zack ’
has many Melbourne friends. She would have
done well to have had a map at hand when writing
her Australian stories. When she writes (in ‘The
Failure of Elipperty’): ‘The great Australian
liner steamed west, and Port Melbourne lay a
bluer streak on the blue horizon,’ she had for-
gotten her geography. Steaming west would have
beached her liner at Williamstowu.
ours affectionately,
GOULI-GOULI.

PERSONAL ITEMS.
Out of 32 Judges of N S.W. Supreme
Court but six have died in office.
Sunday at home. Mr. B. Backhouse,
M.L.C., reading John Norton’s Truth.
Of the 17 examiners in medicine in con-
nection with Sydney University not one figures
with an Irish degree.
Four practising Q.C’s. have been offered
and have declined within the last three years seats
on N.S.W. Supreme Bourt Bench.
Victorian police - court item : —George
Washington. charge —cutting timber without a
license. Thus doth history repeat itself.
Robert Roberts, founder of
the Christadelphian sect, just
dead in ’Frisco, whence he had
gone from Melbourne, having
been domiciled in the latter city
some years, was a Birmingham
man. In his native city he-
launched his harmless, heresy, as
about the 401st bird on the Pro-
testant tree. The theory is that
the wicked are all snuffed out,
Dr. Bromby’s idea, while the just
keep in a sort of Kennedy trance
till Gabriel blows his horn.
Roberts was a nice, fat patriarch,
not wholly unlike Parkes, and
came to church in a natty wag-
gonette, with a flock of chubby
little children, after the style of
most heterodox preachers. His
worry was that he could never
get anybody in Melb. to de-
nounce him, though equally ob-
noxious to Archbishop Carr,
Bishop Goe, Renton], and -Tos.
Symes. His divagation was from
the Plymouth Brethren, the sect
to which Varley belongs.
A customer once said to
blind John Dunn, founder of
Hobart Commercial Bank:
“ With your affliction, Mr. Dunn,
you must find it very difficult
to manage the affairs of the bank:.” “Not
a bit of it, sir,” answered Dunn; “I can smell
a bad bill, feel a bad coin, and tell by a man’s
voice if he’s a rogue I—which1 —which reminds me—what
about your overdrawn account ? ”
Bishop Camidge, of Bathurst, is the senior
Anglican bishop of N.S.W. ; Bishop Thornton, of
Ballarat, is senior of Australia ; and Primate
Co win, of Auckland, senior of Australasia.
J. R. Dickson, Queensland’s new Premier,
was fined in Brisbane Police-court a couple of
years ago for employing kanakas about his house-
hold in occupations interdicted by law.
In the Te Kooti time, the Colonial Office,
with incredible folly, wanted to send Chinese
Gordon to Maoriland, as Dictator. This was only
choked off by a consultation with Grey in London.
The death is cabled of J. J. Coleman, the
mustard double-millionaire. He who said: “I
made my fortune not so much out of the mustard
that people ate as out of what they left on the
plates. ”
One of the .Sydney “bankers,” who has
had his bit of trouble and done his bit of time, has
lately- started in business as a medical specialist,
and, judging by his advts., must be coining
money.
“You cannot all rise to be Ministers of
the Crown, my children, but one thing you can
do ; you can all be heroes and heroesses.”—Aus-
tralian Minister of Education to audience of State-
school pupils.
The present is Chief Justice Way’s 22nd
term of office as Acting-Governor of South Aus-
tralia, the first having been in 1877 on the retire-
ment of Governor Musgrove and prior to the
arrival of Governor Cairns.
Alderman Kippax, who retires from Cook
Ward, of Sydney City Council, has held the posi-
tion of Alderman for that ward for the past 35
years—a municipal record. Nine or ten candi-
dates are in the field for the seat.
Detective “ Mick ” Sexton, who cleared
up the “ Bendigo murder mystery ” by knocking
out the murder theory after three weeks’ fossick-
ing, got his taste for detective work by police-
reporting on Bendigo Independent.
Recent Medical Board registration in
N.S.W.—Ghandy Rastamji Dinshaji, L.S.A.,
London, 1881, and a Licentiate of Glasgow in
Medicine and Surgery, 1882. Most likely some
of the first fruits of the Rauji boom.
Barrister Simpson, who died the other day,
in Sydney, at the early age of 34, never held a
brief in any court over which his father, Justice
G. B. Simpson, presided. This agreement was
entered into by father and son when the former
was elevated to the Supreme Court Bench.
Thomas Gardner, one of the pioneers of
the Gulf country, and the rtian who cut the first
road to Gayndab (Q.j, died the other day in
Maryborough. His life of adventures closed
tamely in the billet of secretary to a school-of-arts.
A friend of Prof. Marshall-Hall’s is re-
sponsible for the following epigram:—
You've heard that the devil’s the father of lies;
I’ll mention his favorite son :
He edits the rag with a hundred eyes
And a squint in every one.
G. H. Reid was the first to dub in print
N.S.W., “ the mother colony of the Australias.”
He published a book with that sub-title away
back in his salad days, and is more than proud of
his sketchy trifle still—particularly of the sub-
title.
David Syme is taking care that when his
sons succeed to the Age property they will know
something of the business. At present one of his
boys is manager ; one oil the reporting staff; and
one behind the counter receiving advts. Scotch
training.
Premier Turner’s punctuality as a borough
councillor of St. Kilda is as mysterious as a saus-
age. It is presumably on the principle of Fantee,
developed by Kipling in a yarn. This means a
man trying to get out of himself, & la Haroun
Alraschid. When Councillor Muggins cheeks the
Premier, over the Public Wurrux Coimnytee, it is
as great a treat to Turner as to Muggins.
Ed. Bulletin. —You were lately talking
of Chief Justice Madden’s goodnature. Well, I
can depose to that. I’ve “ been there.” Once I
wanted to get away to a billet in an Australian
province, and to enable me to do so Dr. Madden
cheerfully stumped up a fiver. I haven't paid
him yet, but am saving up to do so, in the sure
and certain hope that when I offer it to His Ex.
he won’t take it. — Pressman.

The late Q. Premier Byrnes had one noble
and noteworthy characteristic-loyalty and affec-
tion for his mother and sisters. As everybody
knows, Byrnes was born poor. As he climbed the
ladder he was proud to take his relatives on his
shoulders.
Mr. G. H. Reid taking of “ silk ” makes
eight Q.C.’s at N.S.W.- Bar. Mr. B. R.
Wise is now the only ex-Attorney-General wear-
ing “stuff,” and Mr. Justice Cohen is the only
member of N.S.W. Supreme Court who has not
taken “ silk.”
Down South there’s a doctor who, when he
visits a patient with an infectious disease, stands
afar off in the passage and yells, “ put out your
tongue,” “how’s your appetite ?” and so forth,
but couldn’t be induced to go in for any fee you
could think of.
It is more than likely that Major-General
French’s term of office, as N.S.W. Commandant,
will be extended beyond the date originally fixed.
By the way, the Major-General is having a bad
time at the hands of certain grossly insubordinate
amateur warriors who dabble in politics.
Dr. Malcolm Morris, brother of Prof.
Morris, of Melb. University, takes a leading part
in the scheme for putting Koch’s germ theory into
practice for the prevention and cure of tuberculo-
sis in England. The enthusiastic Morris reckons
that consumption 50 years hence will be only an
occasional disease, and rarely fatal.
Charles Graham Wilson, the new member
for Armidale (N.S. W.), was council-clerk for the
borough of Armidale over 20 years ago. As a
lightning-change artist in the borough council
line he can take a front place. He resigned the
clerkship one day, was elected alderman the next
day, and two days later was elected Mayor.
One of Australia’s ablest hangmen went on
strike for increased salary a couple of weeks ago.
He had somewhere picked up the rates of pay
which man-butchers get in other countries (in
France the executioner gets <£3oo a year), and he
protested against playing the blackleg longer. He
was persuasively talked over, however, and the
strike is off.
“Melb.” : One cannot be satiric on the
desire of C. H. James, the boss land merchant, to
get a certificate in Insolvency before be dies. Yet
this salve to the conscience of our whilom leading
Wesleyan (while he was gilded) is an odd idea of
his. Such worldlings as Walter Scott and Mark
Twain wanted to work it off. They wouldn’t take
a certificate.

Newspaper war in progression between the
Vials and the McMahons of Sydney as to which
has the better claim to having saved the life of
the Duke of Edinburgh at Clontarf (N.S. W.) in
March, 18C8. As a matter of fact, honors are
equally divided. McMahon stood between the
prince and O’Farrell, while Vial seized the would-
be assassin from behind.
H. H. Champion is again leader-writer on
the Age, by ilavid Syme’s invitation. Consider-
ing his biting, scornfully-true character-sketch of
David, published in The Champion, this action
shows either magnanimity on the old man’s part,
or that the Age was hard-up for the sort of literary
ability and ideafulness which Champion possesses.
Perhaps a little of both.
Judge Docker, whose erratic proceedings
on N.S.W. District Court Bench have provoked
several Parliamentary debates, and who has, as a
consequence, been remonstrated with by more
than one Premier for speeches “in bad taste,”
was the prize verse-maker in Sydney University
in his early days. He also won the Wentworth
medal for chaste English prose.
Speaker Mason (Vic.) was asked whether
the Leg. Assembly would not take notice of Fat-
man Campbell’s remark that “ the unfortunates of
the city” had control over M.’sP., and would
have more in the event of women’s suffrage being
passed. “Oh, dear no,” neatly replied Mason,
“ Mr. Campbell was, of course, only referring to
M.’sP. in the Legislative Council.”
Poor, solid old Baron von Mueller's grave
in St. Kilda (Vic.) cemetery is still without its
headstone ; and the secretaries to the fund plain-
tively appeal again to a public that so soon forgets
—that mostly has, in fact, after only 12 months,
got so far as to ask what the unselfish botanist
was and did. Only about half the necessary
amount has been laboriously raked in.
M‘Kenzie, the blind member of Vic. Par-
liament, delivered a two-hours’ speech on Minister
Best’s new Land Bill, crammed full of figures,
which he gave with absolute correctness entirely
from memory. M'Kenzie and ex-Premier . Sbiels
were schoolfellows. The blind man is by far the
abler of the two, and might have been Opposition
leader before Duncan Gillies was appointed had
he wished.
Prof. Marshall Hall withdraws his resigna-
tion ; says he didn’t know it was loaded, or words
to. that effect. This looks like a smart bit of
generalship. The scare is dead, the Argus
could not revive it if it howled itself hoarse, and a
committee that might have fired him out on his
musical ear when the unco’ gude were all flapping
their wings may now have the temerity to. give the
young man one chance more, on condition that he
abandons the pursuit of Poetry—a female who
gets further ahead in the race every minute.
