“ We get drowned, or worry to death, or
finish up in a pub.—and do the finishing up
quickly, as a rule.’’ Thus recently Capt. T. W.
Loekyer, about the best-known and most popular
of Australian coastal skippers. In his ease it was
the worry-kill ; he died in Adelaide on Sept. 23.
One of the oldest masters on the coast, his troubles
began when he put a passenger in irons between
Adelaide and Melbourne, and found the man dead
five minutes after ; that was between three and
four years ago. After that he got on to banks
and into collisions, and struck bad weather and
had bad times before Marine Boards, and wound
up by bringing the Adelaide over from the West
in a disabled condition through terrible weather.
That passage was the last straw : he went ashore
and died—“ finished up in a pub.,” but hardly in
the way he meant.
Rev. Steele, Moderator, at a recent
gathering of Melb. Presbyterian Association,
told a good Scotch joke of his own. He was
present when two shearers disputed over the ques-
tion of the immortality of the soul. The reverend
was appealed to by the unbeliever. “Well,” he
answered, “if you will be a beast, I have no
objection.” The assembly was delighted, but
Rev. Steele only dodged the issue. Writer heard
the matter put better by a commonplace agri-
culturist, near Smeaton (Vic.). His reverence
was rating Clarke for giving too much thought to
his horse, The Dawn, a nice little animal, that
had won a race or two. “Annywan wud be
thinkin’ it was a good Christian ye were pattin’
an’ pettin’, an’ fadin’ an’ clanin’ all day long.
Sartin it is, yer horse won’t get into heaven wid
yez,memahn. Fwad’s that! fwad ’re ye ehum-
merin’ about”? “Sure,” said Matty, “I was
on’y sayin’ that, maybe, he loud get in. Up
there, they’ll be bether judges av a horse than yer
'riverance, I’m thinkin ” !
Nick the Second’s, last escape from the
Nihilists 5 was a narrow one. He was, during
November, to have consecrated a chapel which a
rich wool merchant had built at Tsarkoe Selo,
where the Emperor often resides. But an artist
employed to paint the interior found a suspicious
wire which turned out on investigation to lead
down to a carefully-prepared mine in the founda-
tion. Wire and mine must have been put in place
at an early stage of the work, and no outsider
could well have accomplished such a business
without being observed by the workmen.
Agent-General Cockburn, writing to a
S.A. journalist, relates how he is fascinated by
the wilderness of books in the big smoke, and is
tempted to buy by thearmful. Which illustrates the
difference between Cockburn and bis predecessor,
Playford. The latter bad no soul for anything
but produce and profits, but Cockburn is, and
always was, a dreamer, a student of the “isms.”
Lets his hair grow long and his clothes grow
shabby. Cockburn could lecture for an hour on
the history and qualities of a potato; Playford
would simply sell it as a common “spud ”at top
price.
A prominent Victorienne, when travelling
recently in England, much amused a “ noble
Marquis ” by her outspoken* criticism “of whatever
she saw. “Well, now, Lady Blank,” he re-
marked on one occasion, “ What do you think of
our English beauties?” “Beauties? ’Aggard, I
call them,” remarked the Australian lady. “But
take Lady Londonderry, she is conceded on
all sides to be one of the most beautiful women
in all Europe. Do you not admire her?” Lady
Blank sniffingly surveyed her from top to toe, and
at last contemptuously observed : “ Take off her
clothes, and what is she? ”
Miss Helen Hart, the stern, unbending
lecturess and fighter for woman’s rights, has issued
the following in the Yic. country towns where she
is touring :- L
NOTICE—The public are cautioned against men and
woriien circulating unfounded reports against my char-
acter. Sometimes it is said that lam an immoral woman.
This is false. At other times that my expenses are not
paid ; this is also false, as I carry my receipt-book and
can produce it. These reports are circulated and set
going by unknown enemies who are jealous of my success
and the honorable name I have made for myself hv my
platform talent. I offer £5 REWARD to any persons
who will give truthful information which will enable me
to trace such disgraceful traducers of the character of a
respectable and noble woman.
It was the late J. T. M‘Mahon, of Sydney
G.P.0., who on behalf of the Crown identified
O’Farrell in Darlinghurst Gaol on the day after
the latter’s attempt on the life of the Duke of
Edinburgh at Cloptarf. M‘Mahon was the first to
lend a hand to_the Prince, who had fallen to the
ground. William Vial was engaged capturing.the
would-be assassin. The late Sir William Manning
thought it was lie who was shot, and accordingly
laid himself out on the grass and didn’t get up
until the danger was over. The second bullet hit
George Thorn, of Queensland, in the heel, and
that burly politician carried the scare as well as
the scar for years. Most of that old crowd are
now dead, but the Duke is yet to the fore—heavy
and hearty.

A potent factor in Premier Byrnes’ fatal
illness was that he had a rooted objection to muscu-
lar exercise of any kind. A journey of 800 yards
was covered by a cab-drive. When at Brisbane
Grammar School, Byrnes could not be persuaded
to join any kind of athletic club, but hurried
home to study. Add to this the nervous strain of
keeping his cantankerous team in hand and a
weakness for good living, the result of any marked
illness was bound to be serious.
Demise: R. Ford. One of the cleverest
men of affairs—built for biz, like Tramway Sprigg.
After broking on Ballarat, and making a repute
as one of the lions of the Corner, Ford took the
Town Clerkship of Ballarat West, and achieved
success in that line. This procured him the secre-
taryship of Melb. Harbor Trust, whence he
bounded into the position of Assistant Commis-
sioner to Speight, at the railways. Here he was
again so thoroughly at home that dismissal broke
the mainspring of his life.

“C-F.”: Re the cruelty of branding.
About fiv® years ago a Bornean planter was fined
20d0dol -. for branding his Chinese coolies with a
red-hot iron. Evidence showed that there was no
senous objection on the part of the subjects till
JNo. 38 complained on the score that XXXVIII.
took longer to complete than the marking of No.
« W j j a ■k’ ®i s . sense °f equal justice was
offended. In our district we were more merciful,
contenting ourselves with marking our men, on
arrival in port, with dilute nitric acid, in which
a ten-cent piece had been dissolved. Some fellows
saw humor in the situation when the “ Chinkies”
essayed to wash away the tingling sensation,
thereby, of course, intensifying the blackness of
the mark. In a month the blackened skin would
peel off, leaving a pink scar, which in turn faded
to brown; but by then John was on the estate,
and absconding meant death from starvation in
the jungle, or from fever, dysentery or beri-beri
i er l 8 ca P ture a . nd return. Sheep to the
slaughter were nothing for mildness compared
with the Singkehs under this treatment, and white
men in those parts had scarcely the right to talk
about cruelty to the Tanil, who, with a hot iron,
draws caricatures of the moon and other things on
the rump and flank of his pet cow and favorite
waggon-bnllock, finishing off the former with a
lace-frmge pattern just where a lace-fringe pattern
should be.

“Boleg Much interested in “ Yar-
rumi’s ” rabbit. Have never seen him south of
the Murray, but know the southern bunny well.
He rarely finds himself in a place where he can’t
turn. In nine cases out of ten he can turn easily
in a hollow opening, and in the odd case he
scuffles out so suddenly that “ Yarrumi ” might
easily be deceived as to the end first
appearing. Further, when he takes shelter
in a standing hollow tree (going up by means
of back and legs) he never turns to come
down. Have never known a rabbit die in a log
(if blocked in) without a long struggle, evidenced
by scratehmarks , and, broadly speaking, where-
ever a breeze can go bunny can follow. Also,
cover up the openings to his burrow, and the same
bunny will strike daylight at the nearest point.

The ferocious humor of Q. administrators.
Western Queensland is notorious for its sharp
winters with their biting Western blasts. Govt,
therefore very considerately sent out (at the end
of August!) to the Boulia blacks a pile of blankets
to shield their emaciated forms. Probably the
poor nigs, will get a supply of flannels and com-
forters about the middle of December.
• • * •
Birdsville (Q.) police, out with camels after
man wanted for issuing valueless cheque. Passing
Annandale- „orgina River, camel slipped and
put out its right thigh ; constable left black-boy
m charge of camel, went into Birdsville to report,
and was sent back by the sergeant with a flea in
his ear for “ leaving valuable Govt, property in
charge of nigger.” Constable to sergeant, first
report: “12 a.m.—Went down and see (sic) the
camel. _ He is 132 ft. from the bed of the creek;
he’s within high water-mark. Later.—See the
camel agin ; he is now 2ft. higher up the bank ;
he is making for the shade, but he’ll niver reach
it, ’tis up-hill.” The camel was allowed to remain
there for months, and got quite fat feeding on
green boughs, till at last orders came from head-
quarters to shoot the animal and forward the skin
to Blackall.

“Inna”: The mathematical deficiencies
of Australian blacks is “ C.R.’s ” text. I would
like to drag in a yarn about the accountancy of
Australian miners. In the days of the Palmer
three diggers sold their gold at the bank—,£947.
They wanted to equally share the cash, but
couldn’t arrive at an agreement as to the amount
of a one-third share. The manager offered to help
them—after they had wrangled for half-an-hour
at the counter. They wouldn’t trust him. They
drew the amount in £1 notes, and one of the
diggers commenced to deal, as if it were a card
game. But there were 947 cards in the pack, and
quarrels soon arose as to whether two notes had
STRUCK IT.
MINING EXPERT : “ Where is your reef—ei —minah !”
MINAG: “ Why, yer sittin' on it!”
not been dealt to the dealer, and whether the
other man had not been missed. After each dis-
pute, the notes were gathered up again, and the
dealing ’ started afresh. In about three hours
the counting was done—and in three weeks the
cash was done.

“ F.F.O’D.” : Once more. When the ’roo
is hopping along at leisure he often strikes the
ground with his tail, but, as he increases his
speed, he lowers his head until, when he has it
all on his nose is as near to the ground as his
tail. He sees hardly further than a hop ahead of
him, and landing in one spot, has his eyes on
next landing-place. With a wallaby it is differ-
ent. Hundreds of times have I chased him but
never saw him tail the ground.

“Zulu”: Be ’possum-cats. There is a
lady in Terang who owns a female cat which used
to take periodical trips rabbiting. On one occa-
sion she stayed away longer than usual, and on her
return presented her mistress with two stranee-
looking kittens. These kittens are now about 18
months old a,nd are hybrids. The form is after
that of the ordinary cat, but the fur is decidedly
possum, whde the paws and tail show a little of
each, they eat meat and occasionally grass, and
would make excellent domestic felines were it not
for the objectionable ’possum-odour. Saw them
t other day, and one is about to have a litter— of
oats or possums ?

A novel feline fact, and an interesting
variation on the pig-mendacity business. A Bris-
bane publican possesses a cat which is a splendid
ratter. T’other day, she slew a particularly tine
specimen, and Boniface routed out a rat’s nest
and, for curiosity’s sake, put the young rodents
(only a few days old) in among pussy’s kittens.
To everyone’s surprise, madame, in place of
slaughtering the intruders, took kindly to them,
and set to work feeding them and bringing them
up with her own family. . . . Would be interest-
ing to follow up the experiment.
* * * *
A Windsor MAGAziNE-writer declares that,
when the young of the duckbill platypus are
hatched out, they are quite blind and naked, and
their bills are very short; but whether they are
reared in pouches or not is still doubtful, as,
although the marsupial (or pouch) bones are pre-
sent in the skeletons of the platypus, it is not
known for certain whether the young ones are
actually carried in them or not. This statement
ought to arouse The Bulletin aboriginals.

“ Buzzy ” : lie, the great pyjama-question.
Coming home from a dance up Monaro way, self
and friend roused up the (new) local boniface,
who, unlocking the door, led the way into the bar
attired only in his night-shirt. “ Hullo, Bill ” I
said, ‘ £ where’s your pyjamas ? ” He turned to his
shelves, and, after searching among his bottles
for half-a-minute, he replied, “ Well, boss, you
see I am t got into the run of this place yet, an’ I
don t see no pajamas,’ but I can give you some
first-rate three-star.”

“Scotty ” : “ W.S.T.” is not the only one
who has a sort of proof of his bunyip theory. I
am at present camped where last year there was a
well-known old-man Murray cod. He was seen
by everyone near this, and his back was like a
swine s. He started about the mouth of a creek
opposite, visited every snag, and worked up two
or three hundred yards, frightening big fish into
jumping two feet out of the river. One man fired
at him two or three times ; once he jumped a
toot out of the water and fell on his side. If
Oeorge had given him the second barrel he could
n , im : There was no use fishing as soon
as Ihe Bake began his prowl. Again, at Quat
yuatta (Johnny Wallace’s), there is a cod that, I
Pj! 6 .’ weighs 2001bs. The fisherman there has
had mm on more than once, always losing his
hook. Therefore, having heard the splashing of
smaller fish (a bad sign for fishing), I am of the
same opinion as “ W.5.T.,” namely, that the bun-
yip is a Murray cod, and, possibly—beer.

At , a way-back pub.
recently writer sat at
table with the champion
spitter of the Maranoa.
He spat out the gas three
times running; he spat
at a big kangaroo-dog in
the corner until the brute
got up and “cleared.”
Then the champion sat
back, breathed heavily
and spat the fire into
oblivion. Given a good
chew of tobacco, he said,
and he’d blind a mob
of cattle. To-morrow he
was going down to Syd-
ney to spit over the
G.P.O. and G. H. Reid.
Then he arose, threw out
his chest and spat his
initials on the tin looking-
glass. Asked him if there was anyone in Austra-
lia who could beat him at the game. He answered
that nothing but an old-man drought could dry
him, up.
• • • *
“ J. Jingle” seizes Henry Lawson by the
throat and hair and ear and the slack of his
habiliments:—
Dear Bulletin.— Re Lawson’s “Dry Country,” how
much more of this drivelling pessimism are we going to
get? The sensible bushman only laughs at the partly
incomprehensible, partly untrue, jangle, the reading
aloud of which is like the smiting of a kerosene-tin ; but
there is a class to whom this sort of stuff can do infinite
harm. Surely Lawson doesn’t want to do infinite harm I
The man who, in the Far West, or anywhere else, lias
shrivelled leather" and “ brittle reins ”is a man who s
too lazy to look after his gear, and wants “ rounding on,”
not pitying or extolling as a martyr to the “unknown
absentee” and “ dressmakers in Paris,” whatever the3'’ve
got to do with it. Also, if he’s too dirty to keep his hair
cut short when travelling, he deserves to have jt rot off,
though I can’t strike anybody among western travellers
about here that has ever heard of this result from camp-
ing out on the “ashes" (he means dust, I suppose, but
couldn’t get it into that line) of the Dry Countree. A
townsman, reading the second verse of this gruesome
incongruity, would imagine the West to be pretty well
paved with corpses. What with the “ bleaching whitely,”
the “ rather recent,” and the implied other stages of
decomposition, why, the trenches outside Santiago are
not a circumstance to Lawson's West. Worse nonsense
never saw the light of print. Why, more people have
died in London and Paris from sunstroke in the last
two or three months than ever lived on the western
tracks that Lawson has travelled. They’ve got to die,
everywhere 1 Also, the cattle are amusing. 20,000 of
wu Peking up the dust of the rags from the corpse-army!
Why, two thousand head almost always travel in two
mobs of 1000 each with separate gangs of men, and I
doubt if anybody ever saw 20,000 head in a mob anywhere
in Australia. I have seen between two and three thousand
on the main camp of a big Northern cattle station.
Twenty thousand head feeding across a flat plain would
more than reach the horizon ! Then the blacks : first,
you needn’t fear them ; afterwards, you’d better carry a
revolver; what for? Perhaps to shoot yourself if the
water turns out to be a mirage; surely the unpleasant
insect with the proverbially small heart is beaten in that
respect by Lawson’s imaginary westerner. But he is not
like that. His heart is as big as that organ will run to,
and it’s very few of him that feels at all like the miser-
able object Lawson makes him out. The bushman’s row
is hard hoeing enough without a slab of this jangling
misery being thrown at him once a week by the paper
whose next issue he is looking forward to as soon as the
last is finished. In six months any man having work can
save enough to go to almost any part of the globe, and
yet they don’t leave that awful west with its corpse-paved
desert, its rotting hair and change of hells —ugh ! “ Goes
an unprotected maiden ’cross the clearing carrion laden.’’
What’s it all about? “No limb for saddle-strapping.”
Again, what is it all about ? We can’t make anything of
it up here, and some of us really do, or ought to, know
something about the bush.

It was in ’52 that one Dave Devine had
about .£9OO worth of gold to take from Ballarat to
Geelong, and two or three of his “friends” re-
solved to save him the trouble. As it happened,
however, the visit of a bank gold-buyer enabled
Dave to convert the gold into notes, and, with 18
fifties in the lining of his old-fashioned belltopper,
he got beyond Buninyong before he was bailed-up
and ordered to strip. Knowing the men well, he
affected great indignation, and, dashing his hat on
the ground, offered to fight them one after another.
They made him strip, however, and searched his
clothes, cart, and harness—even ripping open the
saddle and collar —but never (as they didn't know
the gold had been converted into notes) thought of
examining the hat lying on the ground, and were
fain to depart cursing. When they found out
afterwards that the money had been under their
noses all the time they were fit to cut their throats.

after a heavy rain, they were compelled to camp
for ten days until things hardened. Another time
lost six days looking for bullocks that had got
away with some wild scrub cattle. With such

The Impure Drug Question.
“ Crotonis Oleum,” to The Bulletin :
Dear Bulletin.— As a qualified pharmacist of 14 years’
standing-, I quite concur with “ Phyyaka” (8., 24/9/’9B) as
to necessity for official drug inspection, as a check on
adulteration. I have had personal experience of the
admirable working, in this respect, of the Continental
system ; but let me point out that, if such should be in-
troduced here, the State will have, sooner or later, to go
further, and enact that only pharmacists may legally dis-
pense prescriptions. Medical men should not be allowed
to compound their own prescriptions, because there is no
check upon blundering, which is something more than
possible with men who have had, at most, only six
months’ training in pharmacy. A much longer period is
required to make a man a reliable compounder. And,
even in places where there is no pharmacist, the physi-
cian should procure his medicines compounded in such a
way that he has only to write directions. The compound
to be labelled with names of constituents, which label
will also serve as a prescription—making the subscribing
pharmacist responsible. And, as regards the medico in
practice large enough to employ a “dispenser” of his
own, you now and then see an advt.
-pvOCTOR’S GROOM wanted. Must be able to dis-
pense!
The State would also have to fix a standard price for a
standard drug, so that prescriptions might be uniform,
not only in quality, but in cost; price of each ingredient
to be marked sepau.tely in margin. Prices also should
be so fixed as to give a fair remuneration for professional
skill in compounding, and not lumped together as at
present at from 4s. 6d. to 2s. 6d. per Boz. bottle of medi-
cine-regardless of how much water or other ingredient
it may contain.
Another difficulty. Water in the B.P. is understood as
distilled, and to get this fresh and pure there is required
a still, to use which in these provinces a license must be
obtained. Few will take the trouble to do this; and,
though one may chance to get distilled water in one or
two establishments, even then it may be only “ engine-
water." . ,
I would restrict the professional part of dispensing en-
tirely to “Doctors” or “Masters” of Pharmacy, who,
for such degree, should have undergone a post-graduate
two-years’ course, and have been at least four years in
practice.
For public information, also, the State, as in Germany,
should have patent medicines analysed ;'and the sale-of-
poisons law should be made less of a dead letter as regards
preparations at present practically shielded by the word
“ patent.”
The laws relating to poisonous drugs, in fact, are, in
any case, only a farce, inasmuch as not only do many
pharmacists neglect the statutory precautions, but most
storekeepers will sell to a regular customer any kind or
quantity of poison without witness, signature, or question
as to use. One fatal result of this laxity, within my own
knowledge, was penalised with a fine of—£l!
“ Bargo ” : Recently heard a “ geebung ”
relating his experience with a palmist and phren-
ologist. “ The bloke asked me if I’d had any
accidents, an’ I said two ; an’ blow me if he
didn’t show me where this gutter round my thumb
—‘life-line,’ he called it—was broken twice.
When I got home I washed my hand and found
three places, an’ then I remembered the fall I
had off old Barclay’s mare ; this ’ere middle line
he called the ‘Fennis line,’ and it joined this one
he called ‘the money gutter.’ Well, I married
old Sam Taylor’s daughter, and he give me a
tenner for a weddin’-present. Everything that
cove said was right.”

THE AUSTRALIAN GEM TRADE.
H. Newman, the well-known jeweller of 175
Elizabeth-street, Melbourne, is the largest buyer
of opal in Australia, and opal miners will find it
to their advantage to send their opal direct to
him. The firm has a reputation for straight deal-
ing extending over a period of 40 years, which is
a sufficient guarantee to consignors that they will
be honorably treated. H. Newman is also a
buyer of all other Australian gems.*
There is a peculiar appropriateness- in the
selection of the “ Divine William ” by the Roman
Catholics of Germany as “ Protector of the Holy
Places in the East.’’ It is some years now since
William the Sore-eared announced his acceptance
of the Deity as a junior partner in the manage-
ment of the universe, and impressed upon his own
subjects the claim of the Supreme Ordainer of
Things to their obedience in all matters outside
the sphere of Imperial Germanic influence. Logic-
ally, the boss-ship of all Holy Places on the earth,
in the East and elsewhere, should be instantly
handed over to the senior influence in the affairs
of men.

“ Steele Rudd ” : Used to wonder at
number of dead lambs strewn round a solitary
tree on a Q. station plain. One day watched a
native bear descend said tree and pounce upon a
sleeping lamb, which he killed. Joyfully in-
formed boss, and was called a fool to the delight
of a raw-boned, blind boundary-rider.

• * • *
The agent and interpreter for the batch
of 100 Japs, lately imported to Mossman, N.Q.,
is having a lovely time. Hardly a day passes but
a mob of Japs, strike and arrive where he lives,
followed presently by furious canegrowers de-
manding that he shall knock sense in or out of his
countrymen. One farmer threatened to “stiffen”
the agent because, said he, “ The brown devils
came up to my house this morning without a stitch
of clothes on them and frightened the wits out of
my wife and family ! ” On another plantation the
Japs, packed up one morning and cleared without
a word, but returned next day with the following
note to their. employer from the agent : —“ To
Mr. James Reynolds, Esqr.—Kindly let the
bearers have some flesh of a female fowl.” Ye
Gods ! the “ flesh of-a female fowl ” ! It takes a
white man all his time to raise three johnnie-
cakes a day.

J. G. Reuthner, a Lutheran missionary in
mid-Australia, has for years been patiently putting
the New Testament into the dialect of the Dieri
natives ; and the print of it has just been issued
in S.A. —it was a two-years’ job for a little Ger-
man printing-office in the country. This is given
as a sample of what the Dieris put up with :
Ja Uakana Mardatandrakampanietja Wokarana Wonti
Multibanila Manila, Ja Nunkangu Jatani Wonti“ Ngu-
jangujara Jinkinietjajai Mina Ngaiani Ngankananto 1 ”
The foregoing means : “ Then came also publicans
to be baptised, and said unto Him, ‘ Master, what
shall we do ?’ ” But the Dieri-man won’t have
any doubt about what he would have the only
publican that he is likely to have ever heard of do.
A N.S.W country-town
shopkeeper, noted for his zoo-
logical proclivities, once placed
a large live carpet-snake in his
window as an attraction, and
as the snake took matters
quietly, a large rat was shut in
with him for company. Wagers
were made that the rat would
be swallowed inside three hours,
but second day found him
alive and very hungry ; and on
the third day the rat had killed
and eaten part of the snake !
* * *
“F.”: The natives of the
Musgrove and McDonnell
ranges in Central Australia
possess foot - coverings. They
are different from those said
to be worn by the natives of
the N.W. sand-ridge country.
The upper part is woven of
human hair. The soles are
thick, made of dry grass mixed
with gum and blood. On the
surface of the sole is stuck a
coating of emu-feathers which
renders them noiseless. Their
use has not been quite deter-
mined. There is a paper in the
records of the Linnean Society
of N.S.W. about them. There
is a pair in the Australian
Museum, Sydney, and several
were brought back by the
“Horne ” party which went on
a scientific quest to the McDon-
nell ranges. The native name
is “kaditcha,” or “ kadisha.”
Favenc has a book based on
them: “ The Mocassins of Si-
lence.”

“ Buck ” : In response to
“W.”( Bulletin, 3/8/98) “ Wok-
ka atchie ” (correctly “Wokka,
or Wogera, atchie”) means a crow. I presume
that the blacks, seeing explorer Kennedy boiling
his billy on their graveyard, mistook him for a
new species of corvus searching for carrion, and
named him accordingly. The blacks of western
N.S.W. and Queensland have a habit of bestow-
ing aboriginal names on white people marked by
any peculiarity. A few instances: “Kumera,”
left-handed ; “ Kurroo,” grey-haired ; “ Koopaa,”
white-haired.
The next horror Australia will have to
keep its eye open for is the chico —the “jigger ” of
travelled Americans : the horrible little flea-like
insect, talked and written of by New Australians,
which lays its ulcerating eggs in the human feet.
From South America, the abomination has
reached the blacks of Central Africa, and will, it
is expected, be soon brought down by “ boys ” to
Natal and the Rand. Then, having been ac-
climatised, it will be ready for introduction to
Australia.
* * * *
Ed. Bulletin.—So the “ steady, saving,
servile individual ” is beneath the blatant, blear-
eyed, besotted, beautiful boozer of the bush ?
Well, God help him ! and help this continent also,
if its free sons and true sons are the yahoos who
periodically stumble into a shanty to talk “ scab ”
and “ scrap,” and roar for rum, and fall down and
lie in the sun with their shirts off and their mouths
dripping, until the shanty-keeper straightens them
up and kicks them and their filthy bundles out on
to the track. The “ independent spirit of a free
and purely Australian nation ” ! — Steele Rudd.
* * * *
. TThe arid Tasmanian now asks in a husky,
chipped voice for a “ Boag ” (Boag is a prosperous
brewer) when his opportunity comes to “moisten.”
The term is unquestionably accepted all over the
island as signifying a long beer, and the irrespon-
sible drunk is no longer boozed but “Boaged.”
Interesting to watch how far it will spread.
* * * *
Ancient Chow (anything from 65 to 125
years, absolutely toothless, and with usual Chinese
custom of emphasising sentences as if abusing
listener), riding along track, after heavy rain, and
looking to his dog-traps, meets manager of an ad-
joining run. “ Day, .1 immy ! ” “ Goo’day, Mitter
Barker. You dam foo’” (undue emphasis on the
noun). “ Hullo, Jimmy ! —what for ?” “ No, no !
you no savee—you dam foo’! ” hurled at the other
this time. Barker gets wild and grabs .Jimmy by
the tail. “ Why, you yellow old ” Jimmy,
with a tremendous effort: “ No, no, Mitter
Barker ! Big lain come alonga here, fillem tanks
and dlains, an’ makee you dam foo' ? ” Then
“Mitter Barker” tumbled, and apologised to
Jimmy’s pigtail.
• * * •
“E.S.S.”: A few years ago there was
a mob of about 80 tame pelicans at Thurlingra
Station, Q. The kitchen stands within a few feet
of a big waterhole, down the bank of which was
thrown all refuse—upon which the pelicans came
regularly to feed. And, being unmolested, they
ventured awhile to the door of the kitchen, and at
last would sit about the place like tame geese,
taking no notice whatever of passers-by.
• » * •
“ Werrimee ’ : On a station near Bourke
there used to be a five-legged steer ; the fifth leg
grew out near the wither. Have seen a chicken
with three legs, a snake with two tails ; and a
sheep with three horns, the third being an osseous
prolongation of the nose. This kind of horn fre-
quently crops out from ear-points. On Boolarwell
Station, near Goondiwindi, there were several
four or five-horned sheep.
• » * *
“ Yang ” : Any of your readers ever
try to kill a crocodile? At six one morning
two of us began on a 14-footer. I took out his
heart and laid it in a billy of warm water ; some-
one had fooled me that it would beat for an hour
or two. At eight we went to breakfast, and at
THE APPROACH OF SUMMER.
8.30 turned-to again, and found the subject ap-
parently very well satisfied with himself. Went
to see if the heart was beating. It was gone !
By 10 o’clock we’d done our duty by the beast,
and were prepared to swear he was dead. My
friend hung the hide out to dry, while I had
another look for that heart. I found it—in the
brute’s stomach; but he hadn’t been able to
manage the billy, which had lodged in his throat
and probably choked him. Otherwise he might be

alive still. [P.S. —What was the ’dile doing while
his heart was being cut out and put in the billy ?
If he was loose, so that he could get at it and
swallow it afterwards, why didn’t he remon-
strate?—Ed. B.]

“ S.R.” : Ran the mail once from Clifton
to Picton (South Q.), and an odd character shep-
herding on the plains for Headington Hill used
regularly waylay me to ask the time and talk
about dead sheep. He had a long-tailed chestnut
mare, pig fat, which he always led after the flock
or wherever he went. Asked him his reason, but
he never obliged. Got off one day to yarn with
him and my horse pulled away. He wouldn’t go
after him on the mare, so I jumped on her and
was immediately fired off on to the broad of my
back. Found out after that the brute hadn’t been
mounted for four years !
“P.”: The poor farmer in
S. A., though he scuffles along 18 hours
or so a day, has just as much of the
tired feeling as most people. A
valuator who went North lately was,
therefore, not surprised to find half-
a-dozen reaping-machines in a circle
in the middle of a paddock; but when
the poor farmer explained that he
had “ drored ’em up that way to pro-
tect one agin the other,” the hustler
felt tired too. On the same farm a
new binding-machine (one of the most
expensive parts of a farm’s equip-
ment) was stuffing up a hole in a
fence. No wonder “ farmin’s played
eout.”
Dear Bulletin. —The rancor of
Gladstone’s clean-shirt misprint in
the Times lies in the fact that the
Irish used to wear saffron-colored
shirts, to save washing. In fact, a law
was passed compelling them to wash
them three times a year, and this was
when Tara’s Hall (built of wood)
held learned monks, or something,
who were in scientific Continental cor-
respondence about eclipses and sich. I
have often been half-eaten, like Brock-
man by the man-eating lion, for dar-
ing to state this, and my authority
was thrown into the fire at Merungle
by an enraged man from Connemara ;
but I give you the name of the
book, “ Larry Lynch.” I wrote to
all the new and second-hand book-
sellers in Sydney and Melbourne for
a copy, but failed to procure one. It
was written by an army-officer, who
was in Ireland in some rebellion or
another (I’m not up in history), and
he quoted Hollinshed, giving page
and chapter. Of course it is a libel,
and another injustice. But, consider-
ing the dirty state of the Saxon in that
period, and the fact that his Govern-
ment wasn’t clean enough to order
him to wash his shirt at all —let
alone three times a year—l don’t know
why the Connemara man got so
angry.— Scotty the Wbinkler.
In the old Longford (Tas.) days
there was an ancient autocrat named
Walker who had a curious habit of
fixing his eye-glass and scanning each
passer-by. A beastly native on a
horse returned the compliment by twigging old
money-bags through one of his stirrup-irons.
He was summoned for insulting behavior, and
got seven days in the logs.
The wife of a former nigh Westralian
official was recently requested by the P.O. autho-
rities to call for a parcel which had arrived from
England, contents not specified. She called.
“ Oh, Mr. B.” (to parcels-post official), “ can't you
give me the parcel without my naming the con-
tents ? ” “ Very sorry, madam, but you must tell
me what is in it.” “Oh, but, really I—er—is
there no lady here to whom I can tell it?” Mr.
B. : “ Oh, yes ; there’s Miss , at the stamp-
counter.” Information accordingly confided to
Miss that there are “ night-dresses ” in the
parcel —“and you know I couldn’t tell the man.
Would you mind doing it?” Miss ——, however,
pretends great horror at the suggestion, and the
hyperdelicate lady is compelled to commit the
dreadful word to paper before she can receive her
lingerie.
“ K.K.” :Be cats and rabbits. Knew an
old woman’s cat in England that would often kill
and bring home rabbits big enough for the family
to make a meal of. Now, what was that cat's
idea in bringing them home? Preference for
cooked meat; love of approbation ; payment for
board-and-lodging ; or simply the same cheerful
folly that induces the worker all the world over to
hand over the swag to the idler in return for a
small percentage of same ?

TO THE
Wool Growers of New South Wales
and Queensland.
The undersigned members of the Sydney Wool-selling Brokers’ Association wish to announce that,
m order to arrive at a uniform system of charges, they have decided to fall into line, and to adopt the
following Scale of Charges on all Wools sold by them in the Sydney market, viz., a
consolidated charge to the grower of 3 per cent. Commission only
on all lots, large and small.
_ To make the position clear, we intend to adhere to the system of charging the buyer for storage,
delivery, repacking, insurance after sale, etc., |d. per lb. on all wools realising 3fd. per lb. and over,'
and Is. 6d. per bale on all sold under that figure.
It will be observed that this is, in two important particulars, a modified system as compared with
that formerly existing. In the first place the commission charge will for the future be uniform to large
and small growers alike, instead of two separate rates of 3 and 3J per cent.; and in the next the delivery
charge to buyers on wools realising under 3fd. is reduced to a fixed rate of Is. 6d. per bale.
It may be pointed out here that in order to facilitate buyers’ arrangements in making shipping
engagements and securing the best terms for freight, we agree to allow them 14 days in which to pay
for and take delivery of their purchases. But in all cases, whether the wool be paid for or not we pay
over proceeds to owners within seven days from date of sale. ’
Australasian Mortgage & Agency Co., T.iwitgfl

Dalgety & Co., Limited.

The Norton-Meagher case in Sydney Police
Courtis a new departure in the administration of
justice. Most of the time the intelligent Court
sn’t even professing to investigate the whip
and pistol charges, but sits helplessly by, doubled
up in its chair, while the two defendants Billings-
gate each other, and tell fish-yarns about each
other. Some samples, reported in Norton’s own
paper, Truth
Mr. Norton : He said here in open court that an order
was made against me in an affiliation-case, and he called
out the name of the girl. Now I have Mr. Levien here to
prove that Meagher is a deliberate liar.
Mr. Meagher : I never said an order was made.
Norton : You’re a damnable liar, you did, and it ap-
peared in the newspaper reports, too.
Meagher : I did not say an ord e.f was made.
Norton : You Dean perjurer and liar, you did say so.
It can be proved by everyone who was in court. All the
reporters heard you say so, and you repeated it over and
over again.
Meagher : You’re a liar too.
Norton : I have Mr. Levien here to prove that you are a
liar in this matter, and also to prove that I was not the
sinner against the girl, but, on the contrary, I was the
man who stood by the girl in her trouble, t hough I was
wrongfully accused.
Meagher : I will deal with you presently ; I have some-
thing here (waving a sheet of foolscap), I’il show you up
you hound.
Norton : You skunk ! you show me up.
Meagher : You’re a skunk ; you’re just as diseased in
body as you are in mind.
Norton : Here’s Mr. Levien ready to state the truth and
bowl you out in more damnable lies, you triple-tongued
Mr. Meagher: I’ve something here (holding up the
foolscap) for you, you skunk, you scaly, scrofulous bit of
carrion ; you can’t grow eyebrows, you diseased creature.
Norton : .Ha 1 ha ! ! ha !! ! Look at the Dean perjurer
and assassin, the triple-tongued liar ; look at his re-

After quite 10 minutes’ struggling with the excited
crowd, the police succeeded in clearing the court.
I was quite new on the station,.
Active, young and fair,
With a fair man’s fatal weakness
For women with ebon hair.
I tried this kind—well, in dozens.
As men will often do,
Then I struck a half-caste native
The station-hands called Loo.
Her sire was a pure-bred darkie,
Tall and fleet and slim ;
Her dam was an Irish “ lady,”
Rightly annexed to him.
The girl was the strangest mixture
That ever a white man knew,
This black-haired, lithesome native
The Darling lads called Loo.
One day I had met her standing
Out from the western town,
Well-dressed in the plainest fashion,
Her black hair trailing down ;
Then I was gone in an instant,
Her black eyes pierced me through,
And a fair man’s fatal weakness
Had drawn me unto Loo.
There’s a neat small hut now standing
Just on the river’s bend,
And the quiet, handsome native
Is mother, wife and friend,
And the man who speaks of “ niggers’*
I’ll riddle him fairly through,
For a woman’s heart beats under
The clear dark skin of Loo.

Story of a Doctor’s Daughter.
A SUFFERER FOR FIVE YEARS.
FOUR DOCTORS FAIL TO CURE HER.
VICTORY FOR BILE BEANS.
Cases come before the notice of the public nearly
every day, wherein doctors are at an entire loss to
afford relief to some poor sufferer. So frequent
have such oases become that a great number of
X
m
people have
altogether
lost faith in
the medical
profession.
Miss Mary
Mure, of 345
Liverpool-
street, Dar-
linghurst,
Sydney, has a
story to tell
i that aptly
illustrates the
above asser-
tion. T h is
young lady is
the daughter
of the late Dr.
Alexander
Mure, of Wel-
lington, N.Z.,
a man who
was highly
esteemed, both personally and professionally.
Miss Mure has lived in Sydney about 11 months,
having come here from New Zealand. It was in
the land of the Maori, her birthplace, that her
sufferings first commenced. “Five years ago,”
began Miss Mure, on being asked to give an ac-
count of her illness, “ I commenced to suffer se-
verely from that tired feeling we hear so much
about. I would always feel languid, and did not
seem to have any energy or ambition. About
the same time I was assailed by severe head-
aches that were most distressing, causing
me great annoyance and inconvenience, and
intense suffering. In fact, I had never known
before that a headache could be so severe.
Although I felt very ill indeed I concluded that
the attack was only a temporary affair, and would
soon be over. I saw a doctor, who said I had
liver complaint. Far, however, from getting over
it in a short time, as I expected, I continued to
get worse and worse; my appetite disappeared
completely ; I wasn’t able to eat anything except
rice water and rolled oats. In the morning when
I got up I could not partake of any breakfast at
all. My sleep, too, was very poor and unrefresh-
ing. I would toss about the greater part of the
night, and arise in the morning unrefreshed, and
heavy headed. A feeling of despondency took
possession of me, and I was always in a great
state of discontent, nervous anxiety and suspicion
of all my surroundings. I continued to grow worse
and worse ; my sight became affected, and I could
not read ordinary newspaper type; in fact,
I could hardly use my eyes at all. I now became
quite alarmed at my condition, and consulted
another doctor. Since that time I have consulted
no less than four doctors, all in New Zealand, by
the way, and they have prescribed for me many
different medicines, all of which, however, abso-
lutely failed to give me any relief. I got very
white in the
£
vm?/,
v
face,(and I had
no color what-
ever, and the
smell of any-
on e smoking
near me would
nauseate me
exceedingly,
and cause vio-
lent retching.
N ow,” con-
tinued Miss
Mure, after a
short pause,
“ I have never
had much faith
in patent
medicines,
nevertheless I
didat this time
try several
much-advertised nostrums, but they, like the doc-
tors’ prescriptions, did me no good. I may also
tell you that I had plenty of change dur-
ing that time, as I lived in four dif-
ferent parts of New Zealand, and spent some
little time in Fiji, therefore you can see that
change of air that doctors so often recommend for
cases such as mine did not benefit me at all. Well,
about 11 months ago X came to Sydney, still in a
very frail state, as I had lost a lot of weight and
was quite thin. I had almost despaired of ever
getting well again, so bad had my condition be-
come. I was now a martyr to constipation in ad.

February a lady friend recommended Bile Beans

Constipation.
Everyone suffers at some time or other from this
most distressing and rather dangerous ailment. It
is an irregular and weak action of the bowels, and
in extreme cases the bowels may not act for weeks.
In a case of this sort, the complaint is very dan-
gerous, and a proper remedy should be applied
immediately. Women suffer more from it than
men, and the frequent headaches suffered by this
sex can nearly always trace their origin to consti-
pation. It is caused in most cases by carelessness
and neglect, but in some cases it is brought about
by a disordered condition of the liver. It is,
nevertheless, a fact that, where regularity of habit
is practised, constipation is seldom found. The
effects of constipation on the system are many and
varied, among the most common of which may be
mentioned piles, sick-headaehes, debility, sour
stomach, humors, and intermittent fevers. Regu-
larity is the main thing to be observed in cases of
constipation, and, in conjunction with a short
course of Bile Beans, will prove very effective.
The early morning, immediately after breakfast,
is the best time to make a trial, which, if unsuc-
cessful, should nevertheless be persevered iff the
next day, and so on, until the case is cured.
Bile Beans for Biliousness act on the liver, en-
abling that organ to furnish the natural cathartic
for the bowels. They are gentle in their action,,
thus avoiding the very harmful results brought,
about by most laxative remedies of to-day, which
act in a violent manner.
The soundest stomach, lungs, and heart will,
falter in their work the moment the liver goes;
wrong.

W. B. Dailey, Fane de Salis,- and John
Haynes have each made serious attempts by Par-
liamentary Bills to abolish capital punishment in
N.S.W. Dailey gave up in despair, de Salis was
jockeyed on to a side-track and never returned,
but John hopefully pushes on, and is likely to-at
least reduce the number of capital offences. .
Several N.S W. Oppositionists were down
with measles when the censure vote drew near,
and the Govt, refused to pair. After many un-
successful attempts to secure a pair for Col.
Mackay, the Opposition was giving up hope
when “ Majah ” Neild came to the rescue,.
Despite Govt, entreaties, the gallant warrior re-
fused to see a brother “officah” left out, paired
with him, and went home to bed.
“P.”: What’s the matter with labor
covenants for pastoral leases? Well, it has
been discussed often. But some of the least-
improved and, from a national point of view,
least valuable squattages employ most men—
at any rate, more than well-improved runs
which shear far more sheep. The improve-
ments save direct lalxw, but furnish vastly
more indirect labor.

Adversity makes strange bedfellows. But
who could have foretold that Sydney D.T., which,
in ’92, gleefully recorded the fact that the ruler
with which bank-manager Cotton assailed Sleath
was on view at the Hill, mounted in silver, would,
in ’9B, be straining its reporters’ ears to catch and
print every inane interjection of that extremely
dull person !
Estimated that in Sept. Westralia lost
967 people by excess of emigration over immigra-
tion, and that the population decreased between
GOO and 700. As a rule, even in these bad times,
the W.A. population has kept on growing, bub
the movement westward now is only the feeble
tail-end of the rush that used to be, and even the
tail is fast disappearing.
S.A. Police Commissioner’s report reveals
the fact that the Northern Territory, of 523,0005 q.
miles, is kept in order by 16 white policemen. At
two stations two officers live together, but at the
other 12 the lone policeman goes slowly mad with
the solitude “ all on his own,” and sees visions in
his spare time. A partially-looney policeman
with a beat of 30,0005 q. miles must be a fearsome
person to be run in by.
J. R. Dickson makes the third Premier
Queensland has seen this year. An acting-Premier
also has left the scene of his labors, and port-
folios generally are in a state of flux. Unfortunate
Q. Govt. ! It hasn’t any programme ;it hasn’t
any principles; it can’t keep its promises, its
pledges, or its Premiers —it can only keep its eye
anxiously on the ’99 elections and howl frantically,
“ Beware of Socialism in our time ! ”
,Wrixon’s hazy scheme for ‘ ‘ reforming ”
Vic. Leg. Council proved to be based on his
anxiety to secure justice to minorities by means of
a proportional voting system, and he would like to
enlarge the “basis and scope” of the Council
upon proportional voting lines. Rats ! The
House of Vic. Landlords is .an ..unequalled palace
of justice for minorities. It represents as much
minority to the square yard as any parliamentary
institution on earth, and Wrixon,- by his ovvn
showing, ought to be quite satisfied with it.
Hence the honest scorn of the other fellows when
Henry W. came along with his suggestion.
Item from the West Australian .
Let the Premier announce to-morrow that the Coolgar-
die Water Scheme is finally abandoned, and those who
care to remain in Western Australia will have a good
chance of sounding the depths to which it is possible for
our colony to sink.
Quite true. It isn’t so much a question of the
workableness or unworkableness of the scheme, but
Westralia must borrow furiously to keep even a
semblance of its big boom going. And it must
have some scheme on hand—even if it is only a
scheme lor tying pink paper-frills round all the
jarrah-trees in W.A. and branding and registering
the emus for future identification—to furnish an
excuse for borrowing the cash that keeps the boom
going.
LAY TO YOUR BOOK.
BARNE Y: “What’s the trouble, Mossy; have
they struck yer on a double? ”
MOSSY : “No, but the bloke in the shop has
just beat me for half-a-quid. He sells me a
blooming, great book on ‘ The Races of Aus-
tralia,’ and blime if there’s a word about
Ranwick or Corfield in it from end to end.”
The Melbourne papers are full to the brim
just now with curse-letters about the muddlement
of the Vie. postal department. If one-quarter of
them are true (and most of them give full particu-
lars and dates, and are verified by the names and
addresses of complainants), P.-M.-G. Duffy must
be the most utterly incapable ass of the seven
governments of Australasia. Yet though nobody
ever takes Duffy seriously, or regards him as
having any administrative ability, or as any-
thing else than an amusing kind of Handy Andy,
his capacityfor getting into Ministries is incredible.
He was Service’s Minister of Lands in 1880 ;
P.-M.-G. under Munro in ’OO-’92 ; Attorney-
General and P.-M.-G. under Shiels , and P.-M.-G.
again under Turner. Trenwith ought to fill the
billet admirably, but the legend that a job must
be found for Duffy stands in the way.
Several of the British West Indies are
now agitating for annexation by the U.S. They
want free admission for their sugar to the States
and Protection against the outside world, both of
which annexation would give them. As part of
the British empire their sugar is shut out of the
U.S. by the tariff, and though it has free access to
Britain there is no money in that. The French
and German beet kills the nigger-grown cane pro-
duct, and the West Indies are fast going back to
jungle and savagery.

No less than four N.S.W. Opposition
members are having their seats challenged before
the Elections and Qualifications Committee by the
bowled-out Government candidates, generally on
vague charges that the member’s committee
“shouted” for a dry-elector. But the wildest
joke of the lot is the case of the man who is charged
with intimidation before and after election. In-
timidation after the election presumably means
threats of the damage the member wouid have
done if he hadn’t got in, which is about as rele-
vant as the black eye that he wishes he had given
the other fellow in’ a previous state of existence.
The most graceful act of Lyne’s career as
leader of N. S. VV. Opposition, was his surrender of
the billet last week ,to Barton. As a Federal
leader Barton is more earnest than all the others
put together, though as a purely party boss he
will probably be a disappointment. Efe doesn’t
reach out after “hoffis” with sufficient fury.
When he came into the House he was apparently
quite -Cvilling to drop all personal and party ques-
tions. and get along with the Federal resolutions.
And though he is leader, and though the griev-
ance re Potato Young’s doings at Hastings-and-
Macleay' election, was all along pre-eminently
Barton’s own grievance, yet he wasn’t sufficiently
interested to take the motion of censure out of
Lyne’s hands and tackle it himself.
The confident announcement made by Pre-
mier Reid that Barton couldn’t sit in N.S.W.
Assembly because of his position as arbitrator in
the M‘Sharry case, has dropped so verv dead that
nobody has even met its skull rolling down the
street. Did Premier Reid deliberately, and with-
out protest, allow a disqualified man to take his
illegal seat, and even to lead the Opposition ? Or
has he found—since he became a Q.C.—that his
law was cronk and his precedents were ignorance?
Or did he know all along that Barton 'wasn’t dis-
qualified, and did he say what he knew was
not the truth in order to try and keep the
most vigorous Federationist of the lot out of the
Parliament which was summoned especially to
deal with Federation ? These are the only three
alternatives The Bulletin knows of,-and they all
leave Premier -Reid looking a tolerably mean
politician.
They have a strange way of doing things
in the Land of Lots of Sleep. Aikenhead, the
Govt, supporter from Devonport, was lately un-
seated on a question of “ corrupt practices ” on the
petition of defeated candidate M‘Call. Ac-
cording to law he can’t stand there again for two
years. But the Govt, member for the adjoining
electorate of E. Devon resigns his seat to fight
M‘Call at the new election. If he gets in for
Devonport then the gentleman just fired out is to
have a run for his seat at E. Devon ; in fact, the
two are to exchange electorates. And if the new
Govt, candidate doesn’t get in, the slipshod Elec-
toral Act is so constructed that he can then with-
draw his resignation of his present seat and stay
where he is. Meanwhile, “leading citizens’’ are
raising an enthusiastic subscription and much
whitewash for the man unseated in a painful mat-
ter of “ corrupt practices.”
Victorian Police-pension list is in striking
contrast to that of N.S.W. It includes 4 inspect-
ing superintendents at £390 each, 17 superin-
tendents at =6275, 3 inspectors at =6240 and about
315 sergeants, senior constables and constables at
amounts ranging from .£l5O to =650 eac-h. The
cost is <£40,000 a year, towards which the police
contribute .£5500, made up of a 21, per cent-
deduction from their pay. The oldest pensioner
is ex-Paymaster Colonel’Mair, who is 97, has been
drawing a pension of =6400 for 37 years, and is
still going strong. Among the special pensions
are a first-class sergeant’s full pay (=6191125. 6d.)
to the widow of Sergt. Kennedy, shot by the
Kellys; =652 to ex-Const. MTntyre, whose health
was shattered by exposure in pursuit of the gang ;
.£52 to a civilian injured at Glenrowan ; =6150 to
ex-Const. Bracken, injured at Glenrowan; and
.£lOO a year to ex-Const. Vizard, injured by
larrikins.
The way in which Q. Labor candidates are
selected hardly justifies their constantly-asserted
claim to precedence over independent Democrats.
A case in point: Worker Higgs and Frank Mac-
donald, M.L. A., were last week “ unanimously "
chosen .to contest the Valley electorate in the
Labor interest. The meeting of the Workers’
Political Organisation, giving them their passport,
consisted of six persons. Yet Higgs and Mac-
donald would probably stigmatise any independent
anti-Govt. candidate as an interloper.
The Bill abolishing the creditor’s
power to attach his debtor’s wages was
rejected, as a matter of course, by Vic-
toria’s House of Rich Landlords. Only
one man spoke—old Frederick Sheppard
Grimwade—and he said two things. One
was that he had “never heard of gar-
nishee orders except in connection with
the civil service,” which suggests a truly
pitiable state of ignorance on Mr. Grim-
wade’s part. The other was that the idea
of abolishing garnishee orders came from
Maoriland, and that the House of Rich
Landlords shouldn’t be a servile copy of
any other country’s legislation—it should
be bright and sparkling and original, and
stand on its own gouty feet. So the
House proceeded to be a servile copy of
N.S.W., Queensland, Tasmania, Wes-
traha, certain of the United States and
other places, by sticking to the old sys-
tern. The spectacle of Victoria’s House
of 48 Dodderers trying to be original is very
impressive.
In getting Neild to move for the Com-
mission to enquire into the charges against “ Cul-
vert Young the Reid Govt, just saved its flabby
bacon, as several members of the party would have
at least been found missing on the censure vote had
they not been provided with an excuse in the
shape of said Commission. As it was. they were
mostly satisfied to give a silent vote, “Sammy”
Moore being about the only one to “ wag his jaw,”
which he did in true yes-no fashion by talking in
favor of the motion and then voting against it.
The selection of J. R. Dickson as deceased
Byrnes’ successor has filled Q. Oppositionists with
a great gladness. Dickson, who is old, dull and
feeble, and devoid bf inspiration, is the very aiiti-
thesis of his predecessor. Byrnes was full of
intellectual resource, his speech was emphatic and
hopeful, he could illuminate a subject with humor,
epigram, historical analogy ; he gave the impres-
sion of a strong man full of possibilities. Dick-
son is funereal and threadbare at all times. When
he tries to joke you think someone is rubbing a
piece of ice up and down your spine. He was
born to sell Bibles to cannibal tribes and preach
statistics to the cheerful raven. What he intends
to do as Premier is not apparent, but as he was
the Treasurer who floated thecelebrated £10,000,000
loan, and believes in immigration of all sorts, a
vigorous policy of borrowing, boom arid reckless
expenditure may be looked 'for. Opposition-leader
Glassey could hardly have wished tor a better
antagonist.
The untimely and lamentable ' death .of
Q. Premier Byrnes is a terrible blow to the R.C.
community in the North. Byrnes, before every-
thing else, was a “good ’" Roman. Since Macros-
san s decease lie has watched over the interests of
the faithful with a zeal that was often blind and
always sleepless. A small coterie' of lawyer co-
religionists will miss him more than anyone.
Barristers of pronounced ; anti - Govt, opinions
hadn’t Buckley’s show of getting a crumb from
the Crown Law Office table. And the partiality
of the ex-Attorney-General led’ to a similar boy-
cott among leading solicitor firms} who are more
or less related to the Govt. The severity of this
ostracism drcve barrister Powers from' Brisbane
bar to a third-rate bush township, and Drake’s
chambers are merely the rendezvous for political
sympathisers. There being now no strong man
in Q. to lead and inspire the R.C. faction, that
party, as a united political factor, may be said to
have been interred with Byrnes.
The Dickson regime in Queensland is only
a few days old and it lias already numbered some
queer experiences. Firstly, some 14 members of
the old Govt, party signed a round robin to the
effect that they wouldn’t recognise the oleaginous
J.R.D. as Premier on any account. No sooner
were they partly pacified' than the Labor-party
started a rumpus, calling for a dissolution
and instant appeal to the Country, defying the
Speaker, who was contemptuously called to
his face “a dirty little nigger-driver”—and resist-
ing ihe officers of the House, who were called
upon to expel the recalcitrants. (It was a most
touching sight to behold the dignified Sergeant-
at-Arms and his satellite trying vainly to expel
a small legislator, upon whom a burly fellow-
Dexnocrat was sitting, by way of showing his
sympathy.) . . . The Governmental rats are
getting ready to desert the sinking ship; the
murmurs of the discontented are heard all around,
and the famous coalition bids fair to collapse like
a house of cards.
“ Queenslander’’ to The Bulletin :
Why do you say “ Jackson violates Q. Labor-platform
by voting for private railway hills’’-and not mention
that Hoolan and Sim voted with Jackson on the Ch illagoe
bill ? Also, do you know that in ’94 the whole of the Q.
Labor members, elected under the same platform as the
present party in the Assembly, allowed a private railway
to go through Parliament wit hout a word of protest ? The
Swanbank railway and the Chillagoe are very different in
detail no doubt, but. the resemblance is sufficient to show
that the Labor-party in ’94 did not draw a hard and fast
line in regard to its plank—“ State ownership and con-
struction of all railways.” Since the recent Labor Con-
vention in Brisbane, the “ railway plank” has been
defined to mean that no imaginable private railway can
be supported by the party ; therefore, in the future, any
member voting for a private line can justly be charged
with violating his programme. The “railway plank" in
the old programme was inserted mainly as a protest
against land-grant, railways; and as the Chillagoe line
could not be regarded in any fair sense as a land-grant
railway, Iloolan, Sim and Jackson considered, after the
’94 precedent, that they were not violating any election
pledges. Also, the Labor-party never carried any resolu-
tion making the vote on the Chillagoe line w party-
question, though the matter was brought up in caucus.

TASMANIAN AGENT :
J. W. HUDSON, Zeehan.
This company is formed to acquire four blocks of 50 acres each, numbered 3199, 3200, 3201, and 3202 over 93M,
situated in the North Pieman district, West Coast of Tasmania. The property is distant 26 miles from Mt, Bischoff,
and 22 miles from Zeehan, 15 miles of which is covered b.y rail. The Emu Bay Railway, now under course of construc-
tion, runs at the foot of the hill. The last progress report furnished by the secretary of the company states that the
bulk of the formation will be completed through to some point beyond the 25 peg about the end of next month, to
which point the rails will be quickly laid. It is obvious, therefore, that there will be direct railway communication
with Emu Bay before the end of the year ; in the meantime it is connected by track with the Pieman crossing, thence
to the township of Rosebery, and so on to the terminus of the Government light railway at Zeehan.
The sections in question rise abruptly from the bed of the Pieman River, and are surmounted by two well-marked
peaks, the higher peak being 1175 ft. above the level of the river. The crown of both peaks is composed of a silicious
mineralised formation, outcropping very boldly, and impregnated throughout by iron pyrites and iron oxide, the
matrix being a quartzose schist, and on the ridge or saddle connecting the peaks, wherever the superficial debris has
been cleared away, the same mineralised formation is exposed.
A considerable amount of work has been carried on, the ground being well cleared and suitable tracks cut. Ex-
ploration work to date may be summarised as follows About 150 ft. below the crown of the lesser peak referred to a
trench has been cut across the capping for 200 ft. in length without defining its limits, the ore underfoot showing con-
sistent improvement throughout, although copper, visible to the eye, only appears towards the end of the trench —
the crosscut, which terminates in a breast of ore some 6ft. or 7ft. high, has evidently not nearly yet traversed the
whole width of the formation - the cropping still continues, and about 100 ft. ahead of the trench a prospecting adit
has been begun and advanced 12ft., the face being in solid pyrites. Still further ahead, about 130 ft., trenching on the
lode capping shows pyrites, though not so freely as before. Adding, therefore, these distances together, the several
points being approximately on line with each other, and, as nearly as can be judged, at right angles to the trend of
the formation, A TOTAL WIDTH OF 430 FT. IS SHOWN WITHOUT DISCLOSING CONTACT WITH STRATA ON
EITHER SIDE—THE WHOLE WIDTH OF THE CAPPING APPEARING ON THE SURFACE AMOUNTING TO
ABOUT 860 FT.
To prove the formation exposed in the trenches at a depth, a tunnel has been started about 200 ft. below the
crown of the second peak referred to, and after passing 60ft. through mineralised country rock, quartzose schist
was encountered, impregnated with iron pyrites, and showing here and there splashes of copper pyrites, through
which it has been advanced 34ft. to date, the rock becoming harder, denser, and more mineralised with every foot
driven. It is expected that the main body of the formation will be encountered, as far as can be judged, within the
next 50ft.
Considering the tremendous mass of pyritic ore affected, it is obvious that only a very low percentage of valuable
metallic contents will represent enormous wealth, more especially considering the unusually great natural advan-
tages commanded for economical mining and transit of ore. An adequate water supply and pressure can probably
be obtained from some of the numerous rapid flowing tributaries of the Pieman to provide all the mechanical power
required.
Fancy assays from picked samples are not given, as any deduction based thereon is almost invariably erroneous
and misleading. Suffice it to sa.v, that rich copper ore can be obtained on the surface, and that a sample of iron
pyrites, taken at haphazard in the tunnel, and showing no trace of copper, contained 7dwt. gold.
A considerable sum of money bas already been expended on the property; four substantial wooden huts, a
blacksmith’s smithy, and a charcoal house are erected ; the mine is fully equipped with tools, etc. ; and there are
nearly one hundred bags of charcoal available.

iledical Board. Founder of and Chief Surgeon to

Sixteen Years Hon. Surgeon to the Sydney
Hospital. Dr. Tarrant has, during his recent
world-wide travels, discovered a
New Method of Healing.
This mode of treatment is absolutely free from
Mercury, and other.deleterious, poisonous, or en-
slaving ingredients, may be stopped at any time,
need not be continued after a cure is effected, and
is unlike any other formulae formerly in use, and is
known to Dr. Tarrant alone. Hypodermic medica-
tion is limited to a few remedies, and is applicable
to a few diseases only. The administration of
drugs through the stomach is also limited by the
danger of injur3' to the organs of digestion.
This New Method of
Treatment
on the contrary, promotes digestion, clears the
Liver and Kidneys, thus paving the way to Perfect
health.
Many sufferers fear lest others should know they
are afflicted. I may assure those who confide their
secret to me that there is not the slightest risk of
exposure, and all letters are regarded as positively
confidential, and are destroyed by fire as soon
as answered, and all appliances are sent securely
sealed.
Dr. Tarrant’s Knowledge of
Special Complaints
from any cause arising', whether hereditary or
acquired, was.gained by careful study and observa-
tion in the principal Hospitals of the world. Hr. TARRANT makes it a special point to eradicate thoroughly
and pleasantly every trace of disease from the system, ensuring not only permanent restoration to health,
but immunity to offspring from hereditary taint.
No man will accomplish a noble purpose, become eminent, or achieve greatness, whose nervous
system is deranged.
JSTo Man or Woman
should marry without first assuring themselves that they are in perfect health. If you are unable to under-
stand your malady, or if it is vour intention to marry, send for Dr. TARRRANT’S exhaustive work,
“FUNCTIONAL DERANGEMENTS.”
Price, Sixpence in Stamps, which contains 164 pages, blocked in gold, including numerous Prescriptions,
Illustrations and Pictures ; it will enable you to understand and describe your case. Sufferers from Special
Complaints should consult Dr. TARRANT, who has by profound research thoroughly investigated their
orisrin, traced deadly, sources and insidious dissemination, and ascertained the surest and safest method
of eradication. •
Dr. TARRANT is frequently consulted by patients who have been erroneously treated for Indigestion,
Liver Complaint, Bright’s Disease, Consumption, and other complaints, whose cases prove to he over-strained
nerves. Many who have thus been experimented upon, fear to consult even a physician so well-known and
universally respected as- Dr. Tarrant, who has cured thousands, who have been thus mistreated, by advice
alone. Therefore, if you are a sufferer, write to the doctor, who will, on receipt of your letter, reply cour-
teously and confidentially, naming your malady and cure. You should consult Dr. TARRANT if
you experience
Dangerous Symptoms,
Foul Breath, Indigestion, Palpitation, Blushing, Shortness of Breath, Impaired Memory, Weak, Sore, Sunken,
or Dark-circled Eyes ; Sallow or Pimply Skin, Dullness of Intellect, Piles, Constipation, or Relaxation of the
Bowels ; Bad Taste, Wind, Desire for Solitude or Drink, Bashfulness, Weak Back, Bad Feet, Oozing of Saliva
from the mouth during sleep, Neuralgia, Hacking Cough, Giddiness. Fear of Insanity or Suicide, Inclination
to Throw Yourself Down when over water. Inability to keep your Thoughts off Impure Subjects, Dreams,
Twitching of the Eyelids, Numbness, or Prickly Sensation, Timidity, Fear of Impending Danger, &c. Dr.
TARRANT makes
Absolutely 3STo Charge
for Consultation ; but as One Pound, is all that is necessary to send for the treatment of almost any com-

SPORTING NOTIONS.
Accepting the Caulfield Stakes as an honest
race, which it probably was, this season’s three-year-olds
are a poor lot. George Frederick and Picture were
backed, but neither of them made a ghost of a show
against The Chief and Battalion. For that matter, the
‘ representative" youngsters never got near to
Wait-a-Bit, who led the field from the start to the dis-
tance-post. The Ballarat horse is the same old YVait-a-
Bit as of yore, seemingly galloping like a good one until
he is collared and then shutting up like a coward. He
ought to win the Caulfield Cup if started, but in the pre-
sent writer s opinion he hasn’t the grit to do it. Such
cocktail steeds are mere vanity and vexation of soul.
, -Bobadil won the Caulfield Guineas all the way,
the time being slow and the victory not of a Carbineio
character. Nothing “ Bobbie ’’-dazzling about it. He’s

The Tut Ryan—Peter Felix fight for the Aus-
tralian championship last Saturday- night in Melbourne
was a sorry show. The darkey, towering 4in. over his
opponent, was in perfect form, and darted about the ring
with his lanky figure glistening in away that made the
r.u £®, S 1 i ve, 'r. to think of be ing chased along the banks
of the Nile by Peter Felix with a shovel-headed spear.
His inordinately long arms swayed in front of him, an
impenetrable barrier, and his left hand shot backwards
and forwards all over the place like a snake’s head poised
for striking. Altogether, Peter was a very awkward cus-
tomer for Ryan, who had a thick flap of flesh hanging
over his belt, and had no muscles apparent under his
copious avoirdupois. Nevertheless, he got home re-
peatedly on the nigger’s skull, but with as little
effect as he would have had on a stone wall.
During the fight, Ryan s hits must have been about seven
or eight to Felix s one. The darkie is a splendid defender,
but he hits seldom, only with, his leit, and with no force.
Ryan in condition, and with the acumen to go for his
body and leave his head alone, would out him in euriit
rounds. As it was, Felix finished as fresh as paint, while
the white was completely done up; and, taking every-
thing into consideration, the referee’s decision of a draw
was as good as could be found.
The fight of the evening or of the year was over
before the champions entered the ring. It vvas an unob-
trusive, unadvertised scrap between two voum>- fellows
named Chappie Williams and Clark. They are li.-hfc-
weights, and unknown at that, but they provided "the
best null that Melb. has seen for a long time. It was
“ willing ’ from start to finish, and, after seven rounds of
real, ding-dong, blood-and-fire slashing, Clark had to
throw in the towel through bad hands, on the advice of
surgeon O Hara, the massive medico, vvhp was picking ud
points for his next go with Purves, and Billy Maloney
W ;RA., vyho was getting a wrinkle to deal satisfactorily
with the lengthy Deakin. J

{Sydney) waterman, holds the record for the hardest Aus-

Bay to catch a vessel bound for Melbourne, but arrived
m time to see her running out between the Heads Thev
rowed on, in the hopes of cutting her off, but it was no

‘‘Zoo”: A hardened liar once told me how he
and a friend nearly made their day’s raoin«--expenses o„f
of a “ three-card man.” After watching mugs
down with regularity, they agreed (privately) that the
art of “spotting” the right card consisted in resisting
the impulse to feel that it was spotted. So they con-
federated that each should select the card which he most
felt was not the nyht one. The “ system n worked nd
mirably at first, and, by carefully disregarding the anm-
rent card m favor of a rank outsider, one or other of
them “ got onto the jack each time. The “ professor ”
wus riled and then things altered. Both partners guessed
wrongly (and Doth invariably chose different cards) six
times running, and another “ system ” earned revilement
They tumbled to it afterwards. It was very simple. The
manipulator, finding he was «-ettin«- spotted too often,
sleeved the Jack each time, and it never was on the
ooard at all! All these men are ciever conjurors.

The Laundryinen’s Association of Sydney
lately handed in this ungrammatical slab* of
bitter truth to the local Water and Sewerage
Board : k
The giving of water free to so-called charitable institu-
tions was condemned, as being most unjust to legitimate
laundries, burdened by the operation of some of the
clauses of the Factory Act, rent, taxes, wages, and pay-
intf for all the water they use, and now met by the grow-
ing- competition of Chinese, and what is falsely named
charitable laundries. The latter is run on free subscribed
capital, free labor, and many other free perquisites,
water being one ; employing canvass rs who appeal to
the bigotry, charity, and cupidity of the public for work.
Many of these establishments are fully-equipped steam
laundries, having all the most modern machinery in use,
and employing a very large number of hands (unpaid)’
and are not interfered with by the Factory Act (as if the
machine that could injure a worker in an ordinary laun-
dry would be powerless to do the same to a worker
in a charitable laundry). The St. Magdalene steam
laundry at Temps is in every way a fully-
equipped mercantile establishment. It puts in ten-
ders for all kinds of work, and for various reasons
obtains the work; one reason especially, because the
price is lower than what any legitimate ‘laundry payiiv
its way could do the work for.' It might not he out of
place to state here that if those charitable institutions
progress at the same rate for the next few years as they
have done in the past, in competing and taking away the
opportunity to earn a living by respectable women it
follows that those women will of necessity gravitate
towards such places where they are converted into slaves
for the benefit of the managers of those institutions and
some of the public who expect a shilling invested in such
charity will return 25 per cent, in low price. Water and
washmg are closely connected-so is water and beer.
What would past and present brewers think if a charitable
institution commenced to brew tanglefoot and obtain
water free, so as to enable them to sell at a cheap rate?
They would soon stop their subscription.
It might be further added that the hardfaced
canvasser gets a good commission out of the
subscriptions in aid of “charity” laundries.
There is money in the business for a thick-
skinned female who must either he paid to leave
or be thrown out (as a rule, females are em-
ployed because there is more reluctance about
throwing them out than about shooting the
male person down the front step, and, besides,
they have hysterics outside afterwards which
the male doesn’t). Also, there must be
money iu the business for a good many people
besides the canvasser. When one considers the
free water supply, the immensely long hours
exacteci, the free labor (the forgiven Flossie
who perishes over the wash-tub merely gets
food and soul-repairs), and the subscriptions
even the very low prices at which the work is
done must leave a good margin for somebody.
Alfred Deakin’s proposal to introduce “ele-
mentary unsectarian religious instruction ” into
Vic. State Schools claims to he “simply
Christian. It is based on the theory that u it
is possible to get passages of Scripture for
reading, which would suit all creeds with the
minor exception of the Jews, avoiding doctrinal
difficulties in the choice of the passages.” Now,
as a matter of fact, the most popular Biblical
recitation in the so-called Christian world or
the Protestant portion of it at any rate—suits
the Jews down to the ground. “ The Lord is
my shepherd,” &c., &c.” But the “ Lord ”in
that case lias no Jewish connection with the
author of “ The Lord’s Prayer.” Where does
Scripture, as Deakin understands the word be-
gin and end, and how much of the Scripture
that isn t strictly Jewish could be instilled into
the infant Protestant mind with the full ap-
proval of Roman Catholicism ? In any State
edueationa dealings with Scripture which is
not the Bible, the Virgin Mary difficulty must
inevitably crop up. The Jewish religion ig-
nores Christ, the various brands of Protestant
religion worship Christ “ on His own ” (not to
speak irreverently), while the R.C. religion
associates Him with His mother. Seeing that
the Lord of David is the only Lord as to whose
spiritual standing they are all of one mind, the
selection of Scripture passages to suit all creeds
might very well be left to a special Jewish
committee.


Surveyor Russell, who actually laid out
and after whom Russell-street is named,
languishes as a very necessitous nonagenarian, in
Richmond suburb. Hoddle, who was to Russell
as Vespucci to Columbus, died rich, and there was
a Monahan row over his leavings. The same mis-
take as over Russell and Hoddle is made with
regard to Fawknerand Batman, in calling 1 >atman
the founder of Melb. Fawkner built the first
house.
Owing to a technicality, Premier Kingston,
Q.C., who is counsel for Harrold Bros, in the big
S.A. insolvency case, will probably get no fees
whatever for nearly two years’ work. Through a
flaw in the insolvency law, there is no provision
for the payment of an insolvent’s counsel’s fees,
although such payments have always been made
in the past. Although the Judges, on appeal,
have decided that he is entitled to nothing, King-
ston says that he will stick to his clients to the
bitter end.
Line upon line. A common remark on entering
a well-stocked ironmongery store is—“ What a number
of lines you keep.” This is true, but the art in keeping
a stock is just to keep the very line your customer wants
at the price he wants. In McLEAN BROS. & RIGG
LTD. Melbourne and Sydney warehouses you will find
the very article you are looking for. Implements for the
farm, ironmongery for the house, guns for the sportsman,
fishing tackle for the fisherman at, eye-opener prices. If
