Some gems of thought and fancy from the
debate in Victoria’s House of Rich Landlords, on
Wrixon’s proposed reform scheme :
“ He did not think any objection could be taken to the
personnel of the Council, nor could the desire of the hon.
members to do their duty to their constituents he called
into question.”—Henry Wrixon.
“ In the constitutional struggles of the past the Council
had had the confidence and support of the great majority
of the people. In no case had the Council rejected pro-
posals, except under a sense of justice and right, and of
responsibility to the great majority of the people.”— Nick
Fitzgerald.
“The main body of the people are satisfied.”—Dr.
Embling.
“ What the Council wants is more power.” —Dowling.
Old Nicholas Fitzgerald is the most beautiful
specimen in the above menagerie. A man who
can get on his hind legs and deliberately assert
that the 48 Rich Landlords are all perfect charac-
ters (for that is what it amounts to), and never
used their power from any motive that wasn’t ab-
solutely pure, requires to be instantly poleaxed.
If he believes what he says he is such a pure
though foolish saint that he shouldn’t remain on
this earth any longer to be contaminated whereby
his front seat in Paradise may be endangered.^

There were sniffs from the Tory Murray
Smith rump of Vic. Opposition when laborist
Hancock brought up the question of passing a
resolution regretting the death of Hori Grey.
Premier Turner said it was only usual to pass such
resolutions in re crowned heads. “ But Grey was
worth a dozen crowned beads,” said little Dr.
Maloney, whereupon the Tory rump groaned and
frowned at him. But Hancock stuck to the point
and the motion was passed, though the graceless
way in which Turner took it up spoiled the doing
of it.
W. H. T. Brown, of Tas. Assembly, some
years ago, did the neatest thing on record in the
way of political vengeance. He was Inspector of
Forests, and the Dobson-Henry Govt, decided to
abolish the office and out Brown adrift. As an
old skipper, he wasn't to be thrown overboard
without a struggle with the mutineers. Just then
there was a bye-election for Campbelltown,
Brown shook the Govt, dust from his feet, hied
himself to the midland town, and lodged his
nomination-paper. He was returned, went and
took his seat. There was a row in the House at
the time, and the fate of the Govt, trembled in
the balance. In the decisive division the. Govt,
was beaten by a majority of one. Brown’s vote
squelched them ! They resigned, and Braddon
came in, with Brown sitting right behind.
Nervous sufferers will find it worth their
while to peruse Herr Rassmussen’s advt. on page
27 of this issue.*

Dear Bulletin. —The pill-advertiser is
leaving us no poetry in life —except, of course,
pill verses. From a page of pill advt. in your
valuable paper, I learn that the first duty of a
lovely girl who has made a triumphant debut in
sassiety is to “keep her entire system in a thorough
state of cleanliness ” by means of somebody’s
patent Peaceful Purgers, two to be taken occa-
sionally. The next things will be Liver Lozenges
t°r Lovers, followed by a Honeymoon Aperient,
or Bonnie Pips for Bridal Pairs.—Cold Shivers.
Literature doesn’t exactly flourish in Aus-
tralia, but it isn’t quite what it might be in the
old country. Walter Besant told lately in a
magazine article how a clever girl was employed
by a well-known London firm to write cheap
novelettes. For a story of 30,000 words she re-
ceived 50s. —a penny for every 50 words. Was
not allowed to put a signature to the story “lest
your price might go up.” Some years ago, Mrs.
Besant, of theosophical fame, wrote a scathing
exposure of London literary sweating. Chief
in this respect (listen, ye orthodox) was
the Society for the Propagation of Christian
Knowledge.

Melb. ’Erald quotes from a letter written
by a member of the Vic. rifle-team a while ago :
“ There is really no London evening paper pub-
lished that comes up to the Herald, and all the
members of our team are of the same opinion.”
Which reminds The Bulletin of an old story
about an Australian Eleven young man (a Mel-
bournian) who refused to see anything worth men-
tioning outside of his native city. He travelled
around Hingland in a chronic state of contemp-
tuous criticism until at last the other members
of the team laid themselves out to get at least one
note of admiration from the snorter. They never
succeeded. He broke them up with his criticism
on Reynolds, the famous cornet-player, when they
went in a body to a promenade concert at Covent
Garden Theatre. After Reynolds had played a
simple air in exquisite style, the other boys im-
plored their scornful mate to admit that there was
no one in Melbourne to equal Reynolds as a brass
instrumentalist. To equal him! ” roared the
patriot, “why, there’s a chap plays the cornet at
the corner of Bourke and Russell-streets who
could blow his head off.”

A BaDy Lion I
Did yon ever see one ? It is as playful and
harmless as a kitten. You might have one
with you for weeks and not suspect danger.
But, beware! Some day it will surely
spring, and the fight for life is at hand.
It is just so with a cough. You may carry
one with you for weeks and not think of
danger. But the danger is there. Some day
the fight will be on, and it is a question which
will win, Consumption or You.

against the ‘cleverness,” the “humor,” or "the “eecen-

adjectives? I questioned the originality of the Seare-
oelnan‘ Two or three people have declared

a truthful caricaturist most of the time and a regular
volcano in petticoats always, but have never been near

about anything on earth. “8.E.” almost convinces
me he is a poor player himself. It is characteristic
of the theatrical mind to read abuse into every criticism
that does not go the whole hogshead of thick treacle.
Mrs. Langtry has determined to return to the stage,
and, Williamson and Musgrove are, it is said (writes Syd-
ney D.T.’s London correspondent), offering her fine terms
for a short Australian season. If Mrs. Langtry visits the
Antipodes, you may recognise her talents and her still
considerable good looks, and the ladies will marvel at her
jewels. But the exquisite complexion of 20 years ago,
which was the beauty’s choicest possession, and which
was what really set society raving, has departed never to
return.
A theatrical agent, just returned from “ up North,”
throws a calcium light on what they read in the back-
blox. ‘Struck Blankwich.with the co. at a very good
time, and had ‘ Boeeacio ’ well-billed in advance to open
in. Landlord prophesied a poor house because, as he ex-
plained, the folks thereabouts were pretty straight-laced,
and objected to anything ‘ blue.’ I couldn't make it out;
but sure enough the house was three-parts empty', and
even those who were there (all men) seemed to be in a
state of half-fearful expectancy. Waylaid one when it
was over, and asked him how he liked the show. ‘ Oh,
it ivas all right,’ he said, ‘ but it ain’t much like the booh :
that g pretty blue, that is ! ” Then it hit me. They
thought ‘ Boccaeio ’ was jm operatic version of the
Decameron ! ”
The most unconvincing of all mummeries is the stage-
death. Some recent Hispano-Ameriean war pictures
show' the thing as it really happens. Here, a dead soldier
in a sitting posture, as though resting ; there, the corpse
of one who has crawled along on his hands and knees,
till he has fallen with his forehead pressed to the ground.
On the stage, and in most pictures, people die at full
stretch, on the back. Can’t any mummer invent a pic-
turesque and yet correct method of pegging-out?

Some bacilli multiply by breaking in half
and the halves growing to the size of the original;
and then dittoing ad infinitum. Anybody can
pick the bacillus of anti-federation in one go. It
has split once, and the halves have grown, so that
t’other is like which. So just you watch it, Toby.
That enterprising man, A. J. Riley, is
responsible for the latest addition to the archi-
tectural beauties of Sydney. The new premises in
Pitt-street, near King-street, are called “The
Mutual Stores, ’ and they have been erected in
the wisdom of 35 years’ extensive experience
Nowhere is this better illustrated than in the
lighting of the new house; this excellence alone
will popularise the establishment with ladies. A.
J. Riley, of course, v ill manage the concern, and*
after his recent business trip to England and the

prosperity.
“Melb.” Martha Perks, widow of the es-
teemed Anglican Pastor Perks, demised this week,
aged 84. Perks only made one mistake in a long
career, when he went to England as Chaplain to
the renowned Jacky Tommy Smith, Melbourne’s
Dick Whittington, seven times Mayor. Smith
was to be the first Melb. knight, but either his
vulgar appearance disgusted Her Gracious, in an
interview, or else she had heard about the shady
character of his pub., next the Queen’s Theatre.
Labor-member Price lately told S. A.
Assembly that “ Dr. Cockburn is the greatest man
who ever put his foot on this soil.” “He is the
greatest thinker Australia has ever seen,” agreed
King O’Malley, “Cockburn saw things coming
years before they arrived, but Kingston is like a
man in a train with his back to the engine. He
doesn’t see anything until he has passed it.”
Certainly most of the progressive legislation of the
Kingston Ministry was suggested by the present
Agent-General. But then Cockburn is a dreamer
without strength or vim enough to live up to his
brains.
This is the season of the fat pig and large
pumpkin show; and in the South Australian
farming districts, where every township consist-
ing of a store, a “chapel,” a pub., and a black-
smith’s shop, has its agricultural society, there
used to be great rivalry between the chapel and
the pub. in the matter of providing “ the lunch ”
—a most important part of the proceedings. But
since women’s franchise came along, the sisters
have won all along the line ; and now “ the ladies
of the Primitive Methodist body provided an ex-
cellent luncheon in Mr. Binks’s barn, kindly lent
for the occasion,” is a common portion of the
secretary-supplied report.
A Sydney cleric to The Bulletin
Re your article op the Marriage Laws, I think it was
the clergy who brought about the sudden and untimely
end of Mr. Parry Long, the N.S.W. Registrar-General.
The deceased was most scrupulous in the discharge of his
duties. Soon after his appointment he im-
proved marriage form, which fairly drove the parsons off
their heads. Every possible detail was crowded into the
small st space. You had to find out the age of the con-
tracting parties—a most difficult matter where the female
was concerned. Her profession or occupation was also a
sof point, especially when she had none. If you
left the space a blank the certificate was returned
to you for completion. If, in a fit of despera-
tion, you wrote that she was a “lady” or living
in her father’s house, it was also returned as being
indefinite. I have known clergymen who seldom have a
marriage to celebrate to spend an hour over one of these
forms, and then to have it returned two or three times
by the local registrar with pencilled comments. The
result was the clerics grew desperate, and sent the docu-
ment back again with all its sins upon it. The wonder
was that it ever reached Parry Long’s office. But the
unkindest cut of all was when they received a further
notice, asking them to inform the local registrar how
many marriages they celebrated during the half-year.
Very likely many of them didn’t get the chance to per’-
form even one, and vet they were expected to say so—and

Sweet are the uses of advertising. In
front of a stylish villa being erected in St. Kilda
Road, Melb., is a signboard setting forth that
“ These premises are being erected for Mr. Blank,
of Blank Brothers, sole manufacturers of Blank’s
celebrated Pills.”
The Bulletin’s office-drunk hands in this
valuable suggestion:—
One of these days, when The Bulletin is an old, 1
respectable millionaire, I will show it how to do goon
service by endowing an inebriate asylum on new and
original lines, combining humanity with utilitarianism,
and effecting a lightning solution of the eternal problem
of how to deal with the beer-soaked ruin to whom life is
nothing but one huge long-sleever. The Bulletin Insti-
tute will not be started as a “society” charity; it will
not become a benevolent asylum for useless public ser-
vants and influential incapables desirous of getting easy
billets at the expense of the advertising public ; and it
will not spend the whole of its capital in erecting a huge
Ealace, encrusted with architectural aberrations. It will
e probably a long, weatherboard structure, with a gal-
vamsed-iron roof, and with a big, friendless Scotchman,
of offensive honesty and execrable sobriety, as overseer.
The walls of the building will be fitted with unplaned
shelves, on which bottles of spirit will be eternally avail-
able ; and beneath a wide verandah there will be half-a-
dozen barrels of beer, ready tapped for all-comers.
Capable barmen of Oalvinistic tendencies will be appointed
to attend these departments, and serve out liquor and
prevent waste ; and, in order that inmates already drunk
may not interfere with other inmates getting into the
same desirable condition, a deadhouse shall be erected,
into which all the well and truly intoxicated shall be
towed b3' the Caledonian outcast referred to, each drunk
being locked in a separate compartment. The insti-
tution, if possible, will be situated on an island
free from the turmoil and worry attendant upon
proximity to any community, however small ; and
away from offensive criticism. Only duly-qualified dipso-
maniacs will be admitted as inmates, and they will first
have to submit unquestionable recommendations, signed
by a clergyman, a justice of the peace, or some other re-
putable citizen, to prevent imposition on the part of
moderate drinkers, teetotallers, &c. But once accepted
by the board of directors, the experienced drunkard will
be admitted to full privileges and given every opportunity
to drink himself to death as speedily as possible. All
parties will thus be satisfied. The dipsomaniac himself
will hail the scheme as a sort of millennium. The Govern-
ment of the country will be relieved of a large amount of
expense and inconvenience, the people generally will be
no longer troubled by quantities of offensive and unpic-
turesque drunks, the crime-rate will go down to zero, and
sober and respectable relatives will be permitted to
live out their lives without the constant interference and
misconduct of the battered boozer of the family, who is
always making his appearance at inopportune times. Of
course the drunk won’t be reclaimed and preserved, but
then there are lots of people without him, and you can
buy a better man with less than the cost of reclamation.

Buonaparte and Bismarck.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
[Bismarck was born during the celebrated “ hundred
days” and was about eleven weeks old on the day of
Waterloo.]
Could he whose sword so long made and unmade
Rulers and realms have laid him down to dream
Ere Waterloo his blood-stained hosts dismayed,
With vision helped by some far Heaven-shot beam
Piercing the night mists with a meteor gleam,
He might have seen, far in the northern sky,
A prattling child smile at his “ power supreme”—
A child who would his nation’s arms defy
When he in mouldering dust low in the grave
should lie!
Yes ! for beyond that lowering field of Fate-
Before the day, dawning with crimson flame,
That found him conquering hero, stay of State,
And left him but a nightmare and a name—
Into the world with baby-tread there came
A Teuton boy destined in manhood’s prime,
Playing with kingdoms to win lasting fame
And stamp his impress on his land and time :
Bismarck, a wizard name—statesman and man
sublime.
The day, given up to bloodiest quest of power
Through heat of gore, well nigh its course had
run;
Time’s hand was almost on the fatal hour
When the bleak night of exile had begun ;
And as the soldier saw his sinking sun,
Prophetic vision might have caught the star
Watching that man-child’s morning, just began,
Who for his country’s sake, in times afar,
Crushed the Napoleon line, and flooded France
with war.
This Seer of State behind the nation’s steel
Faithfully watching with an eagle eye
Had one all-guiding thought—his country’s weal —
One sword, one soul, for his lov’d Germany:
And, even so, where’er his bones may lie
They need no shelter in a royal tomb;
For men will bare their heads in passing by,
Hushing his sleep until the Day of Doom,
Through flood and fire and storm and cannon’s
thundering boom.
D. M. MAGILL.
“ Cosmos ” : Mark Twain’s resumd of the
Boer War reminds me that the Majuba affair was
due to deliberate treachery, known only to Colley,
his staff, and the nearest sentry. This fact has
been carefully suppressed in the general indig-
nation at the burst-up of British “invincibility,”
and the consequent desire to throw all blame on
“the man who is dead.” Spurred by the sneers
of the Cape and Natal press, and by the whinings
of those Transvaal residents who had entered a
foreign country with the idea that, being English,
they were tabu from local customs, Colley had no
alternative but to act with what he knew to be a
grossly insufficient force for the purpose. It was
the old game of directing operations from the
security and ignorance of the War Office.
Cackle.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
The Franchise general meeting awaited their Pre- 1
sident. The room was half-full. The few mem-
bers of the lower sex present were in the train of
stout women, and were talked across and thumped
when emphasis required.
The treasurer, an antiquated damsel, volubly
addressed the front row. “ Why for forty years I
remember— thirty— twenty years I mean .”
“You said forty,” a young man ventured,
laughing nervously.
Her friends promptly spread the slip over the
room.
Voice from the rear : “ I dreamt last night there
was a large fire and I went out on the balcony in
my nightdress to see it. And there was an old
gentleman there, and he says to me —‘Anyone can
see, Mrs. Dennis, you don’t wear a crinoline.’”
Above everyone : ‘ ‘ My old man says it is wast-
ing good clothes to put them on me, because I
look nice in anything.”
“The paper to-night,” said a languid voice,
“ will be read by a man who often favors us. He
takes the name of an abstruse subject, chews it
over for three lines, and then settles down to the
‘ Moral Relations Between the Sexes.’ ”
“ I think I know the man. Puts his foot in his
mouth every time he opens it.”
An old maid, there under protest, engaged a
young member on Industrial Homes. “ There
must be these places, my dear. We know by our-
selves that women were intended to tempt men.
Eve ate the apple ”
“ Oh, if you believe that you believe anything ! ”
the girl interrupted, moving away in disgust.
A member spoke from the other side: “So you
think Eve damns women for all time ? ”
“ Most certainly. I know, by my own family,
that women are worse than men.”
“ Not by yourself ? ”
“The Holy Spirit has kept temptation from
me.”
“ What a pity! Virtue, you know, is only
absence of temptation and opportunity.”
“My dear, the Holy Spirit ” but no one
listened.
Near the door: “ Walter Bentley is amusing.
You remember his famous argument in our favor?
A mare had just drawn him gallantly to the fran-
chise meeting. Therefore, by all the gods and
little fishes—— ”
Enter the president, seventeen minutes and a-
half late.
VINEGAR.
“ Inna ” : Being a Barbarian myself, I was
delighted to see in Carlyle Smythe’s article that
Mark Twain has never been able to find a single
line of interest in Thackeray. Once upon a time
I used to curse my stupidity, and once more take
down “ Vanity Fair” from the shelf, to struggle
with its tediousness. It always beat me. My
interest in Dickens never fails—but Thackeray is
either above or below me. Also, Mark Twain
regards the “Vicar of Wakefield ” as an effort in
unconscious humor. (This appears in “ More
Tramps.”) I hope Mark will continue to make
remarks on literature. We Barbarians have
plenty more deficiencies, but we don’t like to con-
fess to them till someone else has done it.
An Alternative.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Nellie was a Tasmanian, which accounted for a
complexion that braved even the dust and sun of
Coolgardie. And her nativity of the Arcadian
Isle, it was said, accounted for her unwillingness
. to confo m to the unconventional customs of the
country, which enabled other barmaids without
her physical advantages to amass riches, and
sometimes to become leaders of goldfields society.
But, for whatever reason, Nellie was frozen pro-
priety. Tom Hargreaves, who was “something
on a mine,” exerted all the powers of suggestion
and persuasion of which he was capable, and drank
more than was necessary as an excuse for appear-
ing at Nellie’s shrine —in vain. Nellie would
listen to no suggestions of temporary arrange-
ment ; and so, on the spur of the moment,
after a rebuff which he represented to himself,
from the depth of his n a ure knowledge of
womankind, must be final, Tom made his proposal
absolute instead of provisional, and thus brought
it within the scope of respectabili'y. Yet Nellie
fejected the proffered opening to the upper circles
of Coolgardie society. So Tom walked home that
night to his hessian-and-iron bachelor’s quarters,
thinking deeply, and wondering whether to be more
annoyed at the impulsiveness which had induced
him to actually contemplate matrimony, or at
Nellie for refusing him. Next morning he saw
clearly that Nellie was a good sort, but she wasn’t
quite worth that.
Nellie was likewise annoyed. He was a good
fellow, she thought, but a proposal of marriage
did hot bear a nice flavor when put forward as a
dernier ressort. Her refusal need only be tem-
porary, in accordance with the sport of womankind
since the days of Eve, and the temporising would
do him good. For, of course, he would come
back with apologies for his previous conduct, and
maybe would say that he had been misunderstood.
But he did not come back that week, or the next,
and Nellie began to regret her impulsiveness, and
to believe that for once the exercise of the
cherished privilege of the sex had played her a
sorry trick. It was not till a month afterwards
that he returned, with an air of studied non-
chalance, which was meant to suggest that the
events of the past were disposed of., When the
opportunity came for conversation, it was Nellie
who was forced to take the initiative, and to guide
it into the proper channels.
She said: “I hope you did not think me too
hard on you last time you were here. I was
wondering if I hadn’t been. ”
V “Oh, don’t mention it,” he replied, for want
of something better to say. “Perhaps, after all,
it served me right.”
“I know you didn’t mean all you said,’’ she
pursued; “but we will be better friends in
future.” i
He did not reply, but preserved an inscrutable
countenance.* The situation, unless it could b“
saved, was evidently coming to an end.
“I was too hasty when —when—well, just
before you went away,” she suggested.
He understood, and shivered. Then a way of
retreat flashed into his mind. “Nell,” he said,
“ don’t take any notice of what I said last time.
The fact is I am married already. ”
She looked at him, and was silent for a moment.
Then she smiled. “Did you mean, ,hen, that
you warned to marry me the other night? I
was not taking any particular notice, Well, let
me give you a little advice. Nexi lime you have
a proposition to make to a lady, say exactly what
you mean, so that she will undei stand, and then
she will know what answer to give.”
“ I will,” he replied.
“ Try,” she said.

The beds of the dry lakes in Swan Hill
(Vic.) Shire—have been allowed to fall into culti-
vators] hands. Now that these lakes could be

S.A. Chief-Justice Way, who loves legal
niceties, had a glittering time lately. Somebody
had a pepper-tree, and the roots got into some-
body else’s yard ; and the somebody else, being a
cantankerous person, put salt to the roots, and the
tree died. Now the somebody wanted damages.
There were infinite possibilities- in the situation.
For instance, it was admitted that the cantanke-
rous somebody.else might have chopped the roots
through on his boundary, though, if he had gone
a hair’s-breadth over the line, he would have been
in Shylock’s own mess. On the other hand, could
he use the roots as a conduit for the transmission
of poison to the tree ? The C. J. was apparently
inclined to think that he couldn’t; and the can-
tankerous person began to suggest that, if the
other fellow admitted that the roots had been
used as. a conduit by the other fellow’s tree, he
(that is to say, the cantankerous person) would
probahly proceed against him for the theft of the
salt. To meet that and other difficulties, a botanist
stepped up to say that trees didn’t have a circula-
tion like that at all. The Chief began to look up
precedents, but couldn’t find anything nearer it
than something about a dog. Just at this point,
the parties had a flash of horse-sense, and agreed
to a settlement; and so the mighty problem of
the right of Brown to salt his neighbor Smith’s
pepper-tree roots remains potent to earn many a
dinner more for a pair of hungry lawyers.
He may have been romancing, but
this is how he swore he got the life-saving
medal. “ When I was a fireman, we were called
out one night to a fire at ; two-storey nine-
room house. The squad went through first, but
found no one there. I went upstairs on my own,
opened a door at end of passage, and found an old
lady standing by her bedside) apparently dazed.
Put the blankets around , her, and fixed on the
lifelines. The fire hadn’t then broken out pro-
perly, and we could have walked downstairs
all right, but that didh’t.puit my book, so I dragged
the old girl out into the passage, and waited till
the old place was blazing and the smoke pretty
thick. Then I hauled the old lady on to the
balcony, blew my whistle, and the boys ran the
escape up. There was a big crowd in the street,
and, when they saw the ‘heroic rescue’ (that’s
what the papers called it), they cheered like mad.
Could have drowned myself in beer that night.
Got this gold medal, and the old lady calls me her
saviour, and says that I’ll never want for a pound
as long as she is alive. How did I leave the
brigade? Well, tried the same game at ,
about four years later, and fell in. Got copped
holding a boy in a back room. Had promised him
a ‘ quid ’ to keep quiet, and, in two minutes more,
I’d have been safe for a second medal; but the
captain spotted me, and the game was up.”


“S.C.” : Be lepers. In Sweden, life-
prisoners used to be experimented on with disease
microbes, under pretence of vaccinating them.
One case, vouched for by a high authority to the
writer, was that of a young officer convicted of
murder on circumstantial evidence. His friends
and his fiancee believed him innocent. Years
elapsed before the real murderer’s death-bed con-
fession led to the convict’s release. The faithful
fiancee married him ; children were born ; and all
went well till the husband at last displayed the
hideous leprous taint. He had been experimentally
inoculated in gaol, and the disease had taken years
to mature. Curious, by the .way, that only one
“white” case of leprosy was known to the West
Indies during 30 years, although the disease was
common enough amongst the colored races.

The Doubledays.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Doubledat heard voices coming from his wife’s
dressing-room, and was stricken suddenly rigid.
He had thought the theatre deserted. He rarely
came there in the afternoon, but to-day he called
about a forgotten cigar-case.
He stood still a second till he recognised the
masculine tones in the low-laughing duet that had
arrested him, and then strode rapidly across the
stage and struck open the door.
There was a scream and an oath, and he saw
his wife by a sofa, and Clavering, the juvenile
lead—far from her.
Doubleday paused—the moment for righteous
anger went by, and the woman had time to think.
“Didn’t I warn you before?” he said to her,
“ You know what I’ll do.” The woman recog-
nised the self-pity in his voice, and said with easy
certainty, “You won’t . . . you’re too fond of
me, you fool.”
Doubleday, leaning against the door, put his
hands over his eyes as though he would shut out
her contemptuous picture of himself.
“ I know I’m a fool ; but”—turning to Claver-
ing— “ she’ll tire of you as she tires of everything.
She tired of me. ”
Clavering, who had recovered his self-possession,
grew supercilious, glancing at himself in the glass.
Doubleday saw the scorn on the face of his hand-
some rival,-and it brought passion at last to him.
“ Damn you ! ” he cried, “ don’t think I’m afraid
of you because I don’t strangle you. We can’t, go
hurting each other, because of the show.. Your
looks mean cash to me. If I smash your face
I’ve no one to replace you. I can’t hit till I don’t
'• ant you. . . . I don’t know what to do yet,”
- he staggered, with the weight of suppressed feel-
ing, like a drunken man— “ only I shan’t interfere
just yet., You can have a free hand ; in return, I
ask you to cause no scandal—for the sake of the
business. You see, it’s this——” picking up a
society-weekly from the table. “Here it is,”
reading, ‘.‘Mr. and Mrs. Doubleday are a mcdel
of conjugal felicity in a profession where, unfor-
tunately, Happy marriages are the exception.” He
crushed up the paper, the reading of which twisted
the veins in his temples into knots.
“ D'ye hear—both of you—The Doubledays—
happy man aiid wife—that’s what draws our
crowd. 1) ye understand, woman—The Double-
days—we’ve got to. play up to that till . If I
lose that, God help both of you, for there’ll be
no hing to hold me in.”
Then—with a feeling that would be theatrical
in any but an actor—he picked up a nail-scissors
from the table, and, with it, pinned the open
paper to the wall, and passed from the room.
• • * *
Doubleday could not explain how he found him-
self calmly seated at supper with his wife that
evening. There had been a performance, with its
medley of thoughts, emotions, and mechanical ac-
quirements gone through, just the same as usual.
Then the drive home in the cab—also as usual—
with her-who was again faithless to him. He
knew that her infidelity was the gigantic
fact overshadowing every moment, but, luckily for
him, it ad not been strong enough to clog the
wheels of his daily routine. All the evening he
had been wondering why he had not done more.
r .t is very hard for an actor to be natural off the
stage. In drama a like situation would be full
of declamatory passion. He felt uncomfortable
and ashamed of himself because he could not rise
to the heights such a scene would exhibit in a
play.
Meantime the woman sat opposite him with a
plate of oysters liberally cayenned. He wondered
how she could eat— forgetting that he was eating
himself. When the servant was dismissed the
woman rose, her exasperating air of nothing-
ha ving-happened being given its fullest effect, and
made for the bedroom without a word. Double-
day almost felt that he was the sinner, not she.
“Esme I”—she stopped—“what can man do more
for you than I have ? You know what he is ; it’s
only a moment’s amusement to him. But you’re
sure of me.”
“ That’s why,” murmured the woman. Which
he did not hear.
“ I’ve given you everything.”
“ No,” she replied, with an emphasis that would
have made her famous could she command it on
the stage ; “ not everything .”
Doubleday understood, and amazement over-
powered him.
‘ You—but you’ve always said you never would
—never wished— ”
She surveyed his bewilderment with light scorn,
and passed from the room.
• * • •
Doubleday remained in the room with the Devil
and a decanter.
Why he had taken it at first so quietly now be-
came clear to him. She had sinned before, and he
had forgivpn. There had been many episodes be-
sides—which had not gone as far as sin indeed —at
least, he made himself believe so—and these had
gradually bred a tolerance in him. He had be-
come the tame husband who treats these disturb-
ances as inevitable—but temporary.
But now her last words kindled new feelings—
he was no longer resigned. He' was full of active
hate for the man, and a furious love that would
fight. Then his pocket interposed milder councils.
He could not afford an open rupture with Claver-
ing. Clavering was a popular favorite. Clavering
was a draw ; women said he was a tine actor and
had Soul, and got their men to reserve seats to be
stirred by that Soul. They also sent perfumed
notes —for more Soul. Doubleday was artist
enough to know that the other kind of artist like
Clavering was necessary to a show, no .matter what
the brain and talent of the rest of the company
may be.
But the Devil and the decanter drowned con-
sideration ultimately, and he pushed open his
wife’s door. She had not even troubled to latch
it, and the room was in darkness. He could hear
her breathing tranquilly. When he stumbled
against a chair she woke, with a whispered name
on her lips.
“ It’s not Charles,” said Doubleday.
“Oh, it’s you—you’ve woke me; what do you
want?” came from the darkness.
“ Ksme, I only want to ask you if . . . Why,
what’s the matter ? ”
He had lit a match suddenly and saw her sit-
ting up in bed, her face apparently frightfully
contorted.
“Nothing—l wish you’d go,” shesaid pettishly,
burying her head in the pillow again. She had
only been making faces in the dark at this mail
who disturbed her dreams. Then the match
burnt out and he did not light another.
“ I only wanted to ask you ... if it’s only what
you said . . . supposing you . . . would you be
faithful to me ever . . . afterwards.
The woman—profoundly bored by it all—guess*
ing assent would get rid of him, gave it.
Doubleday felt his way out to the sofa.
• • * •
A few months went by. Esme grew more
shameless, but Clavering’s fears lest the intrigue
might fasten unbreakable bonds upon him helped
to restrain scandal within limits. Doubleday was
punctiliously careful, and no open breach occurred.
He was most affable to the man, and asked him
frequently to their house, which gradually took the
form of a triangular establishment. Work kept
Doubleday from feeling the tension, but one day
it grew unbearable—and he gave in. It was the
afternoon of a new piece. He arrived home just
in time for 4 o’clock dinner. There were voices
in his wife’s room—but not laughing this time.
He listened—he felt he had a right to ; they had
taken every other right.
“ It’s a damned noosance,” said the man.
“But I’ll love you more,” said the woman.
Then the pair came into the dining-room and
Clavering’s face was not that of a man who
wanted to be loved any more, while on Esme’s
was the light of a revelation that drove Double-
day mad.
• * • •
He was early down at the theatre that night.
While dressing, the property man brought him
the pistol—an old-fashioned muzzle-loader in
keeping with the date of the piece—that he was
to use in the duel-scene. On top of the charge
Doubleday filled the barrel nearly up to the
muzzle with powder, and then rammed in a plug
of paper as a wad.
• • * •
It was the duel-scene. Clavering, with the eyes
of the house on his handsome profile, raised his
pistol. He was solely absorbed in his part when a
look on Doubleday’s face that he knew Doubleday
could not act warned him of danger. But it was
impossible to break up the scene—he would make
himself too ridiculous. So, nerving himself to
receive the bullet of his anticipated murderer,
he gave the cue.
* * • *
Nobody in front knew there was an accident,
but Clavering had received the charge right in his
eyes, and a doctor called hastily in said the sight
of one was gone and the other badly injured.
Doubleday was profuse in explanations and re-
grets. The pistol was overloaded and kicked,
though, with his usual carefulness, he fired high
in the air. He organised a benefit for “ the finest
juvenile that he ever had,” and the papers spoke
highly of his generosity.
* • * *
Mrs. Doubleday had a child some months later

THE STROLLER.
“Lazarus”: A Melb. Health Society
lecturer has been denouncing dirt. This is too
sweeping. There is dirt and dirt. Honest dirt
may be unpleasant, but is not unhealthy. Knew
a man who killed himself by over-cleanliness. Sat
tucked up over a steam-bath every morning.
Bathe every day, and then see what the first
Turkish Bath will take put of you. You are
covered with a million dirty creeks. For all that
I don’t believe in Turkish Baths. Perhaps—or
probably —old Parr never took a bath. What
if you have a hundred million pores ; they are
given you on the principle of a codfish spawning a
million that one may be preserved.
A £3O Fish.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
“ Well, I once caught a fish in the Derwent for
which I was paid .£30.”
A rather startling statement, even among fish
stories.
“ Was it a whale or a shark, Cronly ? ”
“Neither—it was just the first Tasmanian
salmon. It happened this way; when the Main
Line Railway was being constructed, I had a bit
of a contract out Bridgewater way, and one
morning, at low tide, I noticed a big fish stranded
in a little creek near the river. I just stepped
into the water and chucked the fish out on to the
bank with me two hands, and when I examined it
I thought it looked remarkably like the salmon
me brother and me used to catch in the Shannon
at home. To make sure, I called a navvy over to
look at the curious fish I had caught, and he im-
mediately said ‘That’s a salmon.’ So, thinks I,
if it resembles both English and Irish salmon,
I’ll claim the reward. I packed up the fish, and
sent it to Morton Allport with a bit of a
note, claiming the £3O that was offered for the
first salmon caught in Tasmania. Next day, up
came Mr. Allport and shakes me by both hands,
‘ Cronly,’ says he, ‘ you’re the most talked-of man
in the warld to-day; do you know that the
news has been sent over the world that salmon
has at last been acclimatised in Tasmania? Now
tell me how you caught him —did he give you
much trouble ? ’ Well, I thought if I told him how
I really caught that fish I might lose the £3O, so
I spun a long yarn about the kind of tackle I used
and how I played him till I wearied him out and
then landed him. Then Mr. Allport says, ‘ Now,
Cronly, I want you to do me a very great favor ;
will you give me the hook with which you caught
the first Tasmanian salmon ? ”
“ What did you do, Cronly ? ”
“ Why, I gave it to him 1”
